The Four-Part Structure of Asas al-Taqdis
The author outlines the four main sections of the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi organized his work into four distinct sections to systematically defend divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The book's four sections form a complete defense of divine transcendence (tanzih).

This passage reveals the author's architectural plan for the entire book. Before delving into the arguments, Imam al-Razi provides a clear roadmap of his work, dividing it into four sections that progress from proofs to interpretation to affirming the Salaf's position and finally to remaining issues. Understanding this structure will help you navigate the deeper arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the English translation. Notice the author's explicit enumeration of the sections.
The passage is short but dense with information about the book's structure.
2
Identify each section's purpose
Go through each of the four sections one by one. Summarize in your own words what each section covers based on the passage.
Use the key terms (جسمية, حيز, المتشابهات, مذهب السلف) to reinforce the meaning.
3
Reflect on the order
Consider why the sections are arranged in this specific order. Why do proofs come first? Then interpretation? Then the Salaf's doctrine?
Think about how each section builds on the previous one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورتبته على أربعة أقسام: القسم الأول: في الدلائل الدالة على أنه تعالى منزه عن الجسمية والحيز. والقسم الثاني: في تأويل المتشابهات، من الأخبار والآيات. والقسم الثالث: في تقرير مذهب السلف. والقسم الرابع: في بقية الكلام في هذا الباب.
And I arranged it into four sections: The first section: on the proofs indicating that He, exalted is He, is transcendent above corporeality and spatial location. And the second section: on the interpretation of ambiguous/similar texts from hadiths and verses. And the third section: on establishing the doctrine of the Salaf. And the fourth section: on the rest of the discourse on this topic.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage defines the structure of the entire book. The author states that he arranged the work into four sections. The first section provides proofs that God is transcendent above body (jismiyya) and location (hayyiz). The second section deals with the interpretation (ta'wil) of ambiguous texts, from both hadiths and Qur'anic verses, that might suggest anthropomorphism. The third section affirms the doctrine of the Salaf (the early pious predecessors) regarding these matters. The fourth section contains remaining discussions related to the topic. This outline shows the author's systematic approach to defending divine transcendence.

Key Terms
جسمية
حيز
المتشابهات
مذهب السلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the topic of the first section of the book?
A) Interpretation of ambiguous verses
B) Proofs that God is transcendent above body and place
C) The doctrine of the Salaf
D) Remaining discussions

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi organized his book into four sections: proofs of transcendence, interpretation of ambiguous texts, the Salaf's doctrine, and remaining discussions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to describe Allah in human-like terms, do you immediately try to interpret it based on your own understanding, or do you first ground yourself in the clear proofs of His transcendence? How does the order of this book challenge your typical approach?
?
The Salaf's doctrine is presented as one of four essential sections. Have you ever studied the methodology of the Salaf deeply? What step will you take this week to learn from their approach rather than relying solely on your own reasoning?

Why the Premise Cannot Be Self-Evident
Imam Razi explains why the premise must be shown not self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the premise of God's spatial relation must be shown not to be self-evident, and the logical consequences if it were.

Core Idea
If a premise were self-evident, proving it would destroy the foundation of knowledge.

Imam Razi now addresses a crucial objection: what if the opponent claims that the premise that God is neither inside nor outside the world is self-evident? In this passage, he explains why such a claim would undo the very possibility of rational argument. He then shows the necessary response: we must demonstrate that this premise is not self-evident. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of Imam Razi's argument.
2
Identify the hypothetical
Notice the conditional structure: 'if it were established that this premise is self-evident'. This sets up a counterfactual scenario.
The word 'لو' indicates a hypothetical.
3
Understand the consequence
If it were self-evident, then proving it would invalidate necessary knowledge (الضروريات). That is unacceptable because it impugns the root by the branch.
4
See the required response
Instead of arguing against a self-evident claim, we must show that it is not self-evident. This is the path Imam Razi will take.
This sets up the next section of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أنه لو ثبت كون هذه المقدمة بديهية، لم يكن الخوض في ذكر الدلائل جائزاً، لأن على تقدير أن يكون الأمر على ما قالوه، كان الشروع في الاستدلال على كون الله تعالى غير حال في العالم ولا مباين عنه بالجهة: إبطالا للضروريات. والقدح في الضروريات بالنظريات: يقتضى القدح في الأصل بالفرع، وذلك يرجع تطرق الطمن إلى الأصل والفرع مما. وهو باطل. بل يجب علينا: بيان أن هذه المقدمة ليست من المقدمات البديهية، حتى يزول هذا الإشكال.
And know: if it were established that this premise is self-evident, then delving into mentioning the evidences would not be permissible; because on the assumption that the matter is as they said, commencing to infer that God exalted is not indwelling in the world nor separate from it by direction would be an invalidation of necessities. And impugning necessities by theoretical matters entails impugning the root by the branch, and that leads to suspicion tarnishing the root and the branch both. And it is false. Rather, it is incumbent upon us to show that this premise is not among the self-evident premises, so that this difficulty may be removed.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage explains the consequences if the opponent's claim that the premise is self-evident were true. The author argues that if the premise were self-evident, then attempting to prove it through rational arguments would be illegitimate, because reason would be undermining foundational necessary knowledge. He uses a logical argument: attacking self-evident truths (the root) with theoretical reasoning (the branch) is self-defeating, as it casts doubt on both. Therefore, it is necessary to demonstrate that this premise is not self-evident. This sets the stage for the proofs that follow.

Key Terms
بديهية
الاستدلال
الضروريات
النظريات
القدح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Razi is simply dismissing the opponent's claim without proof.
Correction: He provides a logical argument: if the premise were self-evident, proving it would be self-defeating because it would undermine necessary knowledge. Thus, we must show the premise is not self-evident.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what would be the consequence if the premise were self-evident?
A) It would be easier to prove
B) It would be impossible to discuss
C) Attempting to prove it would invalidate necessary knowledge
D) It would be proven by scripture

Key Takeaway
If a claim were self-evident, attempting to prove it rationally would undermine the very foundations of knowledge; thus, the premise must be shown not to be self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a belief of yours is so obvious that you never questioned it? What if that assumption led you to dismiss rational inquiry that could actually strengthen your faith? Are you willing to re-examine such beliefs today?
?
When you argue for your faith, do you sometimes rely on claims that feel 'self-evident' without checking if they really are? Could that be a weak point in your reasoning that you need to address by showing the premise is not self-evident?
?
If someone challenged a core belief you hold, would you be tempted to say 'it's just obvious' rather than engage rationally? What would Imam Razi say about that approach, and what would you need to change in your own life to follow his method?

Method of Learning
intermediate
4 min
Proving the Premise Is Not Self-Evident
Razi establishes the methodological necessity of proving the premise is not self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the premise about God's relation to the world cannot be self-evident, and why he must prove it discursively.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths cannot be proven; therefore, the premise must be shown to be non-self-evident.

Razi begins by quoting his opponents who claim that affirming two existents outside the seven categories is intuitively false. He then explains a key methodological rule: if that premise were truly self-evident, using proofs to support it would be self-defeating. Instead, he must demonstrate that it is not self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponents assert that the premise is self-evident (badīhiyya). Razi quotes them to set up the problem.
Self-evident means it is known without proof.
2
Grasp the methodological problem
If the premise were self-evident, using proofs would invalidate its self-evidence, leading to absurd consequences.
Proofs are for what is not self-evident; using them on self-evident truths attacks the root.
3
See Razi's solution
Razi states he must show the premise is not self-evident, thereby removing the objection and allowing valid proof.
He will present several aspects (wujūh) to demonstrate non-self-evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالو : وإثبات موجودين على خلاف هذه الأقسام السبعة : باطل في بدائه العقول . واعلم : أنه لو ثبت كون هذه المقدمة بديهية ، لم يكن الخوض في ذكر الدلائل جائزاً ، لأن على تقدير أن يكون الأمر على ما قالوه ، كان الشروع في الاستدلال على كون الله تعالى غير حال في العالم ولا مباين عنه بالجهة : إبطالا للضروريات . والقدح في الضروريات و بالنظريات : يقتضى القدح في الأصل بالفرع ، وذلك يرحب تطرق الطمن إلى الأصل والفرع مما . وهو باطل . بل يجب علينا : بيان أن هذه المقدمة ليست من المقدمات البديهية، حتى يزول هذا الإشكال . فنقول : الذي يدل على أن هذه المقدمات ليست بديهية ، وجوه :
And they said: 'Affirming two existents contrary to these seven divisions is invalid in the primary intuitions of intellects.' And know: if it were established that this premise is self-evident, engaging in mentioning proofs would not be permissible, because on the assumption that the matter is as they said, beginning to infer that God Almighty is not indwelling in the world nor separate from it in direction would be an invalidation of self-evident truths. And attacking self-evident truths by theoretical proofs necessitates attacking the root by the branch, and that causes suspicion to enter the root and the branch. And that is invalid. Rather, it is incumbent upon us to explain that this premise is not among self-evident premises, so that this difficulty is removed. So we say: that which indicates that these premises are not self-evident are [several] aspects:
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is an introductory argument. Razi begins by citing the opponent's claim: affirming two existents (God and the world) that do not fit the seven divisions (being in each other, separate, etc.) is rejected by primary intuition. He then explains a methodological point: if this premise really were self-evident (badīhiyya), then it would be impermissible to engage in discursive proof (dalīl) about it, because using proof to support a self-evident truth would actually be an invalidation of self-evidence itself—like using a derived (farʿ) argument to attack a root (aṣl) principle. That would cause doubt in both. Therefore, Razi states, the correct approach is to demonstrate that this premise is not self-evident, thereby removing the objection. He then announces that he will present several proofs (wujūh) to that effect.

Key Terms
بديهية
ضروريات
إشكال
مقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a premise is self-evident, it can still be proven.
Correction: Razi explains that proving a self-evident premise would actually invalidate its self-evidence, because self-evident truths need no proof.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is it problematic if the premise about God's non-location were self-evident?
A) Because then the premise would be false
B) Because then it would be impermissible to use proof to support it, as that would invalidate self-evidence
C) Because then all rational people would agree, but they don't
D) Because then it would contradict scripture

Key Takeaway
Razi must show the premise is not self-evident to avoid the logical error of invalidating self-evident truths with proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed a belief about God was self-evident without testing it against revelation and reason? What might that cost you in your faith journey?
?
If Razi had accepted the opponent's claim without question, he would have abandoned the entire argument for God's transcendence. What core belief of yours might you be abandoning because you assumed it was 'just common sense' rather than proving it?
?
The opponent claimed that affirming God's existence outside the seven divisions is 'invalid in primary intuitions.' Are you willing to let your raw intuitions dictate what is true about God, or will you submit to rigorous examination, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Second Proof: Comparing Certainty and Hesitation
Razi contrasts the mind's hesitation on this premise with its certainty on necessary truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a psychological comparison to show that the assumption that God must be in a direction is based on imagination, not reason.

Core Idea
Certainty about necessary truths exposes the weakness of assumptions about God's location.

Razi's second proof is a clever thought experiment. He asks us to compare how our mind responds to two different statements: one about God's relationship to the world, and another about simple mathematical truth. The difference in our mental reaction reveals that the first is not a rational necessity but a product of imagination. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the proof.
Notice the contrast between hesitation and certainty.
2
Identify the two premises compared
Find the two propositions presented to the mind: (1) an existent that is neither inside nor outside the world in any direction, and (2) that one is half of two and that negation and affirmation cannot coexist.
The second premise is a necessary truth.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that the intellect is not decisive on the first premise, so it permits its opposite. The inclination we feel comes from imagination, not reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنا إذا عرضنا على العقل ، وجود موجود لا يكون حالا في العالم ولا مباينا عنه فى شىء من الجهات الست ، وعرضنا على العقل أيضا أن الواحد نصف الاثنين ، وأن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان : وجدنا العقل متوقفا في المقدمة الأولى ، جازما في المقدمة الثانية . وهذا التفاوت معلوم بالضرورة . وذلك يدل على أن العقل غير قاطع في المقدمه الأولى لا بالنفى ولا بالإثبات. غاية ما فى الباب: إنا نجد من أنفسنا ميلا إلى القول بأن كل ما سوى العالم ، لابد وأن يكون حالا فيه ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة والحيز . إلا أنا نقول : لما رأينا أن العقل لم يجزم بهذه المقدمة ، مثل جزمه بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين: علمنا أنه غير قاطع بأن ما سوى العالم ، لا بد وأن يكون حالا فيه ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة، بل هو مجوز لنقيضه . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن ذلك الظن إنما حصل بسبب أن الوهم والخيال لا يتصرفان إلا فى المحسوسات. فلا جرم كان من شأنهما أنهما يقضيان على كل شيء بالأحكام اللائقة بالمحسوسات. وهذا الميل إنما جاء بسبب الوهم والخيال ، لا بسبب العقل البتة .
The second: Indeed when we present to the intellect the existence of an existent that is not indwelling in the world nor separate from it in any of the six directions, and we also present to the intellect that one is half of two, and that negation and affirmation cannot coexist, we find the intellect hesitant regarding the first premise and certain regarding the second premise. And this difference is known necessarily. And that indicates that the intellect is not decisive regarding the first premise, neither by negation nor by affirmation. The utmost in this matter is: we find within ourselves an inclination to say that everything other than the world must either be indwelling in it or separate from it in direction and space. However we say: since we see that the intellect has not decisively affirmed this premise like its decisive affirmation that one is half of two, we know that it is not decisive that everything other than the world must be indwelling in it or separate from it in direction; rather it permits its opposite. And if this is established, then we say: that supposition (ẓann) only occurred because imagination (wahm) and fancy (khayāl) do not operate except in sensible things. Hence it is necessarily their nature that they judge everything by the judgments appropriate to sensibles. And this inclination only arose due to imagination and fancy, not due to the intellect at all.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's second proof, which is a psychological/epistemological argument. He asks us to compare two mental presentations: (1) the existence of an existent that is neither indwelling in the world nor separate from it in any direction, and (2) the truth that one is half of two and that negation and affirmation cannot coexist (the law of non-contradiction). When we examine our mind's response, we find that it is hesitant (mutawaqqif) regarding the first premise but certain (jāzim) regarding the second. This difference is itself known necessarily. It shows that the intellect is not decisive about the first premise. The most we have is an inclination (mayl) to think that everything outside the world must be either indwelling or separate. But since the intellect is not as certain as it is about necessary truths, it is not decisive; it permits the opposite (nuqayḍ). Razi then explains the source of this inclination: it comes from imagination (wahm and khayāl), which only operates on sensible things. Because we are used to perceiving bodies and locations, the imagination wrongly applies those categories to God. The inclination is not from pure intellect (ʿaql) at all.

Key Terms
عقل
متوقف
جازم
وهم
خيال
ظن
ميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The feeling that everything must be in a location is a rational necessity.
Correction: Razi shows that the intellect is not certain about this; the inclination comes from imagination and habit, not reason.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof, why is the intellect not decisive about the premise that everything outside the world must be either indwelling or separate?
A) Because the intellect is weak
B) Because it does not have the same level of certainty as it does for necessary truths like 'one is half of two'
C) Because all rational people disagree
D) Because the premise is false

Key Takeaway
The mind's uncertainty about God's relation to the world shows that the assumption of direction is not rationally necessary but arises from imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you catch yourself picturing a direction or location in your mind? How does Razi's argument challenge you to separate your imagination from your intellect when it comes to Allah?
?
Are there other beliefs about Allah that you hold simply because they feel natural or comfortable, not because they are rationally necessary? Will you examine them and ask whether they come from imagination or from the Qur'an and sound reason?
?
This lesson shows that your mind hesitates about God's relationship to the world. Does that hesitation trouble you? Are you willing to accept that the intellect does not have certainty here, and that true faith requires trusting what Allah has revealed—even when your imagination resists?

The Need for a New Intellect in Divine Knowledge
Razi concludes with Aristotle's advice and Ibn Sina's commentary on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi concludes the first introduction by emphasizing that accessing divine knowledge requires a different kind of intellect than that used for physical sciences.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge demands a new nature, not just better reasoning.

Razi has just completed ten proofs establishing that God's transcendence of place and direction is rational. He now concludes with a profound methodological warning from Aristotle, endorsed by Ibn Sina: to truly engage with divine matters, one must acquire a completely new faculty of understanding. This lesson unpacks that insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once, noting the shift from the summary of ten proofs to the citation of Aristotle's saying and Ibn Sina's commentary.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فليستحدث لنفسه فطرة أخرى' – it is the key to the lesson.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify the three parts: (1) the conclusion that the ten proofs show God's transcendence is rational, (2) Aristotle's saying, (3) Ibn Sina's commentary linking it to the Quranic verse on creation.
3
Understand the analogy
Reflect on how the soul-body attachment is described as a 'new creation' different from the preceding physical stages. This illustrates the need for a new faculty when moving from physical to divine knowledge.
The verse: 'ثم أنشأناه خلقاً آخر' (Then We produced it as another creation) is the basis.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why can't the intellect used for physics and chemistry be sufficient for theology? What does this say about the limits of human reason in divine matters?
This ties back to the overall theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الدلائل العشرة : دالة على أن كونه - سبحانه وتعالى ـ منزه عن الحيز والجهة : ليس أمراً يدفعه صريح العقل. وذلك هو تمام المطلوب وبالله التوفيق. ونختم هذا الباب : بما روى عن أرسطاطاليس ، أنه كتب في أول كتابه في الإلهيات : « من أراد أن يشرع في المعارف الإلهية ، فليستحدث لنفسه فطرة أخرى ». قال الشيخ ـ رضى الله عنه : وهذا الكلام موافق للوحي والنبوة . فإنه ذكر مراتب تكون الجسد في قوله تعالى : « ولقد خلقنا الإنسان من سلالة من طين » ، فلما آل الأمر إلى تعلق الروح بالبدن . قال : « ثم أنشأناه خلقاً آخر » ، وذلك كالتنبيه على أن كيفية تعلق الروح بالبدن ، ليس مثل انقلاب النطفة من حال إلى حال ، بل هذا نوع آخر ، مخالف لتلك الأنواع المتقدمة . فلهذا السبب قال : ثم أنشأناه خلقاً آخر ، وكذلك الإنسان إذا تأمل في أحوال الأجرام السفليه والعلوية وتأمل في صفاتها . فذلك له قانون . فإذا أراد أن ينتقل منها إلى معرفة الربوبية . وجب أن يستحدث لنفسه فطرة أخرى ، وعقلا آخر ، بخلاف العقل الذى به اهتدى إلى معرفة الجسمانيات . وهذا آخر الكلام في هذه المقدمة . وبالله التوفيق .
These ten proofs indicate that His being - glorified and exalted is He - transcendent of place and direction is not something that clear reason rejects. That is the complete objective, and with Allah is success. And we conclude this chapter with what is narrated from Aristotle, that he wrote at the beginning of his book on theology: 'Whoever wishes to embark on divine knowledge, let him acquire for himself another nature.' The Sheikh - may Allah be pleased with him - said: This speech agrees with revelation and prophecy. For He mentioned the stages of the formation of the body in His statement: 'And indeed We created man from an extract of clay' (al-Mu'minun 12); then when the matter came to the attachment of the soul to the body, He said: 'Then We produced it as another creation' (al-Mu'minun 14). And that is a hint that the manner of attachment of the soul to the body is not like the transformation of the sperm from state to state, but rather it is another kind, different from those preceding types. For this reason He said: 'Then We produced it as another creation.' Likewise, when a person contemplates the conditions of the lower and higher bodies and contemplates their attributes, that gives him a principle. Then if he wishes to move from them to knowledge of Lordship, he must acquire for himself another nature and another intellect, different from the intellect by which he was guided to knowledge of corporeal things. This is the end of the discussion in this introduction. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage concludes the first introduction of the book. The author summarizes that the ten proofs he presented demonstrate that God's transcendence of place and direction is not opposed by clear reason; rather, it is rationally established. He then cites a famous saying of Aristotle from his Metaphysics: that whoever wants to study divine things must acquire a new faculty (fitra). The Sheikh (i.e., Ibn Sina) comments that this saying agrees with the Quranic verse on the creation of man: after the physical stages, God says 'Then We produced it as another creation,' indicating that the soul's attachment to the body is a new kind of creation, not just a transformation. Similarly, to move from knowledge of physical bodies to knowledge of the divine, one needs a new intellectual faculty.

Key Terms
الفطرة
الجسمانيات
الربوبية
أرسطاطاليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human reason alone, as used for physical sciences, can fully grasp divine matters.
Correction: The passage stresses that a new nature and a different intellect are required for divine knowledge, highlighting the discontinuity between physical and metaphysical reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must one do to move from knowledge of corporeal things to knowledge of Lordship?
A) Increase study of physical sciences
B) Acquire another nature and another intellect
C) Rely solely on revelation
D) Follow Aristotle's method

Key Takeaway
To know the Divine, one needs a different kind of intellect, not just a better use of the one used for physical things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study the world around you—science, nature, human behavior—are you aware of a difference in your heart when you turn to think about Allah? If that difference is absent, have you been using the wrong 'intellect'? What would it take for you to cultivate that new nature today?
?
This lesson demands that you acquire 'another intellect' for divine knowledge. Have you ever consciously asked Allah to grant you a new fitra? If not, pause now and make that du'a, then commit to one concrete change in how you approach learning about Him.
?
You can explain the Quranic stages of creation, but do you feel the truth that your soul is a 'new creation' from Allah? Are you treating your spiritual life as merely an improvement of your physical self, or as a radical transformation? What step will you take this week to live as that 'other creation'?

5 min
The Principle and the First Proof from Intuition
Razi states the thesis and provides the first intuitive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the innate intellect does not repudiate the existence of a unique being without likeness, and thus denying a likeness does not entail non-existence.

Core Idea
Common sense accepts a unique being without equal.

Imam Razi begins this introduction by stating that not every existent must have a likeness, and denying a likeness does not deny existence. He then gives the first of three proofs: the innate intellect is not repulsed by the idea of an existent with exclusive attributes. This establishes a rational foundation for God's uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise style of Razi.
Pay attention to the term 'بديهة العقل'.
2
Identify the thesis and the proof
Find the sentence that states the thesis and the sentence that states the first proof. Understand how the proof supports the thesis.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: Is it rationally impossible for something to exist with unique attributes? Razi says no. Why is that significant?
This will be foundational for later arguments about God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية
في
أنه ليس كل موجود يجب أن يكون له نظير وشبيه، وأنه ليس يلزم من نفي النظير والشبيه، نفي ذلك الشيء
ويدل عليه وجوه:
الحجة الأولى: إن بديهة العقل لا تستبعد وجود موجود، موصوف بصفات مخصوصة، بحيث يكون كل ما سواه مخالفاً له في تلك الخصوصية. وإذا لم يكن هذا مدفوعاً في بدائه العقول، علمنا أنه لا يلزم من عدم نظير الشيء عدم ذلك الشيء.
الحجة الثانية: هي إن وجود الشيء إما أن يتوقف على وجود ما شابهه، أولا يتوقف. والأول باطل لأن الشيئين لو كانا متشابهين، وجب استواؤهما في جميع اللوازم. فيلزم من توقف وجود هذا على وجود الثاني، توقف وجود الثاني على وجود الأول. بل توقف كل واحد منهما على نفسه. وذلك محال في بدائه العقول. فثبت أنه لا يتوقف وجود الشيء على وجود نظير له. فلا يلزم من نفي النظير نفيه.
الحجة الثالثة: هي أن تغير كل شيء من حيث إنه هو ممتنع الحصول في غيره. وإلا لكان ذلك الشيء عين غيره. وذلك باطل في بدائه العقول. فثبت أن تعين كل شيء من حيث إنه هو ممتنع الحصول في غيره. فعلمنا أن عدم النظير والمساوي لا يوجب القول بعدم الشيء، وظهر فساد قول من يقول: إنه لا يمكننا أن نعقل وجود موجود لا يكون متصلا بالعالم ولا منفصلا عنه إلا إذا وجدنا له نظيراً. فإن عندنا: الموصوف بهذه الصفة ليس إلا الله سبحانه وتعالى، وبينا أنه لا يلزم من عدم النظير والشبيه عدم الشيء. فثبت أن هذا الكلام ساقط بالكلية. وبالله التوفيق.
The second introduction: Concerning that not every existent must have a likeness and similitude, and that denial of the likeness and similitude does not necessitate denial of that thing. And proofs indicate it: The first proof: The innate nature of the intellect does not deem unlikely the existence of an existent described with specific attributes, such that everything other than it is contrary to it in that specificity. And if this is not repudiated in the innate natures of intellects, we know that the absence of a likeness of a thing does not necessitate the absence of that thing. The second proof: It is that the existence of a thing either depends on the existence of what resembles it or does not. The first is false because if two things were similar, they would necessarily be equal in all necessary concomitants. Thus, from the dependence of the existence of this on the existence of the second, the dependence of the existence of the second on the existence of the first follows; rather, the dependence of each one on itself follows. And that is impossible in the innate natures of intellects. Thus it is established that the existence of a thing does not depend on the existence of a likeness to it. So denial of the likeness does not necessitate its denial. The third proof: It is that the particularization of each thing inasmuch as it is itself is impossible of occurring in something else. Otherwise, that thing would be identical to something else. And that is false in the innate natures of intellects. Thus it is established that the particularization of each thing inasmuch as it is itself is impossible of occurring in something else. So we know that the absence of a likeness and equal does not necessitate the assertion of the non-existence of the thing, and it becomes clear the corruption of the saying of those who say: It is impossible for us to conceive the existence of an existent that is neither connected to the world nor separate from it unless we find a likeness for it. For according to us: The one described with this attribute is none other than God, glory be to Him and exalted, and we have shown that the absence of a likeness and similitude does not necessitate the non-existence of the thing. Thus it is established that this speech is entirely invalid. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the second preface (المقدمة الثانية) of Asas al-Taqdis, where Imam Razi establishes a key methodological principle: the fact that something has no equal or similar does not mean it does not exist. This is crucial for later arguments about God's uniqueness. The author presents three rational proofs (حجج) in sequence.

First, he states the thesis: not every existent must have a likeness, and denying a likeness does not entail denying the thing itself. Then he says that there are proofs for this.

Proof 1: The innate intellect (بديهة العقل) does not reject the notion of an existent with unique attributes such that everything else differs from it. Since this is not repudiated by common sense, we know that lack of a peer does not imply non-existence.

Proof 2: This is a reductio argument. If the existence of something depended on the existence of something similar to it, then two similar things would have to share all necessary properties. That would lead to circular dependence (each depending on the other) or self-dependence, which is impossible. Hence existence does not depend on having a likeness, so lack of likeness does not negate existence.

Proof 3: This argument concerns particularity (تعين). Each thing has a specific identity that cannot be transferred to another. If something had a perfect equal, that equal would share its identity, which is impossible. Therefore, the absence of an equal does not imply the thing's non-existence.

The author then applies this to the case of God: those who argue that we cannot conceive of a being that is neither connected nor disconnected from the world unless it has a likeness are refuted. God is the only being described with such transcendence, and we have shown that lacking a peer does not negate His existence. Thus their argument is baseless. The passage ends with 'And with God is success.'

Key Terms
نظير
شبيه
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something has no equal, it must be non-existent.
Correction: This lesson shows that the intellect does not find such a concept inherently impossible; therefore existence does not depend on having a likeness.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does the absence of a likeness not necessitate non-existence?
A) Because the intellect repudiates the idea of a unique being.
B) Because the innate intellect does not consider a unique being impossible.
C) Because everything must have a likeness to exist.
D) Because the proof depends on revelation.

Key Takeaway
The innate intellect accepts the possibility of a unique being, so lacking a likeness does not prove non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that because you are unique in your faith or character, your existence is somehow less real or accepted by others? How does Razi's intuitive proof challenge that feeling, and what does it teach you about the reality of the unique Creator?
?
When you face doubts about God's existence because you cannot compare Him to anything, does your innate intellect accept the possibility of His uniqueness? What would it mean for your faith if you rejected that possibility?
?
This lesson argues that common sense does not reject a unique being. In your daily life, do you live as if uniqueness is a flaw or a strength? How does your answer affect your relationship with a God who has no equal?

Method of Learning
intermediate
7 min
The Second Proof: Dependence on a Likeness Leads to Impossibility
Razi provides the second proof, a reductio ad absurdum about dependence on a likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that existence cannot depend on having a likeness, as it leads to circular or self-dependence which is impossible.

Core Idea
If existence depended on a likeness, it would entangle in impossible circularity.

In the second proof, Imam Razi presents a reductio argument. He assumes that existence of a thing depends on existence of a similar thing, and shows that this leads to a logical contradiction. This reinforces the principle that lack of likeness does not entail non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the dilemma
The proof is structured as a conditional argument. Find the two possibilities stated at the beginning.
2
Follow the reductio chain
Trace why the first option (dependence) is false. Notice the reasoning about equality in concomitants and the resulting circularity.
The key term is 'اللوازم' (necessary concomitants).
3
Understand the conclusion
Since dependence on a likeness leads to impossibility, existence does not require a likeness. Therefore, lack of likeness does not negate existence.
This proof is purely logical and does not rely on intuition.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Third Proof: Particularity Prevents Likeness and Refutation of Opponents
Razi gives the third proof based on particularity and then refutes the opponent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the specific identity of each thing makes it impossible to have an exact equal, and thus the opponent's argument is baseless.

Core Idea
Each thing's unique identity cannot be transferred, so lack of equal is not lack of existence.

The third proof focuses on the concept of particularity (تعين). Razi argues that the specific identity of a thing cannot be found in another, so an exact equal is impossible. Then he applies this to God and refutes those who claim that we cannot conceive of God unless we can imagine a likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the third proof. Notice the term 'تعين' (particularization).
2
Understand the argument about particularity
Razi argues that the particularity of each thing cannot occur in something else; otherwise they would be identical, which is impossible.
This is based on self-evident reason (بديهة العقل).
3
Analyze the refutation of opponents
Razi addresses a specific objection: that we cannot conceive of a being neither connected nor separate unless we find a likeness. He shows this is false because it applies to God.
This connects the abstract principle to the theological case.

Source Text

?

?
Razi says it is impossible for a thing's unique particularity to belong to another. How does this absolute uniqueness of every created thing press you to recognize the even greater uniqueness of the Creator? What in your life needs to be reoriented because of that reality?
?
The objection Razi refutes is that we cannot conceive of something without a likeness. Where in your own heart do you feel more comfortable if God were more 'familiar' or comparable to something you know? What would it cost you to surrender that comfort for the truth that He is without peer?

Surah al-Ikhlas as the Decisive Scriptural Proof
Imam Razi introduces the second chapter and the first scriptural proof, establishing Surah al-Ikhlas as decisive (muhkam).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that Surah al-Ikhlas is a decisive (muhkam) revelation that serves as the primary scriptural proof for Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
Surah al-Ikhlas is a direct, decisive answer from Allah about His essence, refuting all contradictory doctrines.

In this passage, Imam Razi moves from rational proofs to scriptural proofs, beginning with Surah al-Ikhlas. He recounts the occasion of revelation—the Prophet being asked about Allah's essence—and argues that because this surah was a direct response to that question, it must be among the definitive (muhkam) verses of the Quran. This establishes the surah as a foundational text for theological reasoning about Allah's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read Imam Razi's Arabic text and the literal translation side by side. Let the structure of his argument become clear before analyzing details.
Notice the flow from general chapter introduction to specific proof.
2
Identify the core claim
Imam Razi claims that Surah al-Ikhlas must be from the muhkamat (definitive verses), not the mutashabihat (ambiguous ones). Find the reason he provides for this claim.
The reason is tied to the occasion of revelation.
3
Understand the implication
Once the surah's status as muhkam is established, what conclusion does Imam Razi draw? He says any school (madhhab) contradicting it is false. Reflect on why this is significant for theological debates.
This makes the surah an authoritative touchstone for correct belief.

الفصل الثاني في تقرير الدلائل السمعية على أنه سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن الجسمية والحيز والجهة. ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: قوله تعالى: قل هو الله أحد. الله الصمد لم يلد ولم يولد. ولم يكن له كفواً أحد. واعلم: أنه قد اشتهر في التفسير: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل عن ماهية ربه، وعن نحته وصفته؟ فانتظر الجواب من الله تعالى فأنزل الله سبحانه وتعالى هذه السورة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: هذه السورة يجب أن تكون من المحكمات، لا من المتشابهات. لأنه تعالى جعلها جواباً عن سؤال السائل وأنزلها عند الحاجة. وذلك يقتضي كونها من المحكمات، لا من المتشابهات. وإذا ثبت هذا، وجب الجزم بأن كل مذهب يخالف هذه السورة يكون باطلاً.

Commentary
This passage introduces the second chapter of the book, which presents scriptural proofs that Allah is free from corporeality, location, and direction. The first proof is from Surah al-Ikhlas. Imam Razi begins by stating the well-known occasion of revelation: the Prophet was asked about the essence and attributes of Allah, and he awaited a divine response, whereupon this surah was revealed. From this, the author argues that the surah must be among the decisive (muhkam) verses, not ambiguous (mutashabih), because it was a direct answer to a specific query. Therefore, any doctrine that contradicts it is necessarily false. This establishes the surah as an authoritative basis for theological reasoning.

The First Premise: The Necessity of a Cause for Immanence or Transcendence
Razi introduces the first premise and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first premise: that the judgment of immanence or transcendence between two existents requires a cause, and his proof based on the distinction between existents and non-existents.

Core Idea
Every judgment about existents requires a cause.

In this passage, Imam Razi lays the cornerstone of his argument about God's relationship to the world. Before tackling transcendence or immanence directly, he first establishes a methodological principle: any judgment that applies only to some things and not others must have a cause. This logical foundation is crucial for his later proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the opening lines where Razi explains that his argument depends on establishing premises. This sets the stage for the first premise.
Note that Razi is building a systematic case, not jumping to conclusions.
2
Read the premise statement
Read the definition of the first premise: the judgment that every two existents must be either immanent or separate by direction requires a cause and proof.
This judgment is about physical existents in the witnessed world (al-shahid).
3
Follow the proof
Analyze the proof: non-existents do not have this judgment, existents do. Therefore, there must be a cause that distinguishes them.
The proof is an argument from difference: if the judgment applied to both existents and non-existents, no cause would be needed, but it doesn't.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this premise matters for the overall discussion of divine transcendence. It shows that judgments about relationships between existents are not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا الكلام لا يتم إلا بتقرير مقدمات نحن نذكرها وتذكر الوجوه التي يمكن ذكرها في تقرير تلك المقدمات. أما المقدمة الأولى فهي أن نقول قولنا إن القول بأن كل موجودين في الشاهد لا بد وأن يكون أحدهما محايثا للآخر أو مباينا عنه بجهة حكم لابد له من علة ويحتاج إلى دليل والدليل عليه هو أن المعدومات لا يصح فيها هذا الحكم وهذه الموجودات يصح فيها هذا الحكم فلو لا امتياز ما صح فيه هذا الحكم عما لا يصح فيه هذا الحكم بأمر من الأمور وإلا لما كان هذا الامتياز واقعا.
Know that this discourse is not complete except by establishing premises which we will mention and we will mention the aspects that can be mentioned in establishing those premises. As for the first premise, it is that we say: our statement that every two existents in the witnessed world inevitably one is immanent in the other or separate from it by direction is a judgment that must have a cause and needs a proof. The proof for it is that non-existents cannot have this judgment, whereas these existents have this judgment. So if there were no distinction between that which has this judgment and that which does not have this judgment by some thing, then this distinction would not exist.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage introduces the need for three premises to support a certain argument, then provides the first premise and its proof. Razi begins by stating that the argument he is building cannot be completed without establishing some premises. He will list them and the various ways to prove them. The first premise is a statement about any two existents in the world: they must be either immanent (muhayyith) in one another—meaning one is contained within the other spatially or inherently—or separate (mubayin) from one another by a direction (jiha). Razi asserts that this judgment itself requires a cause and a proof. He proves it by pointing out that non-existent things do not have this judgment (since they do not exist to be related), while existents do. Therefore, there must be some distinguishing factor among existents that accounts for this judgment; otherwise, the distinction between existents and non-existents regarding this judgment would be arbitrary.

محايثة
مباينة
علة
شاهد
حكم
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The judgment of immanence or transcendence is self-evident and needs no cause.
Correction: Razi argues that because this judgment applies only to existents and not to non-existents, there must be a cause that distinguishes those that have it from those that do not.

Checkpoint
What is the proof that the judgment of immanence or transcendence between two existents needs a cause?
A) It is self-evident.
B) Non-existents do not have this judgment, so there must be a distinguishing factor among existents.
C) The judgment is based on being a substance or accident.
D) The judgment is based on temporality.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first premise shows that any distinction in judgments about existents must be grounded in a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You hold a conviction about God's relationship to creation—that He is either immanent or transcendent. According to Razi, that judgment itself requires a cause. What is the actual cause in your heart for that conviction? Is it rooted in revelation, reason, or something else? If you cannot name a cause, are you willing to remain in that uncertainty?
?
Razi uses the distinction between existents and non-existents to prove the need for a cause. Think of a distinction you make between truth and falsehood in matters of faith. What is the cause that grounds that distinction in your life? If it is not a firm cause, what would it take for you to seek one?
?
The premise that every judgment about God requires a cause invites you to examine the foundations of your own beliefs. Is there a judgment you hold about God that you have never questioned—something you assume is self-evident? Today, will you trace it back to its cause, even if that means uprooting a comfortable assumption?

Second Premise: Judgment Not Due to Substance or Accident
Razi proves that the judgment of immanence/transcendence cannot be caused by being a substance or accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the judgment of immanence or transcendence cannot be caused by the specific nature of being a substance or accident, and how Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to demonstrate this.

Core Idea
Neither substance nor accident can cause the judgment of immanence or transcendence because each would lead to an impossible division.

In this passage, Razi continues establishing the logical foundations for discussing Allah's relationship to creation. He argues that the judgment of whether something is immanent or transcendent cannot be determined by whether it is a substance or an accident. Instead, he shows through a simple reductio that this judgment must be due to something else. Understanding this premise clears the ground for deeper theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text once to get a feel for Razi's concise argumentation. Then read the English translation to ensure understanding.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
Notice that Razi first considers the case of substance, then the case of accident. The argument structure is parallel for both. Identify the premise, the hypothetical, and the impossibility that follows.
The key logical move: if x were the cause, then an impossible division would follow.
3
Understand the impossibility for substance
Focus on why a substance cannot be immanent in another. A substance is self-subsistent; it does not inhere. Therefore it cannot be divided into those that are immanent and those that are separate.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the cause leads to a contradiction with the definition of substance.
4
Understand the impossibility for accident
Similarly, an accident cannot be separate from another by direction; accidents always inhere in substances. So if being an accident caused the judgment, accidents would have to be divisible into immanent and separate, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية وهي في بيان أن هذا الحكم لا يمكن تعليله بخصوص كونه جوهراً ولا بخصوص كونه عرضا فالدليل عليه أن المقتضى لهذا الحكم لو كان هو كونه جوهراً لصدق على الجوهر أنه منقسم إلى ما يكون محايثا لغيره وإلى ما يكون مباينا عنه ومعلوم أن ذلك محال لأن الجوهر يمتنع أن يكون محايثة لغيره وبهذا الطريق تبين أن المقتضى لهذا الحكم ليس كونه عرضا لامتناع أن يكون العرض مباينا لغيره بالجهة.
As for the second premise, it is in explaining that this judgment cannot be attributed to the particularity of being a substance nor to the particularity of being an accident. The proof for it is that if the efficient cause of this judgment were it being a substance, then it would be true of the substance that it is divided into what is immanent in another and what is separate from it. And it is known that that is impossible, because a substance cannot be immanent in another. And by this way it is shown that the efficient cause of this judgment is not it being an accident, because an accident cannot be separate from another by direction.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage explains the second premise: that the judgment of immanence or transcendence cannot be caused by the specific nature of being a substance (jawhar) or an accident (arad). The proof is a reductio ad absurdum. If the judgment were caused by being a substance, then every substance would have to be divisible into those that are immanent in something else and those that are separate. But that is impossible because a substance, by definition, is self-subsistent and cannot be immanent (i.e., inhere) in another substance. The same method shows that being an accident cannot be the cause, because an accident cannot be separate (mubayin) from another by direction; accidents always inhere in substances. Therefore, the judgment is due to something else.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
محايثة
مباينة
مقتضى
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the judgment of immanence/transcendence is determined by whether something is a substance (separate) or an accident (immanent).
Correction: Razi shows that this cannot be the case because assuming either would lead to a contradiction: if being a substance caused the judgment, then substances would have to be divisible into immanent and separate, but a substance cannot be immanent. Similarly for accidents.

Checkpoint
Why cannot being a substance be the cause of the immanence/transcendence judgment?
A) Substances are indivisible.
B) A substance cannot be immanent in another substance.
C) The judgment only applies to accidents.
D) Substances are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The judgment of immanence or transcendence cannot be caused by the nature of being a substance or accident, because such an assumption would lead to a logical contradiction with their definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever assume that because you belong to a certain category (e.g., 'I am a sinner,' 'I am a scholar'), certain spiritual states must follow? How does Razi's method of stripping away false causes challenge you to examine your assumptions about your relationship with Allah?
?
If the judgment of immanence or transcendence cannot be caused by the nature of the thing itself, then how should you approach understanding Allah's attributes? Are there any ways you have limited Allah based on your own categories?
?
Razi uses logic to eliminate false causes. Identify one false cause you have believed about your own spiritual state (e.g., 'my prayer is not accepted because I am not pious enough'). What would it mean to let go of that assumption and instead rely directly on Allah's mercy?

Author introduces the second part: interpretation of ambiguous texts with a given structure.

Core Idea

This passage marks the beginning of the second major section of the book. Here, the author announces the subject—interpreting ambiguous (mutashabihat) reports and verses—and outlines the structure: an introduction followed by chapters. This provides the roadmap for the entire discussion.

Mutashabihat are texts that are not immediately clear in meaning.

The Necessity of Ta'wil: Quranic Body Parts
Imam Razi gives the first Quranic proof for ta'wil via body parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why literal reading of Quranic anthropomorphic expressions leads to absurd conclusions that require ta'wil.

Core Idea
Literal anthropomorphism is rationally impossible; ta'wil is the only coherent approach.

Imam al-Razi begins by stating the consensus among Islamic schools that ta'wil (interpretation) is necessary for certain apparent meanings in the Quran and hadith. He then provides the first of two Quranic proofs: the mention of body parts like face, eye, side, hands, and leg. Taking these literally would force us to imagine a being with one face, many eyes on that face, one side, many hands, and one leg—a grotesque image no rational person would ascribe to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the structure: a consensus statement followed by the first proof.
The passage begins with 'المقدمة في بيان...' and ends with the conclusion of the first proof.
2
Identify the consensus claim
Find the sentence that states all Islamic schools agree on the necessity of ta'wil. Understand that this is not a minority view.
The claim is that ta'wil is needed for some apparent meanings (zawaher) of both Quran and hadith.
3
Analyze the first proof
Imam Razi enumerates the body parts mentioned in the Quran. Imagine the literal combination: one face, many eyes on that face, one side, many hands, one leg. Why is this absurd?
This imagined form is uglier than anything in the world.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since a rational person would not describe God with such an image, the literal meaning cannot be intended. Ta'wil is necessary.
This proof shows that even if we don't know the exact interpretation, we must reject the literal sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة
في
بيان أن جميع فرق الاسلام مقرون بأنه لابد من التأويل فى بعض ظواهر القرآن والأخبار
أما في القرآن . فبيانه من وجوه :
الأول : هو أنه ورد فى القرآن ذكر الوجه ، وذكر العين ، وذكر الجنب الواحد ، وذكر الأيدى ، وذكر الساق الواحدة ، فلو أخذنا بالظاهر ، يلزمنا إثبات شخص له وجه واحد . وعلى ذلك الوجه أعين كثيرة . وله جنب واحد ، وعليه أيد كثيرة . وله ساق واحدة ولانرى في الدنيا شخصا أقبح صورة من هذه الصورة المتخيلة، ولا أعتقد أن عاقلا يرضى بأن يصف ربه بهذه الصفة
.
الثاني : إنه ورد فى القرآن : أنه تعالى نور السموات والأرض ، وأن كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهية : أن إله العالم ليس هو هذا الشيء المنبسط على الجدران والحيطان ، وليس هو هذا النور الفائض من جرم الشمس والقمر والنار ، فلا بد لكل واحد منا ، من أن يفسر قوله تعالى : « الله نور السموات والأرض » ، بأنه منور السموات والأرض أو بأنه عاد لأهل السموات والأرض، أو بأنه مصلح السموات والأرض .
وكل ذلك تأويل
The introduction: Stating that all Islamic schools are agreed that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary in some apparent meanings (zawaher) of the Quran and the reports (akhbar). As for the Quran, its demonstration is from several aspects (wujuh): The first: It is that in the Quran came the mention of the face, and the mention of the eye, and the mention of the single side, and the mention of the hands, and the mention of the single leg. So if we take them by the apparent meaning (zahir), it would necessitate affirming a person with one face, and on that face many eyes, and for him one side, and on him many hands, and for him one leg. And we do not see in this world a person uglier in form than this imagined form, and I do not think that a rational person would be pleased to describe his Lord with this attribute. The second: That in the Quran came that He, exalted, is the light of the heavens and the earth, and every rational person knows by intuition (badiha) that the God of the world is not this thing spread over walls and partitions, and is not this light emanating from the body of the sun, moon, and fire. So it is necessary for every one of us to interpret His saying, exalted: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' as that He is the illuminator of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the guide for the people of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the reformer of the heavens and the earth. And all that is interpretation (ta'wil).
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an introduction in which Imam al-Razi states the consensus of all Islamic schools that ta'wil (interpretation) is necessary for some apparent meanings (zawaher) of both the Quran and hadith. He then provides two proofs from the Quran itself, each illustrating the necessity of ta'wil. The first proof: The Quran mentions body parts such as face (wajh), eye (ayn), side (janb), hands (aydi), and leg (saq) in singular and plural forms in a way that, if taken literally, would imply a being with one face, many eyes on that face, one side, many hands on that side, and one leg. Such a description is grotesque and no rational person would attribute it to God. Therefore, these terms must be interpreted metaphorically (ta'wil) to mean something befitting God. The second proof: The verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' cannot be taken literally, since physical light is a created thing spread on walls or emanating from celestial bodies. Reason dictates that God is not a physical light. So the verse must be interpreted as meaning that God is the illuminator (munawwir), the guide (haadi, here 'aad likely a variant), or the reformer (muslih) of the heavens and the earth. This shows that ta'wil is unavoidable even for straightforward-seeming verses. Thus, the passage establishes the principle of ta'wil as agreed upon among Muslims.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil)
ظواهر (zawaher)
وجه (wajh)
بديهية (badihiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ta'wil is optional or only for problematic verses.
Correction: Imam Razi states a consensus that ta'wil is necessary for some apparent meanings; it is not optional.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does Imam al-Razi draw from taking the Quranic mentions of face, eye, side, hands, and leg literally?
A) It would imply God has multiple bodies.
B) It would imply a person with one face, many eyes on that face, one side, many hands, and one leg.
C) It would imply God is made of light.
D) It would imply God changes over time.

Key Takeaway
Literal anthropomorphic descriptions of Allah lead to irrational images; thus ta'wil is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself picturing Allah as having a body part like a hand or a face? What does this passage reveal about the danger of such mental images? Will you actively correct that image today?
?
If someone you respect tells you, 'God has a hand because the Quran says so,' how will you use Imam al-Razi's argument to defend Allah's transcendence without sounding disrespectful?
?
When you recite verses like 'And your Lord comes' (Quran 89:22), what thought crosses your mind first? Are you humble enough to admit you don't know the 'how,' or do you secretly imagine a physical approach? What will you change about your reflection on such verses?

The Necessity of Ta'wil: The Light Verse
Imam Razi gives the second Quranic proof for ta'wil using the Light verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' cannot be taken literally and requires ta'wil.

Core Idea
God is not physical light; ta'wil rescues the verse from absurdity.

Imam al-Razi presents the second Quranic proof: the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth.' Every rational person knows intuitively (badiha) that God is not the physical light spread on walls or emanating from celestial bodies. Therefore, the verse must be interpreted metaphorically—for example, as meaning that Allah is the illuminator, guide, or reformer of the heavens and the earth. This shows that even seemingly straightforward verses require ta'wil.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'الثاني'. Note the structure: the verse, the intuitive objection, and the proposed interpretations.
The passage ends with 'وكل ذلك تأويل' (all that is ta'wil).
2
Understand the intuitive knowledge
Imam Razi says every rational person knows by badiha (intuition) that God is not the physical light on walls or from sun, moon, fire. Why is this knowledge immediate?
Badiha refers to self-evident truths that require no proof.
3
Examine the possible interpretations
The Imam suggests three possible ta'wils: God is the illuminator (munawwir), the guide (haadi), or the reformer (muslih) of the heavens and earth. These avoid attributing createdness to Allah.
The word 'عاد' in the text may be a corruption of 'هادي' (guide).
4
Conclude the necessity of ta'wil
Since the literal meaning is impossible, interpretation is unavoidable. This reinforces the consensus that ta'wil is necessary for some Quranic verses.
The final sentence 'وكل ذلك تأويل' emphasizes that all these are forms of ta'wil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة
في
بيان أن جميع فرق الاسلام مقرون بأنه لابد من التأويل فى بعض ظواهر القرآن والأخبار
أما في القرآن . فبيانه من وجوه :
الأول : هو أنه ورد فى القرآن ذكر الوجه ، وذكر العين ، وذكر الجنب الواحد ، وذكر الأيدى ، وذكر الساق الواحدة ، فلو أخذنا بالظاهر ، يلزمنا إثبات شخص له وجه واحد . وعلى ذلك الوجه أعين كثيرة . وله جنب واحد ، وعليه أيد كثيرة . وله ساق واحدة ولانرى في الدنيا شخصا أقبح صورة من هذه الصورة المتخيلة، ولا أعتقد أن عاقلا يرضى بأن يصف ربه بهذه الصفة
.
الثاني : إنه ورد فى القرآن : أنه تعالى نور السموات والأرض ، وأن كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهية : أن إله العالم ليس هو هذا الشيء المنبسط على الجدران والحيطان ، وليس هو هذا النور الفائض من جرم الشمس والقمر والنار ، فلا بد لكل واحد منا ، من أن يفسر قوله تعالى : « الله نور السموات والأرض » ، بأنه منور السموات والأرض أو بأنه عاد لأهل السموات والأرض، أو بأنه مصلح السموات والأرض .

The introduction: Stating that all Islamic schools are agreed that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary in some apparent meanings (zawaher) of the Quran and the reports (akhbar). As for the Quran, its demonstration is from several aspects (wujuh): The first: It is that in the Quran came the mention of the face, and the mention of the eye, and the mention of the single side, and the mention of the hands, and the mention of the single leg. And we do not see in this world a person uglier in form than this imagined form, and I do not think that a rational person would be pleased to describe his Lord with this attribute. The second: That in the Quran came that He, exalted, is the light of the heavens and the earth, and every rational person knows by intuition (badiha) that the God of the world is not this thing spread over walls and partitions, and is not this light emanating from the body of the sun, moon, and fire. So it is necessary for every one of us to interpret His saying, exalted: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' as that He is the illuminator of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the guide for the people of the heavens and the earth, or that He is the reformer of the heavens and the earth. And all that is interpretation (ta'wil).

This passage is an introduction in which Imam al-Razi states the consensus of all Islamic schools that ta'wil (interpretation) is necessary for some apparent meanings (zawaher) of both the Quran and hadith. He then provides two proofs from the Quran itself, each illustrating the necessity of ta'wil. The second proof: The verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' cannot be taken literally, since physical light is a created thing spread on walls or emanating from celestial bodies. This shows that ta'wil is unavoidable even for straightforward-seeming verses. Thus, the passage establishes the principle of ta'wil as agreed upon among Muslims.

If not, what will you do to correct it?

Conclusion: Ta'wil is Necessary for Every Rational Person
Imam Razi summarizes that all examples prove ta'wil is obligatory, and the mutakallimun set the rule for interpreting scriptural expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi concludes that resorting to ta'wil is obligatory for every rational person and how the mutakallimun establish their interpretive rule based on divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Ta'wil is not optional: every rational person must interpret ambiguous texts.

Imam Razi closes his introduction with a powerful summary. He declares that all the preceding examples have established one crucial point: ta'wil is a necessity for every rational person, not just for theologians. He then explains the rule set by the mutakallimun for interpreting scriptural expressions in light of Allah's transcendence. This passage encapsulates the entire purpose of the book's introduction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the author's final words sink in before analyzing them.
Notice the phrase 'لئلا للطعن فيها' – the scribe likely intended 'لئلا يطعن فيها'.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes two interconnected claims: first, that ta'wil is necessary for every rational person; second, that the mutakallimun derived a rule based on the proof of Allah's transcendence beyond direction and body.
3
Reflect on the misconception
Some might think ta'wil is only for specialists, but the author explicitly says it is necessary for every rational person. Ponder why this distinction matters for defending scripture.
This misconception is addressed in the lesson's misconception section.
4
Connect to the book's purpose
This passage is the conclusion of the introduction. Consider how it sets the stage for the rest of the work, where the author will apply this principle to specific verses and hadiths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت بكل ما ذكرنا : أن المصير إلى التأويل : أمر لابد منه لكل عاقل . وعند هذا قال المتكلمون : لما ثبت بالدليل أنه سبحانه و تعالى منزه عن الجهة والجسمية ، وجب علينا أن نضع لهذه الألفاظ الواردة فى القرآن والأخبار : محملا صحيحا ، لئلا للطعن فيها . فهذا تمام القول في المقدمة ) وبالله التوفيق )
So it is established by all that we have mentioned that resorting to ta'wil is a matter necessary for every rational person. And upon this, the mutakallimun said: since it is established by proof that He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent beyond direction and corporeality, it is incumbent upon us to assign a correct interpretation to these expressions occurring in the Quran and the hadiths, so that [there be no] ?? objection to them. So this is the complete statement on the introduction, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is the concluding statement of the introduction (muqaddima) of the book. The author summarizes the entire argument: through all the examples given, it is established that resorting to ta'wil (interpretation) is obligatory for every rational person. Then he reports the position of the mutakallimun (theologians), who say: since it has been proven through rational evidence that Allah the Exalted is absolutely transcendent beyond direction (jiha) and body (jism), we must assign a sound interpretation to all the scriptural expressions (from the Quran and hadith) that might otherwise imply anthropomorphism. The purpose of this is to protect these texts from being attacked or rejected by rationalists. The passage ends with a closing remark that this concludes the introduction, asking for divine success.

Misconception: Ta'wil is only for the mutakallimun or specialists, not for every Muslim.
Correction: The passage clearly states that ta'wil is necessary for every rational person (لابد منه لكل عاقل), meaning anyone who can reason must engage in ta'wil when confronted with ambiguous scriptural expressions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Resorting to ta'wil is an obligation for every rational person because rational proof establishes Allah's transcendence, and scriptural expressions must be interpreted to align with this truth.

The author says ta'wil is necessary for every rational person, not just scholars. Have you ever excused yourself from engaging with such interpretations by saying 'I am not a scholar'? How does this passage challenge that excuse, and what will you do to fulfill this obligation?
?
The purpose of ta'wil is to protect the texts from being attacked or rejected. Are you more concerned with defending the literal words of the Quran or with upholding the truth of Allah's transcendence? Which motive is stronger in your heart, and why does it matter?

The Necessity of Ta'wil: Evidence from Hadith
Razi cites a hadith to prove the necessity of ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that ta'wil (interpretation) is necessary for every rational person, based on the Prophet's hadith about Ayat al-Kursi.

Core Idea
Even the greatest Quranic verse requires interpretation — so all anthropomorphic passages must be interpreted.

Imam Razi presents a powerful proof for the necessity of ta'wil. He cites a hadith where the Prophet points to Ayat al-Kursi as the greatest verse and then indicates that it requires interpretation. This establishes that no scriptural text can be taken literally when it comes to divine attributes. In this lesson, we will unpack the argument and see how it leads to the theologians' conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the dialogue: the Prophet asks, Ubayy answers, the Prophet confirms and adds a comment about interpretation.
2
Identify the conclusion Razi draws
After the hadith, Razi states: 'So it is established... that resorting to interpretation is a necessity for every rational person.' This is the main point.
3
Understand the theologians' principle
Razi then explains the mutakallimun's position: once reason proves Allah is beyond direction and body, we must assign correct interpretations to scriptural expressions to avoid fault.
This connects revelation to rational proof.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider: if even the greatest verse requires ta'wil, how much more do other anthropomorphic verses need interpretation? This counters the claim that ta'wil is an innovation.
The hadith itself demands interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : قال عليه السلام لأبي بن كعب : « يا أبا المنذر . أي آية في كتاب الله تعالى أعظم ؟ فتردد فيه مرتين . ثم قال في الثالثة : آية الكرسي . فضرب يده - عليه السلام - على صدره ، وقال : « أصبت . والذي نفسي بيده . إن لها لسانا يقدس الله تعالى عند العرش ، ولا بد فيه من التأويل » . فثبت بكل ما ذكرنا : أن المصير إلى التأويل : أمر لابد منه لكل عاقل . وعند هذا قال المتكلمون : لما ثبت بالدليل أنه سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن الجهة والجسمية ، وجب علينا أن نضع لهذه الألفاظ الواردة في القرآن والأخبار : محملا صحيحا ، لئلا يطعن فيها . فهذا تمام القول في المقدمة وبالله التوفيق
The tenth: He (peace be upon him) said to Ubayy ibn Ka'b: 'O Abu al-Mundhir, which verse in the Book of Allah the Exalted is greatest?' He hesitated twice, then said the third time: 'The Throne Verse (Ayat al-Kursi).' He (peace be upon him) struck his chest and said: 'You are correct. By the One in Whose hand is my soul, it has a tongue that glorifies Allah the Exalted at the Throne, and it requires interpretation (ta'wil).' So it is established by all that we have mentioned: that resorting to interpretation is a necessity for every rational person. And at this point the theologians (mutakallimun) said: When it is established by proof that He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent beyond direction (jiha) and corporeality (jismiyya), we must assign a correct interpretation (mahmal) to these expressions that occur in the Quran and traditions, lest one find fault with them. This completes the discourse on the introduction, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) for the necessity of interpretive ta'wil of anthropomorphic verses and hadith. It begins by citing a prophetic hadith in which the Prophet asked Ubayy ibn Ka'b about the greatest verse in the Quran. Ubayy identified Ayat al-Kursi. The Prophet then revealed that this verse has a tongue that glorifies Allah at the Throne, and that this very fact requires interpretation. The author uses this to show that even the greatest verse cannot be taken literally—if it required interpretation, then other verses certainly do. He then generalizes: the conclusion is that resorting to ta'wil is indispensable for any rational person. Next, he reports the position of the theologians: since rational proof has established that Allah is free from spatial location (jiha) and body (jism), we must provide a sound metaphorical interpretation for any scriptural expression that would otherwise imply these things. The goal is to avoid criticism of the sacred texts. The passage ends by marking the close of the introduction.

Key Terms
التأويل
الجهة
الجسمية
المتكلمون
المحمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'wil is a later innovation introduced by theologians without scriptural basis.
Correction: The Prophet himself indicated that even the greatest Quranic verse (Ayat al-Kursi) requires interpretation, showing that ta'wil is rooted in revelation and is a rational necessity.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Imam Razi draw from the hadith of Ubayy ibn Ka'b?
A) That Ayat al-Kursi is the greatest verse.
B) That the Prophet's words require ta'wil.
C) That ta'wil is necessary for every rational person.
D) That the Throne has a tongue.

Key Takeaway
Ta'wil is not an optional interpretive tool; it is a rational necessity established by revelation itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
