Two Types of Discussion on Anthropomorphic Verses
Razi divides the discussion into two types: proof of transcendence and ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-method approach used by Imam Razi to handle anthropomorphic verses: rational proof of transcendence and hermeneutical interpretation.

Core Idea
Kalam methodology: first establish transcendence rationally, then interpret texts accordingly.

In this passage, Imam Razi outlines the dual strategy he will employ to address verses that seem to attribute physical movement to Allah. He distinguishes between rational demonstration of Allah's transcendence and the need for interpretation (ta'wil) of problematic texts. This two-step method is foundational in classical kalam for reconciling reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the clear division into 'two types'.
2
Identify the Two Types
The first type is proof of transcendence; the second is interpretation (ta'wil). Understand what each entails.
3
Understand the Order
Why is transcendence established first? This provides the rational framework for interpreting the verses.
4
Reflect on the Method
See how this dual approach protects both reason and scripture from conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن الكلام في قوله : هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم الله في ظل من الفهام ، من نوعين : الأول : أن نبين بالدلائل القاهرة أنه سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن المجيء والذهاب . والثاني : أن تذكر التأويلات في هذه الآيات .
And know: that the discussion concerning His statement: 'Do they wait for anything except that Allah should come to them in the shadows of the clouds' is of two types: The first: that we demonstrate with compelling proofs that He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent over coming and going. And the second: that we mention the interpretations regarding these verses.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a methodological classification. The author introduces the two-pronged approach he will take in addressing the verse that seems to attribute physical movement to Allah. The first type involves rational demonstration ( دلائل قاهرة ) that Allah is absolutely transcendent ( منزه ) above the concepts of 'coming' and 'going' ( المجيء والذهاب ) — this is the negative theological argument. The second type involves hermeneutical interpretation ( التأويلات ) of the verses that appear to contradict this transcendence, accepting the necessity of interpreting such anthropomorphic expressions. This two-fold method is a standard kalam approach to scripture: first establish the rational principle (tanzīh), then reconcile the revealed texts with it through ta'wīl.

Key Terms
المجيء والذهاب
التأويل
الدلائل القاهرة
منزه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This method rejects or denies the apparent meaning of anthropomorphic verses entirely.
Correction: The method first establishes transcendence rationally, then interprets the verses in a way consistent with that transcendence, not rejecting them outright.

Checkpoint
What are the two types of discussion for the verse?
A) Proof of judgment and proof of mercy.
B) Proof of transcendence and interpretation of verses.
C) Proof of existence and proof of attributes.
D) Proof of creation and proof of resurrection.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's approach to anthropomorphic verses is twofold: rationally prove Allah's transcendence, then interpret the verses accordingly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to describe Allah in human terms, do you first establish His transcendence rationally, or do you immediately look for an interpretation? Which approach does this lesson demand of you, and why might yours need to change?
?
Razi insists on 'compelling proofs' before interpretation. Do you have a rational foundation for your belief in Allah's transcendence, or have you merely accepted it without evidence? What is one proof you can articulate today to strengthen your conviction?

Figurative Interpretation of 'Foot' in Authentic Hadith
Al-Razi offers a figurative interpretation of the hadith if it is considered authentic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses figurative interpretation (ta'wil) to reconcile problematic hadith texts with theological principles.

Core Idea
Even authentic texts may require figurative interpretation to preserve theological coherence.

Al-Razi presents a final line of defense: even if we accept the authenticity of the hadith, its words are not necessarily literal. He demonstrates, using common Arabic usage, that 'placing the foot' can be a metaphor for intervening to resolve a dispute. This lesson explores that figurative interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Al-Razi begins with a conditional assumption.
The phrase 'if we assume authenticity' sets the stage for figurative interpretation.
2
Identify the figurative usage
Find where Al-Razi gives examples from common speech. How does he link 'placing a foot' to conflict resolution?
He uses the examples of removing a quarrel and calming turmoil.
3
Reflect on theological significance
Ask yourself: why is this figurative interpretation important for defending God's transcendence?
This avoids attributing physical body parts to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : إن قلنا بتقدير صحة هذه الألفاظ فهي محتملة للتأويل . فإن من سعى لإزالة خصومة ، وتسكين فتنة ، صح أن يقال: إن فلانا وضع رجله في هذه الواقعة ، ووضع قدمه فيها . ويقال في المجاز المتعارف الظاهر : لك قدم مبارك ، وضع قدمك فيها حتى يصلح ويزول
Then we say: If we assume the authenticity of these words, they are open to interpretation. For one who strives to remove a quarrel and calm a turmoil, it is correctly said: 'So-and-so placed his foot in this incident and placed his foot in it.' And it is said in common obvious figurative speech: 'You have a blessed foot, place your foot in it so that it may be set right and cease.'
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This final passage offers an alternative approach. Even if we grant that the hadith texts are authentic, they can be interpreted figuratively (ta'wil). The author points out that in common Arabic usage, placing one's foot in a situation is a metaphor for intervening to resolve a conflict. Thus, the hadith's mention of God placing His foot is not literal but a figurative expression meaning that He settles the dispute between Paradise and Hellfire.

Correction: Al-Razi applies ta'wil to hadith as well, showing that even authentic prophetic traditions can require figurative interpretation when a literal reading conflicts with foundational theological principles.

Al-Razi shows that even authentic hadith can be interpreted figuratively when literal reading contradicts theological principles.

The Analogy Between Body Parts and Direction
Razi presents the first answer: a general methodological refutation using analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses an analogy from the denial of God's body parts to argue for the necessity of metaphorical interpretation of verses about direction.

Core Idea
Definitive rational proofs require metaphorical interpretation of ambiguous scripture, just as for body parts.

Razi begins his response to the Karamiyya by outlining two types of answers. The first is a powerful methodological move: he draws an analogy between the denial of God's body parts and the denial of direction, showing that the same principle of prioritizing decisive rational proofs over apparent scriptural meanings applies in both cases. This establishes a consistent hermeneutic and leads to adopting the Salaf's safe approach of tafwid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Razi's argumentative flow. Notice how he states there are two types of answers, then elaborates the first.
The passage is dense but follows a clear logical sequence.
2
Identify the core analogy
Find where Razi compares the denial of body parts to the denial of direction. He argues that just as definitive proofs against body parts require metaphorical interpretation, so too do proofs against direction.
This is the heart of the methodological argument.
3
Trace the objection handling
Razi anticipates an objection: the Karamiyya might doubt the certainty of the rational proofs. He responds by saying that if needed, we can debate and establish their certainty, after which they cannot be countered by mere scriptural appearances.
4
Conclude with the Salafi method
Razi concludes by adopting the way of the Salaf: once we know that Allah does not intend direction, we do not need to specify the exact metaphorical meaning. This is safer and avoids contention.
This shows a commitment to both rationality and scriptural reverence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
راعلم : أن لنا فى الجواب عن هذه الكلمات : نوعان من الجواب : النوع الأول : أن نقول للكرامية : أنتم ساعدتمونا على أن ظواهر القرآن ، وإن دلت على إثبات الأعضاء والجوارح الله تعالى . فإنه يجب القطع بنفيها عن الله تعالى ، والجزم بأنه منزه عنها . وما ذاك إلا أنه لما قامت الدلائل القطعية على استحالة الأعضاء والجوارح على الله تعالى ، وجب القطع بتنزيه الله عنها ، والجزم بأن مراد الله تعالى من تلك الظواهر ، شيء آخر . فكذا في هذه المسألة : نحن ذكرنا الدلائل العقلية القاطعة في أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يكون مختصا بالمكان والجهة والحيز . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب القطع بأن مراد الله تعالى من هذه الظواهر التي تمسكتم بها ، شيء آخر سوى إثبات الجهة الله تعالى . وهذا إلزام قاطع ، وكلام قوى . إلا أن نقول : إن تلك الدلائل العقلية التي تمسكتم بها ، ليست قطعية . بل هي محتملة . فنحن إذا يجب علينا أن نتكلم معكم في تقرير تلك الدلائل، ودفع وجوه الاحتمال عنها. فثبت بهذا الطريق : أنا متى بينا أن تلك الدلائل العقلية قاطعة يقينية لم تقدر الكرامية على معارضة تلك العقليات اليقينية بهذه الظواهر . وهذا كلام فى غاية القوة . وعند هذا نختار مذهب السلف ، ونقول : لما عرفنا بتلك القواطع العقلية : أنه ليس مراد الله تعالى من هذه الآيات : إثبات الجهة الله تعالى ، فلا حاجة بنا بعد ذلك إلى بيان أن مراد الله تعالى من هذه الآيات ما هو ؟ وهذا الطريق أسلم في ذوق النظر ، وعن الشغب أبعد
We are aware that we have in response to these words two types of response: The first type: that we say to the Karamiyya: You have helped us that the apparent meanings of the Quran, even if they indicate the establishment of limbs and organs for Allah Almighty, then it is necessary to decisively negate them from Allah Almighty and to firmly assert that He is transcendent above them. And that is only because when definitive proofs were established for the impossibility of limbs and organs for Allah Almighty, it became necessary to decisively declare God's transcendence above them and to firmly assert that Allah Almighty's intent from those apparent meanings is something else. Similarly in this issue: we have adduced definitive rational proofs that it is impossible for Him to be confined to place, direction, and space. And since that is the case, it becomes necessary to decisively assert that Allah Almighty's intent from these apparent meanings that you have clung to is something other than establishing direction for Allah Almighty. And this is a conclusive argument and a strong statement. Unless we say: those rational proofs that you have clung to are not definitive but are probabilistic. Then we must argue with you regarding the establishment of those proofs and repelling the possibilities from them. Thus it is established by this method: that whenever we demonstrate that those rational proofs are definitive and certain, the Karamiyya cannot oppose those certain rational proofs with these apparent meanings. And this is an extremely strong statement. And at this point we choose the way of the Salaf, and we say: since we have known by those rational proofs that it is not Allah's intent from these verses to establish direction for Allah, then we have no need after that to explain what Allah's intent from these verses is. And this way is safer in the taste of theoretical investigation and further from contention.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a general methodological argument. Razi first states that he will provide two types of answers to the arguments of the Karamiyya for God being in a direction. The first type is an argument from analogy. He says: You Karamiyya already agree that the apparent meaning of Quranic verses that mention God's body parts cannot be taken literally because rational proofs demonstrate the impossibility of God having a body. Similarly, we have brought rational proofs that God cannot be confined to a place or direction. So you must apply the same principle to verses about direction: the apparent meaning must be interpreted metaphorically. Then Razi addresses a possible objection: if the Karamiyya question whether those rational proofs are truly decisive, then we can debate the proofs themselves and show they are certain. Once the rational proofs are established, the Karamiyya cannot counter them with mere apparent meanings of scripture. At that point, Razi says we adopt the way of the Salaf, which is to affirm God's transcendence without specifying the exact metaphorical interpretation of the verses. This approach is safer and avoids contention.

Key Terms
ظواهر
دلائل قطعية
دلائل عقلية
الكرامية
الجهة
مذهب السلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi rejects the apparent meaning of scripture entirely when it conflicts with reason.
Correction: Razi does not reject scripture; he prioritizes definitive rational proofs and interprets metaphorical meanings, consistent with the Salaf's approach of tafwid.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first type of answer, why must verses implying a direction for God be interpreted metaphorically?
A) Because all Quranic verses are metaphorical.
B) Because rational proofs definitively show God cannot be in a direction, just as they show He cannot have a body.
C) Because the Karamiyya said so.
D) Because the Salaf rejected all interpretations.

Key Takeaway
Razi's general methodological refutation establishes that definitive rational proofs necessitate metaphorical interpretation of scripture, following the Salaf's way of entrusting the meaning to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse or hadith that seems to describe Allah in a way that your reason finds impossible, do you instinctively trust your reason or the apparent text? Which has the final say in your heart? This lesson demands you choose. Are you willing to entrust the meaning to Allah like the Salaf, or do you feel compelled to explain it yourself? What does that reveal about your trust in His transcendence?
?
Razi says the Salaf's way is 'safer in the taste of theoretical investigation and further from contention.' Is safety from contention your priority when it comes to Allah's attributes, or do you crave detailed explanations? Which path pleases Allah more—your intellectual comfort or your humble submission? Examine your intention.
?
Have you ever criticized someone for interpreting a verse metaphorically, thinking they were abandoning scripture? This lesson shows that definitive rational proofs can necessitate such interpretation. Could your criticism actually be a rejection of Allah's command to use reason? Ask yourself: where is your ultimate authority—your understanding of the text, or the conclusive proofs of reason that uphold God's transcendence?

Rational Proofs and the Salaf Method
Imam Razi argues that rational certainties (al-qawati' al-'aqliyya) override ambiguous verses, so we need not specify intended meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that rational certainties override literal interpretations of ambiguous verses, and how this leads to adopting the Salaf method of not specifying intended meanings.

Core Idea
Rational certainties are decisive; ambiguous verses cannot oppose them.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a powerful argument against the Karramiyya who insist on literal interpretations of verses that seem to imply direction for Allah. He asserts that once rational proofs establish certainty about Allah's transcendence, the apparent meanings of such verses cannot overrule them. This leads him to adopt the method of the Salaf: refraining from specifying what Allah intends by those verses, as such specification is unnecessary after rational proof. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong language: 'في غاية القوة' (extremely strong).
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence where Razi says rational proofs are decisive and that the Karramiyya cannot oppose them with apparent meanings. This is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the Salaf method
Razi then says he chooses the school of the Salaf. Explain why not needing to specify the intended meaning follows from rational certainty. Also note the benefit mentioned: safer in investigation and further from contentiousness.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And this speech is extremely strong. Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's first general approach to refuting the Karramiyya's reliance on literal interpretations of ambiguous verses. He argues that once rational proofs (al-qawati' al-'aqliyya) are established as certain and decisive, the apparent meanings of scriptural texts cannot oppose them. This, he says, is an extremely strong argument. He then declares that this leads to adopting the method of the Salaf: since we know through rational certainties that Allah does not intend to affirm a direction for Himself from these verses, we do not need to specify exactly what He does intend. This method is safer in intellectual investigation and further from disputation.

Razi Identifies the Philosophical Basis of Ghazali's Throne Explanation
Razi notes the philosophical underpinnings of Ghazali's Throne explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Razi critiques Ghazali's explanation of the Throne by showing it relies on philosophical principles rather than pure revelation, and that he has addressed this topic elsewhere in his rational works.

Core Idea
Ghazali's Throne explanation rests on a philosophical chain, not solely on scripture.

Imam Razi makes a brief but powerful methodological observation: the explanation of the Throne offered by al-Ghazali is not grounded in revealed texts alone. Instead, it is built on a specific philosophical framework—a chain of influence descending from the Creator through the intellect to the celestial and then the sublunar worlds. In this lesson, you will see how Razi identifies this reliance and signals his own critical stance, pointing us to his purely rational works for a deeper discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the English translation. Notice the concise style: Razi outlines the entire philosophical chain in one sentence.
This passage is a single sentence with a clear structure.
2
Identify the chain of influence
The passage describes a three-level chain: Creator → intellect → governance of the higher world → governance of the lower world. Make sure you understand each link.
This chain is reminiscent of Neoplatonic emanationism.
3
Understand Razi's critical distance
Razi does not endorse this chain. Instead, he says 'we have discussed it in purely rational books,' indicating he has dealt with this philosophical framework critically elsewhere. This is a signal that the argument is not being accepted uncritically.
Razi's phrase 'purely rational books' suggests a separate, more rigorous critique.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider what this means for theological methodology: sometimes arguments that appear scriptural actually rely on philosophical assumptions. Razi is teaching us to be aware of such influences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن هذا الكلام مبنى على أصول الحكماء في أن تأثير البارى تعالى في العقل ، وتأثير العقل فى تدبير العالم العلوى ، وتأثير تدبير العالم العلوى فى السفلى . وقد تكلمنا عليه في الكتب العقلية المحضة
Know that this speech is based on the principles of the philosophers, that the influence of the Creator, exalted, is upon the intellect, and the influence of the intellect is upon the governance of the higher world, and the influence of the governance of the higher world is upon the lower world. And we have discussed it in purely rational books.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This is a brief methodological remark by Imam Razi. He points out that the explanation of the Throne's specification given by al-Ghazali (in the previous lesson) is not based on revealed texts alone but on the principles of the philosophers (ḥukamā')—specifically, a chain of influence: from the Creator to the intellect, from the intellect to the governance of the celestial world, and from there to the sublunar world. Razi does not endorse this here; instead, he states that he has dealt with this topic in his purely rational (ʿaqliyya maḥḍa) works, implying a critical or at least independent stance. This shows Razi's awareness of the philosophical underpinnings of some theological arguments and his preference for a purely rational critique of such ideas.

The Philosophical Foundation of Causality
Razi presents the philosophical causal hierarchy underlying his argument.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Razi builds his argument on a chain of causality from Creator to Intellect to Worlds.

Razi begins by laying out the philosophical framework that underlies his entire discussion. He states that the argument rests on the principles of the philosophers (أصول الحكماء) concerning a hierarchical chain of influence. Before engaging with the theological proofs, we must grasp this causal model, as it shapes everything that follows.

Why Solitary Reports Are Invalid for Theology
Imam Razi argues that akhbar al-ahad are conjectural and thus invalid for knowledge of Allah's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) are only conjectural and therefore cannot be used as a basis for knowledge of Allah and His attributes.

Core Idea
Solitary reports are conjectural because narrators are not infallible; conjecture cannot establish truth.

Imam Razi begins a new chapter in Asas al-Taqdis by addressing the use of solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) in theology. He asserts that such reports are conjectural (maznoon) and thus invalid for knowledge of Allah. In this passage, he presents the first proof: since narrators are not infallible, their reports cannot yield certainty. He then distinguishes between subsidiary legal matters (furu') where conjecture is acceptable and foundational principles (usul) where certainty is required.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a concise, argumentative style.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage opens with a clear thesis: solitary reports cannot be used for knowledge of Allah. Find that statement and note the strong language.
The claim is stated at the beginning: 'أما التمسك بخير الواحد في معرفة الله تعالى فغير جائز'.
3
Understand the reasoning
Razi provides a proof: solitary reports are conjectural because narrators are not infallible. Trace the logic: lack of infallibility → possibility of error and lying → report is conjectural, not certain.
He uses a polemical example against the Rafidah to emphasize that claiming infallibility for narrators would be extreme.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
Razi cites four verses to show that conjecture is not acceptable in matters of truth. Read each verse and understand how it supports his argument.
The verses are: 53:28 (twice), 17:36, and 7:33.
5
Note the distinction between furu' and usul
Razi closes by allowing solitary reports in subsidiary legal matters (furu') because flexibility is needed, but not in foundational principles (usul). Reflect on why this distinction is important.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الحادي والثلاثون في كلام كلى في أخبار الآحاد نقول : أما التمسك بخير الواحد في معرفة الله تعالى فغير جائز ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن أخبار الآحاد مظنونة . فلا يجوز التمسك بها في معرفة الله تعالى وصفاته . وإنما قلنا . إنها مظنونة . وذلك لأنا أجمعنا على أن الرواة ليسوا معصومين وكيف؟ والروافض لما اتفقوا على عصمة علي وحده هؤلاء المحدثون كفروهم . وإذا كان القول بعصمة علي - كرم الله وجهه - يوجب عليهم تكفير القائلين بعصمة علي فكيف يمكنهم القول بعصمة هؤلاء الرواة ؟ وإذا لم يكونوا معصومين ، كان الخطأ عليهم جائزاً ، والكذب عليهم جائزاً. وحينئذ لا يكون صدقهم معلوما ، بل مظنونا ، فثبت : أن خبر الواحد مظنون . فوجب أن لا يجوز التمسك به . لقوله تعالى : إن الظن لا يغنى من الحق شينا ، ولقوله تعالى في صفة الكفار : إن يتبعون إلا الظن ولقوله : ولا تقف ما ليس لك به علم ولقوله : وأن تقولوا على الله مالا تعلمون، ترك العمل فى فروع الشريعة - لأن المطلوب فيها الفن – فوجب أن يبقى في مسائل الأصول على هذا الأصل .
The thirty-first chapter: A general discussion on solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad). We say: As for using a solitary report in knowledge of Allah the Most High, it is not permissible, and proofs indicate that. The first: Solitary reports are conjectural (maznoon). So it is not permissible to rely on them in knowledge of Allah the Most High and His attributes. We say they are conjectural because we all agree that the narrators are not infallible. How? The Rafidah, when they agreed on the infallibility of Ali alone, these hadith scholars deemed them disbelievers. If the claim of the infallibility of Ali (may Allah honor his face) obliges them to declare those who claim the infallibility of Ali as disbelievers, how can they claim the infallibility of these narrators? And if they are not infallible, then error is possible from them, and lying is possible from them. At that point, their truthfulness is not known with certainty, but only conjectured. So it is established that a solitary report is conjectural. Therefore, it is obligatory not to rely on it, due to His saying, the Most High: 'Indeed, conjecture does not avail against the truth at all' (53:28), and His saying in describing the disbelievers: 'They follow only conjecture' (53:28), and His saying: 'And do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge' (17:36), and His saying: 'And that you say about Allah what you do not know' (7:33). Acting upon it in subsidiary branches of the law (furu' al-shari'ah) is permissible because what is required in them is flexibility, so it must remain in matters of principles (usul) upon this principle.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage from Imam Razi's Asas al-Taqdis begins a new chapter (31) on the general discussion of solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad). The author states that using a solitary report for knowledge of Allah and His attributes is not permissible, and he provides the first of several proofs. The proof is that such reports are conjectural (maznoon). The reasoning is that all scholars agree that narrators are not infallible. He makes a polemical point against the Rafidah (a Shi'a group) who claim the infallibility of Ali alone; the hadith scholars among the Sunni consider them disbelievers for that claim. If claiming infallibility for Ali is deemed disbelief, how can one claim infallibility for ordinary narrators? Since narrators are not infallible, error and lying are possible from them, so their truthfulness is not known with certainty but is only conjectured. Therefore, the report is conjectural. It is not permissible to rely on conjecture for matters of truth, citing four Quranic verses. Imam Razi then distinguishes between subsidiary branches of law (furu' al-shari'ah) where acting on such reports is allowed because flexibility is required, but in matters of principles (usul), the original rule of requiring certainty remains.

عصمة
رواة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since ahad hadith are accepted in fiqh, they are also acceptable in theology.
Correction: Imam Razi explicitly distinguishes between the two fields: solitary reports are permissible in subsidiary legal matters (furu') due to flexibility, but not in foundational principles (usul) where certainty is required.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why are solitary reports considered conjectural?

D) Because they contradict the Quran

Key Takeaway
Solitary reports are conjectural because narrators are fallible, and conjecture is not sufficient for theological knowledge.

When you find yourself making a statement about Allah, do you pause to ask: 'Do I know this for certain, or am I relying on conjecture?' Imam Razi warns that conjecture does not avail against the truth. Are you willing to stop speaking about Allah based on weak evidence, even if it means admitting ignorance?
?
Imam Razi distinguishes between matters of principle (usul) and practical law (furu'). In your daily life, do you treat your beliefs about Allah with the same rigor as you treat your religious practices, or have you allowed laxity in your theology? Razi's First Proof: Isolated Reports Are Speculative
Razi presents the first proof that akhbar al-ahad are speculative and cannot be used for divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that isolated reports cannot be used for theological knowledge because they yield only probability, not certainty.

Core Idea
Isolated reports yield only probability, and theology requires certainty.

This passage presents the first proof in Razi's critique of using isolated reports in theology. He establishes that such reports are inherently speculative due to the fallibility of narrators, then cites Quranic verses condemning reliance on assumption. This lesson unpacks Razi's reasoning and its implications for theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Razi's original Arabic and the English translation. Notice the concise, argumentative style.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
What is Razi's thesis about isolated reports? He states it clearly at the beginning.
3
Understand the reasoning
Why does Razi say isolated reports are speculative? Focus on the infallibility argument and the inconsistency he points out.
He uses an analogy with the Rafidah's claim of 'Ali's infallibility.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
Razi cites four verses that condemn following assumption. Read each one and consider how they support his case.
The key verse is 'Indeed, assumption does not avail against the truth at all' (53:28).
5
Reflect on the usul/furu' distinction
Razi notes that speculation is acceptable in legal branches (furu') but not in fundamentals (usul). Why? Because legal rulings can be based on probability, but theology requires certainty.
This distinction is crucial for Islamic methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الحادي والثلاثون في كلام كلي في أخبار الآحاد نقول : أما التمسك بخبر الواحد في معرفة الله تعالى فغير جائز ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن أخبار الآحاد مظنونة . فلا يجوز التمسك بها في معرفة الله تعالى وصفاته . وإنما قلنا إنها مظنونة . وذلك لأنا أجمعنا على أن الرواة ليسوا معصومين وكيف؟ والروافض لما اتفقوا على عصمة علي وحده . هؤلاء المحدثون كفروهم . وإذا كان القول بعصمة علي - كرم الله وجهه - يوجب عليهم تكفير القائلين بعصمة علي فكيف يمكنهم القول بعصمة هؤلاء الرواة ؟ وإذا لم يكونوا معصومين ، كان الخطأ عليهم جائزاً ، والكذب عليهم جائزاً. وحينئذ لا يكون صدقهم معلوما ، بل مظنونا ، فثبت : أن خبر الواحد مظنون . فوجب أن لا يجوز التمسك به . لقوله تعالى : ( إن الظن لا يغني من الحق شيئا ) ولقوله تعالى في صفة الكفار : ( إن يتبعون إلا الظن ) ولقوله : ( ولا تقف ما ليس لك به علم ) ولقوله : ( وأن تقولوا على الله ما لا تعلمون ) ، ترك العمل في فروع الشريعة - لأن المطلوب فيها الظن – فوجب أن يبقى في مسائل الأصول على هذا الأصل .
Chapter Thirty-One: A General Discourse on Isolated Reports. We say: As for relying on an isolated report for knowledge of Allah Most High, it is not permissible. Evidence for this is found in several aspects. First: Isolated reports are speculative. So relying on them for knowledge of Allah Most High and His attributes is not permissible. We say they are speculative because we are all agreed that the narrators are not infallible. How? When the Rafidah agreed on the infallibility of 'Ali alone, these hadith scholars declared them disbelievers. If declaring 'Ali infallible—may Allah honor his face—obliges them to declare those who affirm 'Ali's infallibility as disbelievers, how can they affirm the infallibility of these narrators? And if they are not infallible, error and lying are possible from them. In that case, their truthfulness is not known but only speculated. So it is established that an isolated report is speculative, therefore it is not permissible to rely on it, due to His statement Exalted: 'Indeed, assumption does not avail against the truth at all' (53:28), and His statement describing the disbelievers: 'They follow nothing but assumption' (53:28), and His statement: 'Do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge' (17:36), and His statement: 'And that you say about Allah that which you do not know' (7:33). Acting on assumption is permissible in the branches of the law because the required in them is assumption; therefore it necessarily remains in the matters of fundamentals upon this principle.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against using isolated reports (akhbar al-ahad) in theology. Razi begins by stating the thesis: relying on an isolated report for knowledge of Allah is impermissible. He then gives the first argument: such reports are speculative (maznuna) because the narrators are not infallible (ma'sumin). He argues by pointing to the inconsistency of hadith scholars: the Rafidah (Shia) affirm the infallibility of 'Ali alone, yet the hadith scholars declare them disbelievers for that. If they consider belief in infallibility of one person as disbelief, how can they claim infallibility for many narrators? Since narrators are fallible, their truthfulness is not certain but only probable. Therefore isolated reports are speculative. Razi then cites four Quranic verses condemning reliance on assumption, demonstrating that speculation has no place in theology. He notes that speculation is acceptable in practical branches of law (furu'), but in matters of fundamentals (usul) it remains prohibited. The argument is structured as a syllogism: (1) isolated reports are speculative, (2) theological knowledge requires certainty, (3) therefore isolated reports cannot ground theological knowledge.

ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an isolated report is authentic (sahih), it can be used in theology.
Correction: Razi argues that even authentic isolated reports only yield probability because narrators are fallible. Theology requires certainty, not probability.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Razi gives for ruling that isolated reports cannot be used in theology?
A) They are too few in number.
B) They are speculative because narrators are not infallible.
C) They contradict the Quran.
D) They are only about legal matters.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof establishes that isolated reports are speculative due to the fallibility of narrators, and since theology demands certainty, such reports cannot be used for knowledge of Allah.

?
Think of a belief you hold about Allah that you assumed was correct but never actually verified. What would it cost you to abandon it if it turns out to be based only on speculation?

Companions' Reports Lack Certainty Due to Mutual Criticism
Razi enumerates instances of Companions criticizing each other to show lack of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses examples of mutual criticism among the Companions to argue that their reports do not yield certainty.

Core Idea
Even the Companions did not accept each other's reports uncritically.

Razi now tackles the most respected narrators—the Companions. He argues that even their reports are not certain because they themselves criticized each other. This challenges the assumption that Companions' reports are automatically reliable in theological matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let Razi's examples come through before you interpret.
The passage lists many examples of criticism; try to identify each one.
2
Identify the main claim and evidence
Razi claims that Companions' narrations do not yield certainty. His evidence is that hadith scholars recorded them criticizing each other. Note how this challenges the idea of inherent reliability.
3
Examine the examples of criticism
Go through each example: Umar vs Khalid, Ibn Mas'ud and Abu Dharr vs Uthman, Aisha vs Uthman, Umar on Talha and Zubayr, Ali questioning Abu Hurayra, Umar forbidding Abu Hurayra from narrating, Ibn Abbas criticizing specific hadiths. Understand what each shows about mutual criticism.
These examples are not just historical trivia; they serve as proof that Companions did not blindly accept each other's reports.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
If the Companions themselves were critical of each other's narrations, then their isolated reports cannot be considered certain. This undermines the validity of using such reports for establishing creedal matters.
This connects to the broader argument against relying on ahad reports in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن أجل طبقات الرواة قدراً ، وأعلاهم منصباً : الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم - ثم إنا نعلم ، أن رواياتهم لا تفيد القطع واليقين. والدليل عليه : أن هؤلاء المحدثين رووا عنهم : أن كل واحد منهم طعن في الآخر، ونسبه إلى ما لا ينبغي أليس من المشهور : أن عمر طعن في خالد بن الوليد ؟ وأن ابن مسعود وأبا ذر ، كانا يبالغان في الطعن في عثمان ؟ ونقل عن عائشة رضي الله عنها - أنها بالغت في الطعن في عثمان - وأليس أن عمر قال في عثمان : إنه يحلف بأقاربه ؟ وقال في طلحة والزبير : أشياء أخر ، تجري هذا المجرى . أليس أن علياً - كرم الله وجهه - سمع أن أبا هريرة يوما كان يقول : أخبرني خليلي أبو القاسم . فقال له علي : متى كان خليلك ؟ أليس أن عمر - رضي الله عنه - نهى أبا هريرة عن كثرة الرواية ؟ أليس أن ابن عباس - رضي الله عنهما - طعن في خبر أبي سعيد في الطرق ، وطعن في خبر أبي هريرة في غسل اليدين . وقال كيف يصنع بمهر أمنا ؟
Second: The highest rank of the narrators in dignity and the most elevated in status are the Companions—may Allah be pleased with them. Yet we know that their narrations do not yield certainty and conviction. The proof of this is that these hadith scholars have narrated from them that each of them criticized the other and attributed to him what is not fitting. Is it not well-known that 'Umar criticized Khalid ibn al-Walid? And that Ibn Mas'ud and Abu Dharr were excessive in criticizing 'Uthman? And it is narrated from 'A'isha—may Allah be pleased with her—that she was excessive in criticizing 'Uthman. And did not 'Umar say about 'Uthman that he swears by his relatives? And he said about Talha and al-Zubayr other things of this nature. Did not 'Ali—may Allah honor his face—hear that Abu Hurayra one day was saying, 'My beloved Abu al-Qasim told me,' so 'Ali said to him, 'When was he your beloved?' Did not 'Umar—may Allah be pleased with him—forbid Abu Hurayra from excessive narration? Did not Ibn 'Abbas—may Allah be pleased with them both—criticize the hadith of Abu Sa'id concerning the road, and criticize the hadith of Abu Hurayra concerning washing the hands, and say, 'How would he do with the dowry of our mother?'
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against relying on isolated reports in theology. Razi argues that even the most highly esteemed narrators—the Companions of the Prophet—do not provide certainty through their narrations. He supports this with examples of mutual criticism among the Companions as recorded by the hadith scholars themselves: 'Umar criticized Khalid ibn al-Walid; Ibn Mas'ud and Abu Dharr were harsh in criticizing 'Uthman; 'A'isha also criticized 'Uthman; 'Umar commented on 'Uthman's favoritism and criticized Talha and al-Zubayr; 'Ali questioned Abu Hurayra's claim that the Prophet called him his beloved; 'Umar stopped Abu Hurayra from narrating excessively; and Ibn 'Abbas criticized specific hadiths of Abu Sa'id and Abu Hurayra. These examples demonstrate that the Companions themselves did not accept each other's reports uncritically, showing that their narrations are not certain. Therefore, using isolated reports from them for theological knowledge is invalid.

Why Weak Hadith Cannot Ground Theology
Razi lists early hadith criticism examples and argues they cannot ground theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that weak or isolated hadith reports cannot be used to establish doctrines about Allah's essence and attributes.

Core Idea
Theology requires certainty, not mere presumption from isolated hadith.

Razi begins by listing several historical instances where Companions criticized hadith reports. These examples show that early Muslims were rigorous in evaluating narrations. However, this does not mean all hadith are rejected; rather, Razi draws a crucial distinction between legal rulings and theology. The passage then sets a foundational methodological principle for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical examples
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the six rhetorical questions that list cases of hadith criticism. Notice the specific reports and who criticized them.
These examples show that criticism was not rare but common among the Sahaba.
2
Recognize the pattern
Observe that the criticism is directed at individual reports, not the entire corpus. The Companions used Quranic principles or demanded corroboration.
3
Understand the distinction
Razi then explains that despite this criticism, the general Quranic praise of the Companions gives their narrations a presumption of truthfulness, which is sufficient for legal matters.
This is a key shift: legal rulings can be based on probable evidence, but theology requires certainty.
4
Apply the conclusion
Finally, Razi asks how theology can be built on such weak reports, implying that only mutawatir or widely accepted reports are suitable for theology.
Reflect on the implications for how we approach hadith in 'aqidah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أليس أن أبا هريرة لما روى : «من أصبح جنيا فلا صوم له» طعنوا فيه؟ أليس أن ابن عمر لما روى : أن الميت ليعذب ببكاء أهله عليه ، طعنت عائشة فيه ، بقوله تعالى : ( ولا تزر وازرة وزر أخرى ) اليس أنهم طعنوا في خبر فاطمة بنت قيس ، وقالوا : لا ندع كتاب ربنا ، وسنة نبينا ، الخبر امرأة ، لاندرى أصدقت أم كذبت ؟ أليس أن عمر طالب أبا موسى الأشعرى في خبر الاستئذان بشاهد وغلظ الأمر عليه ؟ أليس أن علياً كان يستحلف الرواة ؟ أليس أن عليا قال لعمر (رضى الله عنهما) في بعض الوقائع : إن قاربوك فقد غشوك ؟ و اعلم : أنك إذا طالعت كتب الحديث ، وجدت من هذا الباب ما لا يعد ولا يحصى . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : الطاعن . إن صدق ، فقد توجه الطعن على المطعون، وإن كذب فقد توجه على الطاعن . فكيف كان فتوجه الطعن لازم إلا أنا قلنا : إن الله تعالى أثنى على الصحابة ـ رضى الله عنهم - في القرآن على سبيل العموم . وذلك يفيد ظن الصدق . ولهذا الترجيح قبلنا رواياتهم في فروع الشريعة. أما الكلام في ذات الله وصفاته ، فكيف يمكن بناؤه على هذه الروايات الضعيفة ؟
Is it not that when Abu Hurairah narrated: 'Whoever becomes junub in the morning has no fast' they criticized him? Is it not that when Ibn Umar narrated that the dead is punished by the weeping of his family, 'A'ishah criticized him by His saying: 'And no bearer of burdens will bear the burden of another' (35:18)? Is it not that they criticized the report of Fatimah bint Qais and said: 'We will not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet; the report is from a woman, we do not know whether she told the truth or lied'? Is it not that 'Umar demanded from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari a witness for the report of seeking permission and made the matter serious for him? Is it not that 'Ali used to make the narrators take an oath? Is it not that 'Ali said to 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both) in some incidents: 'If they are near you they have deceived you'? And know: If you peruse the books of hadith, you will find from this chapter what cannot be counted or enumerated. And when this is established, we say: The critic, if he is truthful, then criticism falls upon the criticized, and if he is lying, then criticism falls upon the critic. So how was it? The directing of criticism is necessary, except that we said: Allah Almighty praised the Companions — may Allah be pleased with them — in the Qur'an in a general manner. And that yields a presumption of truthfulness. And due to this preponderance, we accept their narrations in the branches of the Law. As for speech concerning the Essence of Allah and His attributes, how can it be built upon these weak reports?
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a historical and methodological argument. Razi first provides several examples from early Islamic history showing that the Companions themselves were rigorous in criticizing hadith reports. For instance, Abu Huraira's report on fasting when junub was rejected; 'A'ishah rejected Ibn Umar's report about the dead being punished by weeping, citing the Qur'an; the report of Fatimah bint Qais was dismissed because it came from a woman; 'Umar challenged Abu Musa's report on seeking permission; 'Ali would make narrators swear and once warned 'Umar. Razi then concludes that such criticism is common and unavoidable. He then makes a key distinction: because the Qur'an generally praises the Companions, their narrations carry a presumption of truthfulness in legal matters (furu' al-shari'ah). However, when it comes to theology—God's essence and attributes—such weak reports cannot serve as a foundation. This establishes a methodological principle: hadith that are not mutawatir or widely accepted cannot be used to determine divine attributes.

Key Terms
الطعن
الروايات الضعيفة
الظن
فروع الشريعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi rejects all hadith in theology.
Correction: Razi only rejects weak or isolated reports; mutawatir or widely accepted hadith are not excluded. His argument is about the insufficiency of zann (presumption) in theological matters.

Checkpoint
What reason does Razi give for accepting the narrations of the Sahaba in legal matters despite the existence of criticism?
A) Because the Qur'an praises them generally, yielding a presumption of truthfulness.
B) Because they were all infallible.
C) Because the criticisms were all false.
D) Because legal matters are trivial.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that while hadith criticism exists, the general praise of the Companions allows their reports to be used for legal rulings, but theology requires stronger evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about Allah or His attributes simply because you heard it from a preacher or read it in a book, without verifying its evidence? What would it take for you to require that level of certainty for such a belief?
?
Razi shows that even the Companions—the best generation—criticized each other's reports. Does your own approach to learning about Allah reflect this same rigor, or do you passively accept whatever you hear? Will you commit to examining the strength of every proof you rely on for your faith?
?
If you discovered that a cherished belief about Allah's attributes was based on a weak hadith, would you be willing to abandon it? Are you more attached to the comfort of that belief or to the truth that Allah deserves to be known only through certain evidence?

Heretics Fabricated Hadith and the Limits of Scholars
Razi argues heretics fabricated hadith and scholars' limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that heretics fabricated objectionable hadith, why even Bukhari and Muslim were not infallible in detecting them, and how to evaluate such reports.

Core Idea
Heretics can infiltrate hadith, so critical judgment is needed even on sound collections.

In this passage, Razi presents his third argument against relying on anthropomorphic hadith. He points to a historical reality: heretics fabricated objectionable reports and tricked hadith scholars into accepting them. Even the greatest imams like Bukhari and Muslim were not omniscient — they did their best but could not know all the unseen. Therefore, when we encounter a hadith that describes God in a way that contradicts His transcendence, we must judge it as fabricated, regardless of its chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let Razi's own argument unfold before interpreting.
The passage is part of a larger discourse; note the 'third argument' opening.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes two key points: (1) heretics fabricated hadith and scholars unknowingly accepted them, (2) even Bukhari and Muslim were not omniscient. Identify where each claim appears.
The first claim uses 'اشتهر' (well-known), the second uses 'أما البخاري'.
3
Understand the defense of Bukhari and Muslim
Razi defends Bukhari and Muslim by affirming their effort and precaution, but denies they had knowledge of the unseen. Consider why this defense is important for his overall argument.
He does not accuse them of negligence, but acknowledges human limitation.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on how Razi's rule works: when we see a hadith containing something objectionable that cannot be from the Prophet, we cut off that it is fabricated. Think of an example.
This connects to the earlier lesson on anthropomorphic hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : وهو أنه اشتهر فيما بين الأمة : أن جماعة من الملاحدة ، وضعوا أخباراً منكرة، واحتالوا في ترويجها على المحدثين . والمحدثون لسلامة قلوبهم ما عرفوها ، بل قبلوها . وأي مشكر فوق وصف الله تعالى بما يقدح في الإلهية ، ويبطل الربوبية ؟ فوجب القطع في أمثال هذه الأخبار بأنها موضوعة . وأما البخاري والقشيرى فهما ما كانا عالمين بالغيوب ، بل اجتهدا واحتاطا بمقدار طاقتهما ، وأما اعتقاد أنهما علما جميع الأحوال الواقعة في زمان الرسول إلى زماننا ، فذلك لا يقوله عاقل . وغاية ما في الباب : أنا نحسن الظن بهما ، وبالذين رويا عنهم إلا أنا إذا شاهدنا خبرا مشتملا على منكر ، لا يمكن إسناده إلى الرسول قطعا بأنه من أوضاع الملاحدة ، ومن ترويجاتهم على أولئك المحدثين .
Third argument: And it is that it is well-known among the ummah that a group of heretics fabricated objectionable reports and devised strategies to promote them among the hadith scholars. And the hadith scholars, due to the soundness of their hearts, did not recognize them, but rather accepted them. And what disbelief is greater than describing Allah Almighty with what impugns divinity and invalidates lordship? So it is necessary to judge definitively about such reports that they are fabricated. As for al-Bukhari and al-Qushayri (i.e., Muslim), they were not knowers of the unseen, but rather they exerted effort and took precautions to the extent of their capacity. As for believing that they knew all the circumstances that occurred in the time of the Messenger until our time, no intelligent person would say that. The utmost in the matter is that we think well of them and of those from whom they narrated. However, when we see a report containing something objectionable that cannot possibly be attributed to the Messenger, we cut off that it is from the fabrications of the heretics and their promotions upon those hadith scholars.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This is the third argument presented by Razi. He states that it is a known fact that heretics fabricated hadith and successfully promoted them to unsuspecting hadith scholars, who accepted them due to their good-heartedness. The most severe of such fabrications are those that describe God in ways that impugn His divinity and lordship. Therefore, such reports must be definitively judged as fabricated. Razi then defends the great hadith imams like Bukhari and Muslim by noting that they were not omniscient; they did their best with the tools available. To claim that they knew all the events from the Prophet's time to their own is absurd. The proper attitude is to have good opinion of them, but when a report contains something objectionable that cannot be from the Prophet, we must rule it as a fabrication by heretics that misled the scholars.

وضع الأخبار
المحدثون
الغيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi is accusing Bukhari and Muslim of incompetence or negligence in hadith verification.
Correction: Razi affirms their effort and precaution but acknowledges human limitation. He does not blame them, but rather uses their example to show that even the best scholars are not infallible.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why must reports that describe God in a way that impugns divinity be cut off as fabricated?
A) Because all hadith scholars were heretics.
B) Because they cannot be attributed to the Messenger.
C) Because Bukhari and Muslim said so.
D) Because the Sahaba rejected them.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that even the most reliable hadith collections may contain fabrications by heretics, so any hadith that contradicts God's transcendence must be rejected as fabricated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Razi says the scholars accepted fabricated reports due to ‘soundness of their hearts’. Could it be that your own good-heartedness has led you to believe falsehoods about Allah because you trusted a teacher or a book without question? What is one belief you hold about Allah that you have never critically examined? Write it down and test it against the Quranic principles of tanzih.
?
Razi says we must ‘cut off’ that a report is fabricated when it contains something objectionable about Allah. Are you willing to ‘cut off’ a beloved hadith or a respected scholar’s opinion if it contradicts the clear transcendence of Allah? What is one such report you find difficult to let go? Pray for strength to prioritize Allah’s majesty over emotional attachment.

The Double Standard in Hadith Criticism on Theological Distortion
Razi enumerates examples of hadith critics' double standard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi exposes the inconsistency of hadith critics who reject narrators for minor sectarian leanings but overlook major theological distortions.

Core Idea
Hadith critics apply stricter standards to minor biases than to major theological errors.

Imam al-Razi continues his critique by pointing out a glaring inconsistency in hadith criticism. He observes that scholars readily reject narrations due to minor tendencies like love for Ali (rafd) or belief in free will (qadr), yet they fail to apply the same strictness to those who describe God in ways that undermine His divinity. This passage highlights a troubling double standard in the methodology of hadith authentication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the examples Razi gives of minor leanings rejected by critics.
The examples are Rafidi (love of Ali) and Qadari (belief in free will).
2
Identify the double standard
Compare the two standards: critics reject narrators for minor leanings, but they do not reject those who describe God falsely. Ask yourself why Razi finds this astonishing.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what Razi is trying to show about hadith criticism methodology. Why is it problematic to ignore theological distortion while rejecting minor biases?
This reveals inconsistency and possibly hidden biases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن هؤلاء المحدثين يخرجون الروايات بأقل الملل . مثل : إنه كان مائلا إلى حب «على»، فكان رافضيا ، فلا تقبل روايته . ومثل : كان معبد الجهن، قائلا بالقدر ، فلا تقبل روايته . وما كار فيهم عاقل يقول : إنه وصف الله تعالى بما يبطل الهيته وربوبيته ، فلا تقبل روايته . إن هذا من العجائب
Fourth: These hadith scholars invalidate narrations for the least leaning. For example: 'He was inclined to love 'Ali, so he was a Rafidi, therefore his narration is not accepted.' And like: 'He was Ma'bad al-Juhani, a proponent of Qadr, therefore his narration is not accepted.' And none among them is a sensible person saying: 'He described Allah Almighty with what invalidates His divinity and lordship, therefore his narration is not accepted.' This is among the wonders.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This is the fourth argument. Razi points out a glaring inconsistency in the practice of hadith critics. They reject narrations for the slightest leaning—such as being a Rafidi (due to love for 'Ali) or a Qadari (like Ma'bad al-Juhani). Yet, they do not apply the same standard to those who describe God in ways that invalidate His divinity and lordship. No sensible critic among them says: 'This narrator described God falsely, so his narration is rejected.' Razi finds this astonishing, as it shows a double standard where minor human biases are considered ground for rejection, but major theological errors are overlooked.

Key Terms
الرافضي
القدري
الطعن
الإلهية
الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is defending Rafidis or Qadaris.
Correction: Razi is not defending these groups; he is highlighting the inconsistency in hadith criticism methodology.

Checkpoint
What double standard does Razi highlight in this passage?
A) Critics accept weak narrations for theology but not for law.
B) Critics reject narrators for minor leanings but not for describing God falsely.
C) Critics accept all Sahaba narrations without question.
D) Critics only reject narrations from heretics.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that hadith critics are inconsistent: they reject narrations for minor sectarian leanings but not for theological distortions that deny Allah's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate someone's faith, do you focus on their minor differences (like a different opinion about a companion) while ignoring their misconceptions about Allah's nature? How will you change your priorities today?

Are you more concerned with outward labels (like Sunni or Shia) than with whether someone's belief about Allah is correct? What would it take for you to confront a major distortion even if it comes from someone you consider part of your group?
?
Razi found it astonishing that scholars reject narrations for small sectarian leanings but stay silent on descriptions that invalidate Allah's divinity. Do you have a similar blind spot in your own life – condemning small sins while tolerating greater ones? Ask Allah to open your eyes and commit to correcting that inconsistency starting now.

Oral Transmission and Memory Error in Hadith Wording
Razi argues that narrators transmitted meaning not exact words after many years.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the wording of individual hadith reports cannot be definitively attributed to the Prophet due to the reliance on memory after long intervals.

Core Idea
Memory after decades cannot preserve exact wording.

Razi continues his critique of isolated hadith by focusing on the practical reality of oral transmission. The Prophet's Companions and later narrators did not write down reports verbatim; they relied on memory after many years. This simple fact, Razi argues, makes it impossible to claim that the words of a hadith are the Prophet's exact words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the flow of Razi's argument.
2
Identify the premises
Note that Razi states two key facts: narrators did not write the words from the Prophet's mouth, and they narrated after 20-30 years after hearing only once.
3
Understand 'known necessarily'
Razi claims it is a necessary truth that such narrators cannot reproduce exact words. Ask yourself why he considers this obvious.
4
Trace the conclusion
Follow the logic: since exact wording is impossible, the words we have are from the narrator, not the Prophet. This undermines certainty about the wording.
5
Consider the implications
Reflect on how this argument applies to ahad hadith but not to mutawatir reports, which Razi does not discuss here.
This helps avoid the misunderstanding that Razi rejects all hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن الرواة الذين سمعوا هذه الأخبار من الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ما كتبوها عن لفظ الرسول ، بل سمعوا شيئا في مجلس ، ثم أنهم رووا تلك الأشياء بعد عشرين سنة أو أكثر. ومن سمع شيئا في مجلس مرة واحدة ، ثم رواه بعد العشرين والثلاثين ، لا يمكنه رواية تلك الألفاظ بأعيانها . وهذا كالمعلوم بالضرورة فإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان القطع حاصلا بأن شيئا من هذه الألفاظ : ليس من ألفاظ الرسول بل ليس ذلك إلا من ألفاظ الراوي وكيف يقطع بأن هذا الراوى ما
Fifth: The narrators who heard these reports from the Messenger, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, did not write them from the words of the Messenger, but rather they heard something in a gathering, and then they narrated those things after twenty years or more. And whoever hears something in a gathering once, and then narrates it after twenty or thirty years, cannot narrate those exact words. This is known necessarily. And if the matter is so, then the definitive judgment is attained that none of these words are from the words of the Messenger, but rather that is only from the words of the narrator. And how can one cut off that this narrator [what?]
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This is the fifth argument. Razi focuses on the oral nature of hadith transmission. The narrators who heard hadith did not write them down verbatim from the Prophet's lips. Instead, they heard something in a gathering and later narrated it after twenty or thirty years. It is a matter of necessity that such a narrator cannot reproduce the exact wording after such a long interval. Therefore, we can definitively conclude that the wording of these reports is not the Prophet's wording but the narrator's. The final sentence seems cut off, but the argument is complete: since the wording is the narrator's, we cannot be certain that the words reported are the Prophet's. This further weakens the reliability of isolated hadith for theological claims.

Key Terms
الرواة
الألفاظ
الحفظ
الضرورة
القطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi denies all hadith and considers every report as fabricated.
Correction: Razi only argues that the exact wording of non-mutawatir reports cannot be definitively attributed to the Prophet due to memory issues; he does not reject the core meaning or the use of ahad hadith outside of creedal certainty.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue about the narrators' transmission of hadith?
A) They wrote down everything immediately.
B) They transmitted exact words after many years.
C) They relied on memory after decades, so exact wording is doubtful.
D) They were divinely protected from error.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that due to the long interval between hearing and narrating, the exact wording of ahad hadith cannot be definitively attributed to the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that after twenty years, exact words cannot be preserved. Think of the most important religious advice you have received. How sure are you that you remember it exactly? How does this affect your reliance on hadith that were transmitted orally without writing?
?
You have likely memorized some Quranic verses. Contrast that with the hadith transmission Razi describes. What does the Quran's preservation demand from you in terms of transmitting Islamic knowledge accurately to others?
?
Razi says we cannot be certain that the narrator's words are the Prophet's. When you share something you learned about Islam, do you check the exact wording? How often do you paraphrase? How might Razi's critique expose a weakness in your own practice of conveying the deen?

Rejecting Ahad Hadith in Theology: Two Key Arguments
Imam Razi presents the fourth and fifth reasons against using ahad hadith in theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two of Imam al-Razi's arguments against using solitary reports in theology: the double standard in rejecting narrators and the inevitable alteration of wording through oral transmission.

Core Idea
Ahad reports cannot yield certainty in theology due to unreliable transmission and textual alteration.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a robust critique of using solitary hadith in theological matters. In this passage, he continues his argument by highlighting two critical flaws: the double standard in rejecting narrators and the impossibility of preserving exact wording over decades of oral transmission. This lesson unpacks these arguments to show why ahad reports are not valid evidence in usul al-din.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
The passage contains two enumerated reasons (fourth and fifth) along with preceding context.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Locate the fourth and fifth reasons. For each, understand the specific problem al-Razi is pointing out.
The fourth reason deals with inconsistency in rejecting narrators; the fifth deals with textual alteration over time.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these arguments challenge the reliability of ahad reports in theology. Why does this matter for knowing God's essence and attributes?
Al-Razi is not rejecting all hadith, only their use in matters requiring certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السلامة قلوبهم ما عرفوها، بل قبلوها. وأي مشرك فوق وصف الله تعالى بما يقدح في الإلهية، ويبطل الربوبية؟ فوجب القطع في أمثال هذه الأخبار بأنها موضوعة. وأما البخاري والقشيري فهما ما كانا عالمين بالغيوب، بل اجتهدا واحتاطا بمقدار طاقتهما، وأما اعتقاد أنهما علما جميع الأحوال الواقعة في زمان الرسول إلى زماننا، فذلك لا يقوله عاقل. وغاية ما في الباب: أنا نحسن الظن بهما، وبالذين رويا عنهم إلا أنا إذا شاهدنا خبرا مشتملا على منكر، لا يمكن إسناده إلى الرسول قطعا بأنه من أوضاع الملاحدة، ومن ترويجاتهم على أولئك المحدثين. الرابع: إن هؤلاء المحدثين يخرجون الروايات بأقل الملل. مثل: إنه كان مائلا إلى حب «علي»، فكان رافضيا، فلا تقبل روايته. ومثل: كان معبد الجهن، قائلا بالقدر، فلا تقبل روايته. وما كاد فيهم عاقل يقول: إنه وصف الله تعالى بما يبطل إلهيته وربوبيته، فلا تقبل روايته. إن هذا من العجائب. الخامس: إن الرواة الذين سمعوا هذه الأخبار من الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ما كتبوها عن لفظ الرسول، بل سمعوا شيئا في مجلس، ثم أنهم رووا تلك الأشياء بعد عشرين سنة أو أكثر. ومن سمع شيئا في مجلس مرة واحدة، ثم رواه بعد العشرين والثلاثين، لا يمكنه رواية تلك الألفاظ بأعيانها. وهذا كالمعلوم بالضرورة. أو إذا كان الأمر كذلك، كان القطع حاصلا بأن شيئا من هذه الألفاظ ليس من ألفاظ الرسول بل ليس ذلك إلا من ألفاظ الراوي. وكيف يقطع بأن هذا الراوي ما سمع ما جرى في ذلك المجلس؟ فإن من سمع كلاما في مجلس واحد، ثم إنه ما كتبه، وما كان يرد عليه كل يوم، بل ذكره بعد عشرين سنة أو ثلاثين. فالظاهر: أنه ينسى منه شيئا كثيرا، أو يتشوش عليه نظم الكلام وترتيبه وتركيبه. ومع هذا الاحتمال فكيف يمكن التمسك به في معرفة ذات الله تعالى وصفاته؟ واعلم أن هذا الباب كثير الكلام. إلا أن القدر الذي أوردناه كاف في بيان أنه لا يجوز التمسك في أصول الدين بأخبار الآحاد. والله أعلم.
Their hearts did not know soundness, but they accepted it. And what polytheist is beyond describing God Almighty with what impugns divinity and nullifies lordship? So it is necessary to definitively judge such reports as fabricated. As for al-Bukhari and al-Qushayri, they were not knowers of the unseen; rather, they exerted effort and were cautious according to their capacity. As for the belief that they knew all the conditions that occurred in the time of the Messenger until our time, no rational person would say that. The most that can be said is that we think well of them and of those from whom they narrated, except that when we see a report containing an objectionable matter, it cannot be definitively attributed to the Messenger, but rather is from the fabrications of the heretics and their deceptions upon those hadith scholars. Fourth: These hadith scholars reject narrations on the slightest sectarian grounds. For example, if a narrator inclined to love 'Ali, he was a Rafidite, so his narration is not accepted. Another example: if he was Ma'bad al-Juhani, a proponent of Qadariyya, his narration is not accepted. Hardly any rational person among them says: if he described God Almighty with what nullifies His divinity and lordship, then his narration is not accepted. This is truly astonishing. Fifth: The narrators who heard these reports from the Messenger (peace be upon him) did not write them down from the utterance of the Messenger; rather, they heard something in a gathering, then they narrated those things after twenty years or more. And one who heard something in a single gathering and then narrated it after twenty or thirty years cannot narrate those exact words. This is known necessarily. If this is the case, then it is definitively established that none of these words are the words of the Messenger—they are only the words of the narrator. How can one be certain that this narrator heard what took place in that gathering? For if someone heard speech in a single gathering, then did not write it down, and did not repeat it daily, but mentioned it after twenty or thirty years, it is apparent that he forgot much of it, or the order, arrangement, and composition of the speech became confused for him. With this possibility, how can one rely on it to know the essence of God Almighty and His attributes? Know that this topic is extensive, but the amount we have presented is sufficient to show that it is not permissible to rely on solitary reports in the principles of religion. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument against using solitary reports (أخبار الآحاد) in matters of theology, specifically regarding God's essence and attributes. The author, Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, presents multiple points to demonstrate the unreliability of such reports. He begins by criticizing those who accept problematic descriptions of God, likening them to polytheists. He then asserts that reports containing anthropomorphic or otherwise objectionable content must be definitively judged as fabricated. Addressing the authority of hadith scholars like al-Bukhari and al-Qushayri, al-Razi clarifies that they were not infallible knowers of the unseen; they did their best, but we cannot attribute perfect knowledge of all historical events to them. Instead, when we find a report contrary to reason and divine transcendence, we can definitively say it was fabricated by heretics. The fourth point criticizes hadith critics for rejecting narrators on minor sectarian grounds (e.g., love of 'Ali or Qadariyya) while failing to reject them when they attribute degrading descriptions to God—a glaring inconsistency. The fifth and most detailed point argues that the oral transmission process inevitably alters wording. Since narrators often heard a report once and later narrated it decades later without writing it down, it is impossible to preserve exact wording. This is self-evident. Thus, such reports cannot provide certainty in matters of God's essence and attributes. The passage concludes by stating that although the topic is vast, the presented arguments suffice to show that relying on ahad reports in usul al-din is impermissible.

Key Terms
أخبار الآحاد
موضوعة
البخاري
القشيري
الملاحدة
الرواة
ألفاظ الرسول
أصول الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam al-Razi rejects all hadith, including those used in practical law.
Correction: Al-Razi only rejects solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) in theological matters (usul al-din), not in practical jurisprudence (furu') where they are accepted.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi's fifth argument, why cannot solitary reports be used to know God's essence and attributes?
A) Because the narrators were unreliable.
B) Because oral transmission over many years inevitably alters the exact wording.
C) Because the Prophet forbade writing hadith.
D) Because these reports were all fabricated by heretics.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi argues that solitary hadith reports are unreliable for theology due to inconsistent rejection criteria and inevitable textual alteration from oral transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith about Allah that seems surprising or problematic, do you immediately assume it is authentic because it is in a famous collection? Or do you pause to ask: ‘Can I be certain this is the Prophet’s exact word, or might it have been altered in transmission?’ How does this lesson challenge your default trust in all hadith?
?
You likely accept many theological beliefs about Allah based on what you’ve learned from scholars and texts. But are you certain that every belief you hold is based on definitive, undeniable proof? What would you do if you discovered that one of your cherished beliefs rests on a solitary report that could have been misremembered?
?
Al-Razi warns that even well-intentioned narrators can change the wording of a report over decades. Think of a time you relayed a story years after hearing it. How much detail did you get wrong? If you cannot trust your own memory for a simple story, how can you trust the exact words of a hadith narrated after thirty years? What does this demand from you regarding the sources of your faith?
