Four Cases When Reason and Revelation Conflict
Razi classifies four possibilities and argues only one is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical possibilities when definitive rational proofs conflict with apparent meanings of transmitted texts and why only one approach is rationally acceptable.

Core Idea
When reason and revelation conflict, reason must guide interpretation of revelation.

Imam Razi presents a crisp logical classification of possible responses when rational proofs appear to contradict transmitted texts. He systematically eliminates three options as impossible or self-defeating, leaving only one valid path: affirm reason and interpret transmission accordingly. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the four options Razi lists and how he rejects each.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the conclusion is clear from context.
2
Identify the four possibilities
List the four possible responses Razi considers. Understand why affirming both (1) and negating both (2) are logically impossible.
Affirming both entails believing two contradictories; negating both denies two contradictories.
3
Analyze the third and fourth options
Option 3 (deny transmission, affirm reason) and Option 4 (deny reason, affirm transmission). Razi argues Option 4 is invalid because transmission's validity depends on reason.
This is the key move: reason is needed to establish the principles that validate transmission.
4
Understand why reason must lead
Razi shows that attacking reason to uphold transmission destroys both. Therefore, the only remaining valid option is to follow reason and interpret transmitted texts accordingly.
This establishes the methodological principle of reason's primacy in case of conflict.
5
Reflect on the broader methodology
Consider how this principle applies to theological disputes. It is not a rejection of transmission but a hermeneutical rule for resolving apparent contradictions.
Razi's approach preserves both reason and revelation by giving reason interpretive authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني والثلاثون في أن البراهين العقلية إذا صارت معارضة بالظواهر النقلية فكيف يكون الحال فيها ؟ اعلم : أن الدلائل القطعية العقلية إذا قامت على ثبوت شيء، ثم وجدنا أدلة نقلية يشعر ظاهرها بخلاف ذلك. فهناك لا يخلو الحال من أحد أمور أربعة : إما أن نصدق مقتضى العقل والنقل - فيلزم تصديق النقيضين وهو محال - وإما أن نبطلهما - فيلزم تكذيب النقيضين . وهو محال ( وإما أن تكذب الظواهر النقلية، وتصدق الظواهر العقلية ) وإما أن تصدق الظواهر النقلية وتكذب الظواهر العقلية وذلك باطل - لأنه لا يمكننا أن نعرف صحة الظواهر النقلية، إلا إذا عرفنا بالدلائل العقلية : إثبات الصانع، وصفاته، وكيفية دلالة المعجرة على صدق الرسول وظهور المعجزات على ( يد ) محمد ولو صار القدح فى الدلائل العقلية القطعية، صار العقل متهما، غير مقبول القول ولو كان كذلك الخرج عن أن يكون مقبول القول في هذه الأصول وإذا لم تثبت هذه الأصول، خرجت الدلائل النقلية عن كونها مفيدة فثبت : أن القدح في العقل لتصحيح النقل، يفضي إلى القدح في العقل والنقل معا . وإنه باطل . ولما بطلت الأقسام الأربعة لم يبق إلا أن ( يقطع بمقتضى ) الدلائل العقلية القاطعة : بأن هذه الدلائل
Chapter Thirty-Two: On the case when rational proofs are opposed by transmitted apparent meanings, what is the situation regarding them? Know: that definitive rational proofs when they are established upon the affirmation of something, then we find transmitted proofs whose apparent meaning indicates contrary to that. Then the situation is not devoid of one of four matters: Either we affirm the import of reason and transmission—then affirming two contradictories follows, which is impossible; or we negate them both—then denying two contradictories follows, which is impossible; (or we deny the transmitted apparent meanings and affirm the rational apparent meanings); or we affirm the transmitted apparent meanings and deny the rational apparent meanings—and that is invalid—because we cannot know the correctness of the transmitted apparent meanings except if we know by rational proofs: the affirmation of the Maker, His attributes, and the manner of the miracle's indication of the truthfulness of the Messenger, and the appearance of miracles at the hand of Muhammad. And if disparagement occurs in definitive rational proofs, then reason becomes accused, not accepted in speech. And if it were like that, it would fall out from being accepted in these principles. And if these principles are not established, transmitted proofs exit from being beneficial. So it is established: that disparaging reason to correct transmission leads to disparaging both reason and transmission together. And it is invalid. And when the four divisions became invalid, nothing remains except to (decide by the import of) the definitive rational proofs: that these proofs...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of possible responses when definitive rational proofs conflict with apparent meanings of transmitted texts. The author enumerates four logical possibilities: (1) affirming both, which is impossible because it entails affirming two contradictories; (2) negating both, which also entails denying two contradictories, equally impossible; (3) denying the transmitted texts and affirming the rational proofs; (4) affirming the transmitted texts and denying the rational proofs. The fourth option is deemed invalid because the very validity of transmitted texts depends on rational proofs establishing the existence of God, His attributes, and the authenticity of prophethood and miracles (specifically those of Muhammad). If reason is undermined, then these foundational principles collapse, and the transmitted texts lose their evidential value. Thus, attacking reason to uphold transmission actually destroys both. Therefore, after eliminating the three invalid options, the only remaining course is to follow the definitive rational proofs. The text is cut off, but the conclusion is clear: the rational proofs take precedence and the transmitted texts must be interpreted accordingly.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author denies all transmitted texts and relies solely on reason.
Correction: Razi does not deny transmitted texts; he affirms them but insists on interpretation when they conflict with definitive reason, because reason is the foundation for validating transmission.

Checkpoint
Which of the four possibilities does the author consider the only valid approach when reason and transmitted texts conflict?
A) Affirming both reason and transmitted texts.
B) Negating both.
C) Denying the transmitted texts and affirming reason.
D) Affirming transmitted texts and denying reason.

Key Takeaway
When definitive reason and transmitted texts conflict, the only rationally valid approach is to follow reason and interpret the transmitted texts accordingly.

This lesson shows that only one path is logically valid—will you have the courage to follow it, even when it challenges what you previously believed?
?
Razi says disparaging reason to correct transmission destroys both. In your own life, have you ever rejected a rational argument because it threatened a cherished religious interpretation? ?

This lesson gives you a clear method—but it demands intellectual honesty. Resolving Reason and Revelation Conflicts
Imam Razi enumerates four possibilities when reason and revelation conflict, refutes three, and establishes the rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's method for resolving apparent conflicts between definitive rational proofs and the apparent meanings of revealed texts.

Core Idea
When reason and revelation conflict, reason takes priority and revelation is interpreted accordingly.

Imam al-Razi tackles a foundational methodological question: what should a Muslim scholar do when a definitive rational proof seems to contradict the apparent meaning of a revealed text? He systematically enumerates all logical possibilities, eliminates the impossible ones, and establishes the only viable path. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Note the author's formal style and logical structure.
Pay attention to the four possibilities he lists; they are the backbone of the argument.
2
Identify the four possibilities
Imam al-Razi outlines four possible responses when reason and revelation conflict. List them in your own words.
The possibilities are: (1) accept both, (2) reject both, (3) accept reason and reject revelation, (4) accept revelation and reject reason.
3
Understand why three are invalid
He quickly dismisses the first two as logically impossible (affirming or denying two contradictories). Then he gives a longer refutation of the fourth option (rejecting reason to uphold revelation).
The fourth option is invalid because the validity of revelation itself depends on rational proofs.
4
Grasp the remaining option and its sub-options
The only valid possibility is to uphold the rational proof and then deal with the textual evidence: either deem it inauthentic, interpret it (ta'wil), or consign its meaning to Allah (tafwid).
This becomes the universal principle for all ambiguous texts (mutashabihat).
5
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this principle resolves potential conflicts and preserves both reason and revelation without contradiction.
It avoids rejecting revelation outright while maintaining the primacy of certain reason in establishing foundational beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني والثلاثون
في
أن البراهين العقلية إذا صارت معارضة بالظواهر النقلية فكيف يكون الحال فيها ؟
اعلم : أن الدلائل القطعية العقلية إذا قامت على ثبوت شيء ، ثم وجدنا أدلة نقلية يشعر ظاهرها بخلاف ذلك. فهناك لا يخلو الحال من أحد أمور أربعة : إما أن يصدق مقتضى العقل والنقل - فيلزم تصديق النقيضين وهو محال - وإما أن نبطلهما (۱) - فيلزم تكذيب النقيضين . وهو محال ( وإما أن تكذب الظواهر النقلية ، وتصدق الظواهر العقلية ) (۲) وإما أن تصدق الظواهر النقلية وتكذب الظواهر العقلية وذلك باطل - لأنه لا يمكننا أن نعرف صحة الظواهر النقلية ، إلا إذا عرفنا بالدلائل العقلية : إثبات الصانع ، وصفاته ، وكيفية دلالة المعجزة على صدق الرسول وظهور المعجزات على ( يد ) (۳) محمد ولو صار القدح في الدلائل العقلية القطعية ، صار العقل متهما ، غير مقبول القول . ولو كان كذلك خرج عن أن يكون مقبول القول في هذه الأصول وإذا لم تثبت هذه الأصول ، خرجت الدلائل النقلية عن كونها مفيدة فثبت : أن القدح في العقل لتصحيح النقل ، يفضي إلى القدح في العقل والنقل معا . وإنه باطل . ولما بطلت الأقسام الأربعة لم يبق إلا أن يقطع بمقتضى الدلائل العقلية القاطعة : بأن هذه الدلائل النقلية إما أن يقال إنها غير صحيحة ، أو يقال : إنها صحيحة إلا أن المراد منها غير ظواهرها . ثم إن جوزنا التأويل : اشتغلنا على سبيل التبرع بذكر تلك التأويلات على التفصيل . وإن لم تجوز التأويل فوضنا العلم بها إلى الله تعالى . فهذا هو القانون الكلي المرجوع إليه في جميع المتشابهات وبالله التوفيق
Chapter Thirty-Two: Concerning how the situation is when rational proofs confront textual appearances. Know that when definitive rational proofs establish something, then we find textual evidence whose apparent meaning indicates otherwise. In that case, the situation is one of four matters: Either we affirm both the implication of reason and the implication of transmission—necessitating affirmation of two contradictories, which is impossible; or we reject both (1)—necessitating denial of two contradictories, which is impossible; or we deny the textual appearances and affirm the rational implications (2); or we affirm the textual appearances and deny the rational implications. That is invalid because we cannot know the validity of textual appearances except by knowing through rational proofs: the existence of the Maker, His attributes, the manner of the miracle's indication of the truthfulness of the messenger, and the occurrence of miracles at the hands of Muhammad. If there were criticism of definitive rational proofs, reason becomes suspect, not accepted. If it were like that, it would cease to be accepted in these principles. When these principles are not established, textual proofs cease to be useful. So it is established that criticizing reason to uphold transmission leads to criticizing both reason and transmission together, and that is invalid. Since the four divisions are invalid, nothing remains except to decide according to definitive rational proofs that these textual proofs are either said to be invalid, or it is said: They are valid but their intended meaning is other than their apparent meanings. Then, if we permit interpretation, we engage voluntarily in mentioning those interpretations in detail. And if we do not permit interpretation, we consign knowledge of them to God Almighty. This is the universal principle to be referred to in all ambiguous matters. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi on how to handle apparent conflicts between definitive rational proofs and the apparent meanings of revealed texts. He begins by stating the problem: when definitive rational proofs establish something, and we find textual evidence that seems to contradict it, what should we do? He then enumerates four logical possibilities:

1. Affirm both the rational and textual implications. This is impossible because it would require affirming two contradictories simultaneously.
2. Reject both. This is also impossible because it would require denying two contradictories simultaneously.
3. Deny the textual appearances and affirm the rational implications.
4. Affirm the textual appearances and deny the rational implications.

He quickly dismisses the fourth possibility as invalid. The reason is that the validity of textual proofs depends on rational proofs: we need reason to establish the existence of God, His attributes, the veracity of miracles as proof of prophethood, and the specific miracles of Prophet Muhammad. If we undermine definitive rational proofs, we undermine reason itself, and then reason cannot be trusted to establish these foundational principles. Without those principles, the textual proofs themselves lose their evidentiary value. Thus, attacking reason to uphold the letter of revelation destroys both reason and revelation.

Having eliminated three possibilities, only the third remains: we must uphold the rational proof and interpret the textual evidence in a way that does not contradict it. Razi then explains the two sub-options: either we say the textual evidence is not authentic (i.e., its chain is weak or it is not a text at all), or if it is authentic, its intended meaning is not its apparent literal meaning. Then, if we are able to provide a valid interpretation (ta'wil), we do so voluntarily and in detail. If we cannot (or choose not to) interpret, we consign the true knowledge to God (tafwid). He concludes that this is the universal principle to be followed for all ambiguous verses (mutashabihat).

Key Terms
البراهين العقلية
الظواهر النقلية
التأويل
التفويض
المتشابهات
القانون الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when reason and revelation conflict, one must simply reject reason entirely and blindly follow the apparent text.
Correction: Imam al-Razi shows that rejecting reason to uphold revelation is self-defeating because the credibility of revelation itself depends on rational proofs. Instead, reason is upheld and the text is interpreted or its meaning consigned to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, how many possible positions can one take when a definitive rational proof conflicts with the apparent meaning of a revealed text?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The universal principle for resolving conflicts between reason and revelation is to uphold the rational proof and then either deem the text inauthentic, interpret it, or consign its meaning to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a Quranic verse that seems to contradict a clear logical truth, do you feel anxious or defensive? Have you ever suppressed a rational doubt to avoid the discomfort of re-examining your understanding? Al-Razi's principle demands that you trust reason as a gift from Allah to understand revelation. Can you honestly say you do that, or do you cling to comfortable surface meanings even when they cause inner conflict? What would it cost you to admit that some verses may mean something other than what you first thought?
?
Al-Razi argues that rejecting definitive reason to preserve the letter of revelation actually destroys revelation itself, because reason is needed to prove that the revelation is from God. Have you ever, even unintentionally, weakened your own faith by refusing to think critically about what you believe? If so, how might you change that today?
?
This teaching leaves no middle ground: either you uphold reason and interpret (or consign) the text, or you uphold the literal text and deny reason, which leads to the collapse of faith. Which side do you practically lean on when you encounter a difficult passage? Are you willing to surrender your need for absolute textual certainty in favor of a deeper trust in Allah's wisdom and the tools of reason He gave you?

The Universal Principle for Ambiguous Texts
Imam Razi states the general principle for dealing with ambiguous scriptural texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's three‑tiered principle for handling scriptural texts that appear to conflict with reason: rejection, interpretation, or deferral.

Core Idea
Ambiguous texts are handled by a structured principle: reject, interpret, or defer.

Imam Razi provides a clear and concise rule for dealing with ambiguous scriptural passages (mutashābihāt). Before you can decide what a text means, you must first decide whether it is authentic, and then whether its literal meaning is intended. This passage lays out the universal principle that guides the scholar's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Imam Razi uses a very structured, conditional style.
2
Identify the three possible approaches
The passage presents two main options and then two sub‑options under the second. List them: (1) reject the text as unsound, (2) affirm its soundness but reinterpret, then (2a) interpret in detail if allowed, or (2b) defer knowledge to God if not allowed.
The three approaches are rejection, interpretation, and deferral.
3
Understand the conditions
The second option assumes the text is sound but its intended meaning is not its literal meaning. Then the choice between interpretation and deferral depends on whether ta'wīl is deemed permissible.
Imam Razi does not specify here when ta'wīl is permissible; that belongs to other discussions.
4
Reflect on the principle's universality
Imam Razi states that this is the universal principle to be referred to in all ambiguous texts. Consider how this principle provides a consistent methodology for dealing with any apparent conflict between scripture and reason.
This principle is foundational in Ashʿarī hermeneutics.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to consolidate what you have learned from this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقلية إما أن يقال إنها غير صحيحة ، أو ( يقال : إنها صحيحة ) إلا أن المراد منها غير ظواهرها . ثم إن جوزنا التأويل : اشتغلنا على سبيل التبرع بذكر تلك التأويلات على التفصيل . وإن لم تجوز التأويل فوضنا العلم بها إلى الله تعالى . فهذا هو القانون الكلى المرجوع إليه في جميع المتشابهات ( وبالله التوفيق )
The scriptural [texts] are either said to be unsound, or it is said that they are sound, but the intended meaning is other than their apparent meanings. Then if we allow interpretation, we engage, as a voluntary service, in mentioning those interpretations in detail. And if we do not allow interpretation, we consign knowledge of them to God Almighty. This is the universal principle to be referred to in all ambiguous [texts]. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage defines the general principle (al-qānūn al-kullī) that Imam Razi establishes for dealing with ambiguous scriptural texts (al-mutashābihāt). The passage begins by stating two possible approaches when scriptural texts appear to conflict with rational proofs: either we deem those texts to be unsound (ghayr ṣaḥīḥa), or we affirm their soundness (ṣaḥīḥa) while asserting that their intended meaning is not their literal or apparent meaning (ẓawāhir). This second option allows for interpretation (ta’wīl). The author then presents two sub-cases: if interpretation is permissible, we undertake it voluntarily and expound the interpretations in detail; if interpretation is not permissible, we consign full knowledge of the true meaning to God (tafwīḍ). This three-tiered approach—rejection of textual authenticity, interpretation, or deferral—is the comprehensive rule to be applied to all ambiguous passages. The phrase 'wa bi-Llāh al-tawfīq' ('and with God is success') concludes the statement as a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms
النقلية
المتشابهات
التأويل
التفويض
القانون الكلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the principle allows arbitrary interpretation of any problematic text.
Correction: Imam Razi's principle is a structured hierarchy: first assess the text's authenticity, then either interpret (if permissible and within bounds) or defer to God. It does not grant license to reinterpret arbitrarily.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's general principle, what are the three possible approaches when a scriptural text appears to conflict with reason?
A) Reject the text as unsound, interpret it figuratively, or defer knowledge to God
B) Accept the text literally, reinterpret reason, or ignore the conflict
C) Reject reason, interpret the text arbitrarily, or declare the text abrogated
D) Assume the text is metaphorical, seek a hidden meaning, or deny its authenticity

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's universal principle for ambiguous texts is a structured hierarchy: first assess authenticity, then interpret if permissible, otherwise defer to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a Qur'anic verse or hadith whose meaning is unclear, do you immediately try to interpret it, or do you first pause to remind yourself that it might be beyond your understanding? This lesson gives you a structured approach. Which of its three steps have you truly implemented in your study? Starting today, will you commit to either accepting the text's soundness and deferring to God, or seeking a valid interpretation—rather than imposing your own opinion?
?
Think of a specific verse about Allah's attributes that has troubled you. According to this principle, you must first affirm it is sound but not literal. Have you ever rejected such a verse in your heart because it seemed contradictory? This lesson demands that you either interpret it within the bounds of scholarly ta'wīl or humbly say 'God knows best.' Which will you choose, and what will that choice cost your ego?
?
Imam Razi says that interpretation is a 'voluntary service' (على سبيل التبرع). This means it is not an obligation but a gift. Are you studying Islamic theology out of a sincere desire to serve God, or to satisfy your own intellectual pride? If interpretation is optional, would you be content to remain silent about a text's meaning if you lack certainty? What is the state of your heart when you read ambiguous texts—is it humility or arrogance?

The First Proof: Quranic Verses on Understandability
Razi enumerates three Quranic proofs for comprehensibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quranic verses to argue that the Quran is comprehensible and why this refutes those who claim parts of it are inaccessible.

Core Idea
The Quran commands reflection, proving it must be understandable.

In this passage, Razi tackles a fundamental question: can the Quran contain passages that are beyond human understanding? He notes that some scholars accept this possibility, but theologians (mutakallimun) deny it. Razi then presents the first set of proofs from the Quran itself. By examining three verses, he shows that the Quran's own commands and purposes require it to be comprehensible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and positions
Read the first part of the passage to understand the question being debated and note the two opposing views mentioned.
The issue is whether the Quran can contain things beyond human knowledge.
2
Examine the first verse
Look at the first verse cited (Surah Muhammad 24) and the argument Razi draws from it. What does the command to 'reflect' imply?
3
Examine the second verse
Consider the second verse (Surah An-Nisa 82) and how it links reflection to recognizing the absence of contradiction.
This verse also commands reflection to verify divine origin.
4
Examine the third verse
Study the third verse (Surah Ash-Shu'ara 192-195) and the argument that warning requires understanding.
5
Synthesize the overall argument
Summarize how all three verses collectively prove that the Quran must be comprehensible.
Each verse presupposes understanding for its purpose (reflection, warning).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في أنه هل يجوز أن يحصل في كتاب الله تعالى ، ما لا سبيل لنا الى العلم به ؟ اعلم : أن كثيرا من الفقهاء والمحدثين والصوفية ، يجوزون ذلك والمتكلمون ينكرونه . واحتجوا بالآيات والأخبار و المعقول . أما الآيات فكثيرة . أحدها : قوله تعالى : « أفلا يتدبرون القرآن، أم على قلوب أقفاطا »، أمر الناس بالتدبر في القرآن . ولو كان القرآن غير مفهوم ، فكيف يأمرنا بالتدبر فيه ؟ الثاني : قوله تعالى : « أفلا يتدبرون القرآن ؟ ولو كان من عند غير الله لوجدوا فيه اختلافا كثيرا »، فكيف يأمرنا بالتدبر فيه ، لمعرفة التناقض في الاختلاف ، مع أنه غير مفهوم للخلق ؟ الثالث : قوله تعالى : ( وإنه لتنزيل رب العالمين ، نزل به الروح الأمين، على قلبك . لتكون من المنذرين، بلسان عربي مبين )، ولو لم يكن مفهوما ، فكيف يمكن أن يكون الرسول منذرا به ؟
The First Chapter: On whether it is permissible that there occur in the Book of God, exalted, that for which we have no way to knowledge? Know that many of the jurists, hadith scholars, and Sufis permit that, while the theologians deny it. And they argued with the verses, the reports, and the rational. As for the verses, they are many. First: His saying, exalted: 'Do they not then reflect on the Quran? Or are there locks upon their hearts?' He commanded people to reflect on the Quran. If the Quran were not understandable, how could He command us to reflect on it? Second: His saying, exalted: 'Do they not then reflect on the Quran? And if it had been from other than God, they would have found in it much contradiction.' So how does He command us to reflect on it, in order to know the contradiction in the discrepancy, while it is not understandable to the creation? Third: His saying, exalted: 'And indeed, it is a revelation of the Lord of the worlds, brought down by the Trustworthy Spirit, upon your heart, that you may be of the warners, in a clear Arabic tongue.' And if it were not understandable, how could the messenger warn by it?
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents the first type of evidence—Quranic verses—for the position held by the theologians (mutakallimun) that the Quran is comprehensible. The author begins by defining the issue: whether it is possible that the Quran contains something inaccessible to human knowledge. He notes that many jurists, hadith scholars, and Sufis affirm this possibility, but the theologians deny it. Then he states that the theologians adduce proofs from verses, hadiths, and reason. Focusing on the verses, he gives three examples. The first is from Surah Muhammad (47:24): 'Do they not then reflect on the Quran? Or are there locks upon their hearts?' The argument is that God commands reflection (tadabbur), which would be meaningless if the Quran were incomprehensible. The second verse is from Surah An-Nisa (4:82): 'Do they not then reflect on the Quran? And if it had been from other than God, they would have found in it much contradiction.' Here, reflection is commanded to discover the absence of contradiction, which proves divine origin. This again presupposes understanding. The third verse is from Surah Ash-Shu'ara (26:192-195): 'And indeed, it is a revelation of the Lord of the worlds, brought down by the Trustworthy Spirit, upon your heart, that you may be of the warners, in a clear Arabic tongue.' The warning requires understanding; if the Quran were incomprehensible, the Prophet could not warn people with it. Thus, all three verses implicitly assert that the Quran is understandable.

Key Terms
التدبر
المفهوم
المنذر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'tadabbur' (reflection) means mere recitation without understanding.
Correction: The passage shows that tadabbur implies comprehension, as the command to reflect would be meaningless if the Quran were incomprehensible.

Checkpoint
Which verse does the author use to argue that the Quran must be understandable because it is a warning in clear Arabic?
A) Surah Muhammad 24
B) Surah An-Nisa 82
C) Surah Ash-Shu'ara 192-195
D) Surah Al-Baqara 2

Key Takeaway
Razi uses three Quranic verses to demonstrate that the Quran's own commands to reflect and its purpose of warning require it to be comprehensible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly reflect on its verses as God commands, or do you let your mind wander? What specific step will you take today to ensure you understand what you recite?
?
Razi argues that the Quran must be understandable for the Prophet to warn with it. Are you treating the Quran as a warning that demands a response from you, or as a text you have heard so often that it no longer moves you? What is one warning from a recent recitation that you have ignored?
?
God commands reflection to find consistency in the Quran. Examine your own life: is there a contradiction between what you believe the Quran says and how you live? If you find one, what will you change this week to align your actions with the Quran?

Linguistic Meaning of Muhkam and Mutashabih
Imam Razi defines muhkam and mutashabih linguistically with examples from Arabic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meanings of the Qur'anic terms 'muhkam' and 'mutashabih', and how their root meanings relate to clarity and ambiguity.

Core Idea
Muhkam means 'prevented from ambiguity' and mutashabih means 'resemblance causing confusion.'

Before diving into the complex theological debates about clear and ambiguous verses, Imam Razi grounds us in the Arabic language. He shows that the words 'muhkam' and 'mutashabih' have concrete meanings rooted in everyday usage. This passage lays the linguistic foundation, helping us see that muhkam is about prevention of confusion, while mutashabih is about resemblance that puzzles the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own progression from muhkam to mutashabih come through.
Notice the Qur'anic verse cited at the beginning.
2
Identify the root meaning of muhkam
The passage explains that muhkam comes from roots meaning 'to prevent' or 'to restrain.' Look for the examples: the judge, the bridle bit, the strong building, wisdom. Each illustrates prevention.
This root meaning gives muhkam its sense of clarity – it prevents ambiguity.
3
Identify the root meaning of mutashabih
Mutashabih is defined as two things resembling each other so closely that the mind cannot distinguish them. Find the Qur'anic examples and the hadith about ambiguous matters.
The core idea is confusion due to similarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو الذي أنزل عليك الكتاب منه آيات محكمات هن أم الكتاب وأخر متشابهات. ولابد لنا من تفسير المحكم والمتشابه بحسب أصل اللغة، ثم من تفسيرهما في عرف الشريعة. أما المحكم في اللغة: فالعرب تقول حكمت وأحكمت وحكمت، بمعنى: رددت ومنعت، والحاكم يمنع الظالم عن الظلم، وحكمة اللجام تمنع الفرس عن الاضطراب. وفي حديث الشخصى: أحكم اليتيم كما تحكم ولدك، أي امنعه من الفساد. وقوله: أحكموا سفهاءكم، أي امنعوهم. وبناء محكم أي وثيق يمنع من تعرض له. وسميت الحكمة حكمة، لأنها تمنع الموصوف بها عما لا ينبغي. وأما المتشابه: فهو أن يكون أحد الشيئين متشابها للآخر، بحيث يعجز الذهن عن التمييز. قال الله تعالى: إن البقر تشابه علينا. وقال: تشابهت قلوبهم. ومنه اشتبه الأمر، إذا لم يفرق بينهما. ويقال لأصحاب المخاريق: أصحاب الشبهات. وقال عليه السلام: الحلال بين والحرام بين وبينهما أمور مشتبهات، وفي رواية أخرى متشابهات. فهذا تحقيق الكلام في المحكم والمتشابه بحسب أصل اللغة.
He is the one who sent down to you the Book. From it are clear verses – they are the mother of the Book – and others ambiguous. And we must interpret muhkam and mutashabih according to the original language, then interpret both in the custom of the Law. As for muhkam in language: the Arabs say 'hakamtu', 'ahkamtu', and 'hakkamtu', meaning 'I prevented' and 'I restrained'. The judge prevents the oppressor from oppression. The bit of the bridle prevents the horse from agitation. In the hadith of the person: 'Restrain the orphan as you restrain your own child', i.e., prevent him from corruption. And his saying: 'Restrain your foolish ones', i.e., prevent them. A strong building is called muhkam because it prevents whoever approaches it. Wisdom (hikma) is so called because it prevents the one described by it from what is not proper. As for mutashabih: it is that one of two things resembles the other such that the mind is incapable of distinguishing. Allah said: 'Indeed, the cows have become similar to us' (2:70). And He said: 'Their hearts have become similar' (2:118). From it is 'the matter became ambiguous' when one cannot differentiate between them. The people of trickery are called the people of ambiguities (shubuhat). The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The lawful is clear, the unlawful is clear, and between them are ambiguous matters' – in another narration 'mutashabihat'. So this is the verification of the speech concerning muhkam and mutashabih according to the original language.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage defines the terms muhkam and mutashabih linguistically. The author begins by citing the Qur'anic verse (3:7) that mentions both, establishing the need for interpretation. He then explains that 'muhkam' in Arabic comes from roots meaning 'to prevent' or 'to restrain', giving examples from the language: the judge prevents injustice, the bridle bit prevents the horse's agitation, a strong building prevents entry, and wisdom prevents foolishness. This root meaning of prevention implies clarity and certainty, so muhkam means a clear, unambiguous statement. Then 'mutashabih' is defined as two things that resemble each other so closely that the mind cannot distinguish between them, citing Qur'anic instances and the Prophet's hadith about lawful, unlawful, and ambiguous matters. The conclusion: these are the linguistic foundations for the terms.

Key Terms
محكم (muhkam)
متشابه (mutashabih)
حكمة (hikma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muhkam simply means clear or plain.
Correction: Muhkam linguistically means 'prevented' from ambiguity, implying clarity through the prevention of confusion.

Checkpoint
What is the linguistic root meaning of muhkam?
A) Resemblance
B) Prevention
C) Clarity
D) Ambiguity

Key Takeaway
The linguistic meanings of muhkam (prevention) and mutashabih (resemblance causing confusion) are foundational for understanding their technical Qur'anic usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter verses of the Quran that seem 'mutashabih' to you, do you react with humility and seek knowledge, or do you rush to interpret them according to your own understanding? What prevents you from seeking clarification?
?
Your own soul can be muhkam or mutashabih. Are you 'muhkam'—firmly grounded, prevented from corruption—or are you easily confused and resembling those who follow desires? What specific steps will you take today to become more muhkam?
?
The Prophet said 'between them are ambiguous matters.' Think of an area in your life where the ruling is not clear to you. Have you left it and sought clarity (as the hadith implies), or have you remained in doubt? What is one ambiguous matter you will resolve today by asking a scholar or studying?

Classification of Words into Nass, Zahir, Mujmal, Mu'awwal
Imam Razi classifies words into nass, zahir, mujmal, and mu'awwal based on probability of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi classifies words based on their probability for multiple meanings, distinguishing nass, zahir, mujmal, and mu'awwal.

Core Idea
Words in the Qur'an are classified by their degree of clarity: nass is clear, zahir is probable, mujmal is ambiguous, mu'awwal is interpreted.

Imam Razi builds on earlier discussions to provide a systematic classification of Qur'anic words. He focuses on whether a word can bear multiple meanings and how likely each meaning is. This classification is essential for understanding how scholars derive rulings and interpret the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification criteria
Read the Arabic passage where Imam Razi explains the basis of classification: whether the word is subject to another meaning.
Focus on the logical distinctions.
2
Identify the four categories
Find the four types: nass, zahir, mujmal, and mu'awwal. Notice how each is defined.
Nass is unequivocal; zahir has a preponderant meaning; mujmal is equally balanced; mu'awwal is the outweighed meaning.
3
Distinguish between nass and zahir
Both have preponderance, but nass prevents the opposite meaning while zahir does not.
This difference is crucial for interpretation.
4
Understand mujmal and mu'awwal
Both lack preponderance; mujmal has no preponderance at all, while mu'awwal has preponderance for one side but that side is not the intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في عرف العلماء. فاعلم: أن الناس قد أكثروا في تفسير المحكم والمتشابه، وكتب من تقدمنا مشتملة عليهما. والذي عندى فيه: أن اللفظ الذي جعل موضوعاً لمعنى. إما أن يكون محتملا لغير ذلك المعنى، أو لا يكون؟ فإن كان موضوعاً لمعنى ولم يكن محتملا لغيره فهو النص وإن كان محتملا لغير ذلك المعنى. فإما أن يكون احتماله لأحدهما راجحاً على الآخر، وإما أن لا يكون. بل يكون احتماله لهما على السوية. فإن كان احتماله لأحدهما راجحاً على احتماله للآخر، كان ذلك اللفظ بالنسبة إلى الراجح ظاهرا، وبالنسبة إلى المرجوح مؤولا، وأما إن كان احتماله لهما على السوية. كان اللفظ بالنسبة إليهما معاً مشتركا، وبالنسبة إلى كل واحد منهما على التعيين محملا. خرج من هذا التقسيم: أن الألفاظ إما أن تكون نصاً أو ظاهراً أو مجملا أو مؤولا. فالنص والظاهر يشتركان في حصول الترجيح، إلا أن النص راجح مانع من النقيض، والظاهر راجح غير مانع من النقيض. وهذا القدر هو المسمى بالمحكم. وأما المجمل والمؤول. فهما يشتركان في أن دلالة اللفظ غير راجحة. إلا أن المجمل لا رجحان فيه بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من الطرفين، والمؤول فيه رجحان بالنسبة إلى طرف المشترك، وهو عدم الرجحان بالنسبة إليه، وهو المسمى بالمتشابه، لأن عدم الفهم حاصل فيه. ثم اعلم: أن اللفظ إذا كان بالنسبة إلى المفهومين على السوية. فهنا يتوقف الذهن. مثل القرء بالنسبة إلى الحيض والطهر. وإنما الصعب المشكل: أن يكون اللفظ بأصل وضعه راجحاً في أحد المفهومين، ومرجوحاً في الآخر. ثم إن الراجح يكون باطلا، والمرجوح حقاً. مثاله من القرآن: قوله تعالى: وإذا أردنا أن نهلك قرية أمرنا مترفيها ففسقوا فيها. فظاهر هذا الكلام: أنهم يؤمرون بأن يفسقوا. ومحكمه قوله تعالى: إن الله لا يأمر بالفحشاء، رداً على الكفار.
As for in the custom of the scholars: Know that the people have spoken much about the interpretation of muhkam and mutashabih, and the books of our predecessors contain them. What I hold concerning it is that the word which is made to signify a meaning is either susceptible to another meaning or not. If it is made to signify a meaning and is not susceptible to another, then it is nass (plain text). And if it is susceptible to another meaning, then either its probability for one of them is preponderant over the other, or it is not, but rather its probability for both is equal. If its probability for one of them is preponderant over its probability for the other, then that word is, with respect to the preponderant meaning, zahir (apparent), and with respect to the outweighed meaning, mu'awwal (interpreted). And if its probability for both is equal, then the word is, with respect to both together, mushtarak (shared), and with respect to each one individually, muhtamal (possible). From this classification emerges: that words are either nass, or zahir, or mujmal (ambiguous), or mu'awwal. Thus nass and zahir share in having preponderance, except that nass is preponderant and prevents the opposite, while zahir is preponderant but does not prevent the opposite. This amount is what is called muhkam. As for mujmal and mu'awwal, they share in that the indication of the word is not preponderant, except that mujmal has no preponderance with respect to each of the two sides, while mu'awwal has preponderance with respect to one side of the shared meaning – that is, lack of preponderance with respect to it – and this is what is called mutashabih, because lack of understanding occurs in it. Then know: if the word is with respect to the two meanings equally, then the mind halts, like al-qur' (menstrual period) with respect to menstruation and purity. The difficult and problematic case is when the word, according to its original usage, is preponderant in one of the two meanings and outweighed in the other, and then the preponderant meaning is false and the outweighed meaning is true. An example from the Qur'an is His saying: 'And when We intend to destroy a township, We command its affluent ones, then they commit transgression therein' (17:16). The apparent (zahir) of this speech is that they are commanded to commit transgression. Its muhkam is His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not command immorality' (7:28), as a refutation against the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's classification of the Qur'anic text types in the terminology of scholars. He first notes the extensive discussions among predecessors. Then he introduces his own view based on whether a word is subject to multiple meanings. He divides words into four categories: nass (unequivocal text that cannot bear another meaning), zahir (apparent meaning that is preponderant but not exclusive), mujmal (ambiguous, equally balanced between meanings), and mu'awwal (interpreted meaning that is outweighed but true). He then maps these to muhkam and mutashabih: nass and zahir together constitute muhkam (clear) because they have preponderance, though nass prevents contradiction while zahir does not. Mujmal and mu'awwal together constitute mutashabih (ambiguous) because neither has definitive preponderance; mujmal is completely balanced, while mu'awwal has preponderance for one side but that side is false, leading to ambiguity. He provides an example from Qur'an 17:16 which apparently commands sin, but the muhkam verse 7:28 clarifies that Allah does not command sin, requiring a ta'wil (interpretation) of the apparently contradictory verse.

Key Terms
نص (nass)
ظاهر (zahir)
مجمل (mujmal)
مؤول (mu'awwal)
مشترك (mushtarak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Zahir and nass are the same because both have clear meanings.
Correction: Nass is unequivocal and prevents contradiction, while zahir is preponderant but open to interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, which category describes a word that can bear only one meaning?
A) Zahir
B) Nass
C) Mujmal
D) Mu'awwal

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's classification of words into nass, zahir, mujmal, and mu'awwal is based on the probability of multiple meanings, essential for understanding Qur'anic interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a clear command in the Qur'an (nass), do you accept it without hesitation, or do you look for ways to avoid it? What does your reaction reveal about your heart?
?
Have you ever insisted on an interpretation (mu'awwal) of a verse that contradicts its apparent meaning (zahir) because it suited your desires? Will you repent and submit to what is more probable?
?
Do you sometimes treat ambiguous verses (mujmal) as if they support your views when you haven't sought proper knowledge? Commit today to ask a scholar before assuming you understand.

Mapping to Muhkam and Mutashabih
Imam Razi links the four word categories to muhkam and mutashabih with a relevant example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi maps nass and zahir to muhkam, and mujmal and mu'awwal to mutashabih, with a practical Qur'anic example.

Core Idea
Muhkam includes nass and zahir (clear with preponderance), while mutashabih includes mujmal and mu'awwal (ambiguous or requiring interpretation).

Having classified words, Imam Razi now connects these categories to the well-known Qur'anic terms muhkam (clear) and mutashabih (ambiguous). He explains that nass and zahir together form muhkam, while mujmal and mu'awwal form mutashabih. He then illustrates the challenging scenario where the apparent meaning is false and requires interpretation, using a verse from the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mapping
Read the passage where Imam Razi states that nass and zahir share preponderance and this is muhkam.
Notice that nass prevents the opposite while zahir does not.
2
Define mutashabih
The passage states that mujmal and mu'awwal share lack of preponderance, and this is called mutashabih.
In mu'awwal, there is preponderance for one side but that side is false, causing ambiguity.
3
Examine the example
Imam Razi gives the example of Qur'an 17:16 which apparently commands sin, and the muhkam verse 7:28 clarifies that Allah does not command sin.
This shows how a zahir meaning is outweighed by a stronger proof.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this classification helps resolve apparent contradictions in the Qur'an.
The mu'awwal interpretation is the true meaning despite being less apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في عرف العلماء. فاعلم: أن الناس قد أكثروا في تفسير المحكم والمتشابه، وكتب من تقدمنا مشتملة عليهما. والذي عندى فيه: أن اللفظ الذي جعل موضوعاً لمعنى. إما أن يكون محتملا لغير ذلك المعنى، أو لا يكون؟ فإن كان موضوعاً لمعنى ولم يكن محتملا لغيره فهو النص وإن كان محتملا لغير ذلك المعنى. فإما أن يكون احتماله لأحدهما راجحاً على الآخر، وإما أن لا يكون. بل يكون احتماله لهما على السوية. فإن كان احتماله لأحدهما راجحاً على احتماله للآخر، كان ذلك اللفظ بالنسبة إلى الراجح ظاهرا، وبالنسبة إلى المرجوح مؤولا، وأما إن كان احتماله لهما على السوية. كان اللفظ بالنسبة إليهما معاً مشتركا، وبالنسبة إلى كل واحد منهما على التعيين محملا. خرج من هذا التقسيم: أن الألفاظ إما أن تكون نصاً أو ظاهراً أو مجملا أو مؤولا. فالنص والظاهر يشتركان في حصول الترجيح، إلا أن النص راجح مانع من النقيض، والظاهر راجح غير مانع من النقيض. وهذا القدر هو المسمى بالمحكم. وأما المجمل والمؤول. فهما يشتركان في أن دلالة اللفظ غير راجحة. إلا أن المجمل لا رجحان فيه بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من الطرفين، والمؤول فيه رجحان بالنسبة إلى طرف المشترك، وهو عدم الرجحان بالنسبة إليه، وهو المسمى بالمتشابه، لأن عدم الفهم حاصل فيه. ثم اعلم: أن اللفظ إذا كان بالنسبة إلى المفهومين على السوية. فهنا يتوقف الذهن. مثل القرء بالنسبة إلى الحيض والطهر. وإنما الصعب المشكل: أن يكون اللفظ بأصل وضعه راجحاً في أحد المفهومين، ومرجوحاً في الآخر. ثم إن الراجح يكون باطلا، والمرجوح حقاً. مثاله من القرآن: قوله تعالى: وإذا أردنا أن نهلك قرية أمرنا مترفيها ففسقوا فيها. فظاهر هذا الكلام: أنهم يؤمرون بأن يفسقوا. ومحكمه قوله تعالى: إن الله لا يأمر بالفحشاء، رداً على الكفار.
As for in the custom of the scholars: Know that the people have spoken much about the interpretation of muhkam and mutashabih, and the books of our predecessors contain them. What I hold concerning it is that the word which is made to signify a meaning is either susceptible to another meaning or not. If it is made to signify a meaning and is not susceptible to another, then it is nass (plain text). And if it is susceptible to another meaning, then either its probability for one of them is preponderant over the other, or it is not, but rather its probability for both is equal. If its probability for one of them is preponderant over its probability for the other, then that word is, with respect to the preponderant meaning, zahir (apparent), and with respect to the outweighed meaning, mu'awwal (interpreted). And if its probability for both is equal, then the word is, with respect to both together, mushtarak (shared), and with respect to each one individually, muhtamal (possible). From this classification emerges: that words are either nass, or zahir, or mujmal (ambiguous), or mu'awwal. Thus nass and zahir share in having preponderance, except that nass is preponderant and prevents the opposite, while zahir is preponderant but does not prevent the opposite. This amount is what is called muhkam. As for mujmal and mu'awwal, they share in that the indication of the word is not preponderant, except that mujmal has no preponderance with respect to each of the two sides, while mu'awwal has preponderance with respect to one side of the shared meaning – that is, lack of preponderance with respect to it – and this is what is called mutashabih, because lack of understanding occurs in it. Then know: if the word is with respect to the two meanings equally, then the mind halts, like al-qur' (menstrual period) with respect to menstruation and purity. The difficult and problematic case is when the word, according to its original usage, is preponderant in one of the two meanings and outweighed in the other, and then the preponderant meaning is false and the outweighed meaning is true. An example from the Qur'an is His saying: 'And when We intend to destroy a township, We command its affluent ones, then they commit transgression therein' (17:16). The apparent (zahir) of this speech is that they are commanded to commit transgression. Its muhkam is His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not command immorality' (7:28), as a refutation against the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's classification of the Qur'anic text types in the terminology of scholars. He first notes the extensive discussions among predecessors. Then he introduces his own view based on whether a word is subject to multiple meanings. He divides words into four categories: nass (unequivocal text that cannot bear another meaning), zahir (apparent meaning that is preponderant but not exclusive), mujmal (ambiguous, equally balanced between meanings), and mu'awwal (interpreted meaning that is outweighed but true). He then maps these to muhkam and mutashabih: nass and zahir together constitute muhkam (clear) because they have preponderance, though nass prevents contradiction while zahir does not. Mujmal and mu'awwal together constitute mutashabih (ambiguous) because neither has definitive preponderance; mujmal is completely balanced, while mu'awwal has preponderance for one side but that side is false, leading to ambiguity. He provides an example from Qur'an 17:16 which apparently commands sin, but the muhkam verse 7:28 clarifies that Allah does not command sin, requiring a ta'wil (interpretation) of the apparently contradictory verse.

Key Terms
محكم (muhkam)
متشابه (mutashabih)
قرء (al-qur')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All mutashabih verses are completely unclear and cannot be understood.
Correction: Mutashabih includes mu'awwal where interpretation yields the true meaning, as in the verse about command to transgress.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what is the defining characteristic of mutashabih?
A) The word has a clear single meaning
B) The indication of the word is not preponderant
C) The word is always interpreted in a metaphorical sense
D) The word is used only in legal verses

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi maps nass and zahir to muhkam, and mujmal and mu'awwal to mutashabih, illustrating with a Qur'anic example where the apparent meaning is false and requires interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Qur'anic verse that seems to contradict other clear verses or your understanding of Allah's attributes, do you immediately seek deeper interpretation, or do you let the apparent meaning cause doubt? How can you train yourself to follow the example of the scholars who prioritize muhkam over zahir?
?
The example of verse 17:16 shows that the apparent meaning can be false and even harmful if taken literally. Have you ever clung to a literal reading of a text (not necessarily the Qur'an) that led you to a wrong conclusion? What steps can you take to ensure you interpret Allah's words correctly?
?
Imam Razi mentions that mu'awwal involves a preponderant meaning that is false. This requires humility to admit that our first understanding may be wrong. Are you willing to abandon a comfortable interpretation when it contradicts the muhkam? What is one belief or practice you hold that you have never critically examined against the clear verses of the Qur'an?

The Problem of Subjective Interpretation in Muhkam and Mutashabih
Razi illustrates subjective sectarian claims about muhkam and mutashabih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a neutral fundamental principle is needed to determine which Quranic verses are muhkam (clear) or mutashabih (ambiguous).

Core Idea
Without a standard, each sect claims its favored verses are clear and opponents' are ambiguous.

Razi opens the third chapter by pointing out a practical problem: Muslims disagree on which verses are muhkam and which are mutashabih, often based on their theological commitments. He gives concrete examples from the Mu'tazila and Sunni schools to show that without an objective criterion, the classification becomes subjective. This lesson explores the nature of that problem and the need for a foundational principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the contrasting claims
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Observe how each sect (Mu'tazili and Sunni) labels verses supporting their view as muhkam and opposing verses as mutashabih.
The examples involve free will and divine will verses.
2
Identify the core problem
Razi states that each school claims its own verses are muhkam and the opponent's are mutashabih. This creates a subjective gridlock.
3
Understand the need for a principle
Razi concludes: 'So there must be here a fundamental principle to refer to in this matter.' This is the key takeaway – without a neutral rule, the debate cannot be resolved.
The phrase 'qānūn aṣlī' (fundamental principle) is central.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث: الطريق الذي يعرف به كون الآية محكمة أو متشابهة. اعلم: أن هذا موضع عظيم وذلك لأن كل واحد من أصحاب المذاهب يدعى أن الآيات الموافقة لمذهبه: محكمة، والآيات الموافقة لمذهب الخصم: متشابهة. فالمعتزلى يقول: إن قوله: «فمن شاء فليؤمن، ومن شاء فليكفر»: محكم. وقوله: «وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله»: متشابه. والسنى يقلب القضية فى هذا الباب والأمثلة كثيرة. فلا بد ههنا من قانون أصلى يرجع إليه في هذا الباب. فنقول: إذا كان لفظ الآية والخبر ظاهرا في معنى، فإنما يجوز لنا ترك ذلك الظاهر بدليل منفصل، وإلا لخرج الكلام عن أن يكون مفيدا، ولخرج القرآن من أن يكون حجة. ثم ذلك الدليل المنفصل إما أن يكون لفظيا أو عقليا. أما القسم الأول: فنقول هذا إنما يتم إذا حصل من ذينك الدليلين اللفظيين تعارض. وإذا وقع التعارض بينهما، فليس ترك أحدهما لإبقاء الآخر أولى من العكس. اللهم إلا أن يقال: أحد الدليلين قاطع، والآخر ظاهر، والقاطع راجح على الظاهر. أو يقال: كل واحد منهما وإن كان ظاهرا إلا أن أحدهما أقوى. إلا أنا نقول: أما الأول فباطل. لأن الدلائل اللفظية لا تكون قطعية، لأنها موقوفة على نقل اللغات، ونقل وجوه النحو والتصريف، وعلى عدم الاشتراك، والمجاز،
Chapter Three: The method by which it is known whether a verse is muhkam or mutashabih. Know that this is a great topic, because each one of the adherents of the schools claims that the verses agreeing with his school are muhkam, and the verses agreeing with the opponent's school are mutashabih. The Mu'tazili says: His saying: 'So whoever wills, let him believe; and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' is muhkam. And His saying: 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' is mutashabih. And the Sunni reverses the case in this matter, and the examples are many. So there must be here a fundamental principle to refer to in this matter. So we say: If the wording of a verse or report is apparent in a meaning, then it is only permissible for us to leave that apparent meaning due to a separate evidence; otherwise speech would cease to be beneficial, and the Qur'an would cease to be a proof. Then that separate evidence is either verbal or rational. As for the first division, we say: This only holds if there occurs a conflict between those two verbal evidences. And when conflict occurs between them, it is not that leaving one to preserve the other is more appropriate than the reverse. Unless it is said that one of the two evidences is definitive and the other is apparent, and the definitive outweighs the apparent. Or it is said that each of them, even if apparent, one of them is stronger. But we say: As for the first claim, it is false, because verbal evidences cannot be definitive, since they depend on transmission of languages, transmission of aspects of grammar and morphology, and on the absence of homonymy and figurative usage.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the third chapter, where Razi establishes the need for an objective principle to determine whether a Quranic verse is muhkam (clear) or mutashabih (ambiguous). He observes that each sect (e.g., Mu'tazila vs. Sunnis) claims that verses supporting their view are muhkam and those supporting the opponent are mutashabih, citing examples from verses on free will and divine will. To avoid this subjectivity, a fundamental principle (qānūn aṣlī) is required. Razi then proposes a principle: if a text is apparent (ẓāhir) in meaning, we can depart from that apparent meaning only based on a separate evidence (dalīl munfaṣil). Otherwise, the text would lose its communicative benefit and the Qur'an would cease to be a proof. This separate evidence can be verbal (lafẓī) or rational (ʿaqlī). He then addresses the case of conflict between two verbal evidences. If they conflict, neither can be simply abandoned in favor of the other unless one is definitive (qāṭiʿ) and the other merely apparent. But Razi argues that this is false because verbal evidences can never be definitive, as they depend on uncertain factors like transmission of languages, grammar, etc. Thus, the apparent meaning of a verse cannot be overridden by another verbal evidence that is also merely apparent. This sets the stage for the role of rational evidence.

Key Terms
محكم
متشابه
قانون أصلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muhkam and mutashabih are fixed, objective categories inherent in the text.
Correction: Razi shows that different sects classify the same verses differently based on their doctrines, so an external neutral principle is needed to avoid subjectivity.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say a fundamental principle is needed?
A) Because the Quran is unclear
B) Because different sects claim different verses as muhkam or mutashabih
C) Because the Prophet did not explain the categories
D) Because there are too many mutashabih verses

Key Takeaway
A neutral fundamental principle is necessary to resolve subjective sectarian claims about which Quranic verses are muhkam or mutashabih.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you read the Quran with a filter that confirms your own beliefs, or are you willing to let the text challenge you? Razi exposes how sects twist verses to suit their agendas—where might you be doing the same without realizing it? Commit to one verse you've been avoiding because it unsettles you, and study it with an open heart this week.
?
If you were to apply Razi's call for an objective principle to your own life, what would that principle be? Is your understanding of the Quran governed by a genuine desire for truth, or by comfort and habit? Ask yourself: Am I ready to accept a truth that contradicts what I've always assumed?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to support your opinion, do you immediately label it 'clear'? And when you read one that opposes your view, do you dismiss it as 'ambiguous'? How can you actually submit to the Quran's authority rather than using it to justify your own? Name one specific area where you need to surrender your preference to the text.

The Principle: Apparent Meaning Requires Separate Evidence
Razi proposes the principle and argues against definitive verbal evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's principle that the apparent meaning of a text can only be abandoned due to separate evidence, and why verbal evidences are never definitive.

Core Idea
The apparent meaning stands unless a separate evidence overrides it, but verbal evidences can never be definitive.

Having established the need for a principle, Razi now proposes one: if a verse or report has an apparent (ẓāhir) meaning, we may depart from it only if there is a separate evidence. This evidence can be verbal or rational. He then examines a potential conflict between two verbal evidences and argues that no verbal evidence can be definitive due to its dependence on uncertain linguistic factors. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle statement
Read the beginning of the passage where Razi states the principle: 'If the wording... is apparent in a meaning, then it is only permissible for us to leave that apparent meaning due to a separate evidence.'
The principle avoids arbitrary departure from apparent meaning.
2
Understand the classification of separate evidence
Razi divides separate evidence into two types: verbal (lafẓī) and rational (ʿaqlī). This is an important classification for the argument.
3
Examine the conflict scenario
Razi considers what happens when two verbal evidences conflict. He notes that it is not simply a matter of preferring one, unless one is definitive (qāṭiʿ) and the other apparent, or one is stronger.
4
Grasp why verbal evidences are never definitive
Razi rejects the possibility of a definitive verbal evidence because it relies on uncertain factors: transmission of language, grammar, and absence of homonymy and figurative usage. This is a key critique.
This means the apparent meaning cannot be overridden by another verbal evidence that is merely apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The Problem of Interpreting Quranic Verses
Razi introduces the problem of sectarian bias and the need for a principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the problem Imam Razi identifies in interpreting the Qur'an and the foundational principle he sets down to resolve it.

Core Idea
Every sect claims its verses are clear and the opponent's are ambiguous.

Imam Razi begins by highlighting a critical issue in Islamic theology: each school of thought interprets verses according to its own doctrine, calling supporting verses muhkam (clear) and opposing verses mutashabih (ambiguous). To escape this subjective impasse, he proposes a foundational principle (qanun asli) that governs all interpretation: you cannot abandon the apparent meaning of a verse without a separate evidence. This lesson introduces that problem and the initial framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage up to the foundational principle
Read from the beginning until 'then that separate evidence is either verbal or rational'. Focus on understanding the problem and the principle.
Notice how Razi sets up the need for an objective standard.
2
Understand the problem of sectarian bias
Consider the example of Mu'tazili and Sunni each labeling different verses as muhkam or mutashabih. Why is this a problem for sound interpretation?
3
Identify the foundational principle
Locate the line where Razi states: 'There must be here a foundational principle to refer to in this topic.' What is that principle?
The principle is: the apparent meaning cannot be abandoned without a separate evidence.
4
Distinguish the two types of separate evidence
Razi categorizes separate evidence as either verbal or rational. Pause to absorb this division before moving to the next lesson.
Verbal evidence will be criticized later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث: الطريق الذي يعرف به كون الآية محكمة أو متشابهة. اعلم: أن هذا موضع عظيم وذلك لأن كل واحد من أصحاب المذاهب يدعي أن الآيات الموافقة لمذهبه: محكمة، والآيات الموافقة لمذهب الخصم: متشابهة. فالمعتزلي يقول: إن قوله: «فمن شاء فليؤمن، ومن شاء فليكفر»، محكم. وقوله: «وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله»، متشابه. والسنى يقلب القضية في هذا الباب والأمثلة كثيرة. فلا بد ههنا من قانون أصلي يرجع إليه في هذا الباب. فنقول: إذا كان لفظ الآية والخبر ظاهرا في معنى، فإنما يجوز لنا ترك ذلك الظاهر بدليل منفصل، وإلا لخرج الكلام عن أن يكون مفيدا، ولخرج القرآن من أن يكون حجة. ثم ذلك الدليل المنفصل إما أن يكون لفظيا أو عقليا. أما القسم الأول: فنقول هذا إنما يتم إذا حصل من ذينك الدليلين اللفظيين: تعارض. وإذا وقع التعارض بينهما، فليس ترك أحدهما لإبقاء الآخر أولى من العكس. اللهم إلا أن يقال: أحد الدليلين قاطع، والآخر ظاهر. والقاطع راجح على الظاهر. أو يقال: كل واحد منهما وإن كان ظاهرا، إلا أن أحدهما أقوى. إلا أنا نقول: أما الأول فباطل. لأن الدلائل اللفظية لا تكون قطعية، لأنها موقوفة على نقل اللغات، ونقل وجوه النحو والتصريف، وعلى عدم الاشتراك، والمجاز، والتخصيص، والإضمار، وعلى عدم المعارض النقلي والعقلي. وكل واحدة من هذه المقدمات مظنونة. والموقوف على المظنون أولى أن يكون مظنونا. فثبت: أن شيئا من الدلائل اللفظية لا يمكن أن يكون قطعيا. وأما الثاني: وهو أن يقال: أحد الظاهرين أقوى من الآخر، إلا أن على هذا التقدير يصير ترك أحد الظاهرين لتقرير الظاهر الثاني: مقدمة ظنية. والظنون لا يجوز التعويل عليها في المسائل العقلية القطعية. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن صرف اللفظ عن ظاهره إلى معناه المرجوح لا يجوز إلا عند قيام الدليل القاطع على أن ظاهره محال ممتنع. وإذا حصل هذا المعنى، فعند ذلك يجب على المكلف أن يقطع بأن مراد الله تعالى من اللفظ ليس ما أشعر به ظاهره. ثم عند هذا المقام: من جوز التأويل عدل إليه، ومن لم يجوزه فوض عليه إلى الله تعالى. وبالله التوفيق.
Chapter Three: The Way by Which One Knows Whether a Verse is Muhkam or Mutashabih. Know that this is a great place, because each one of the adherents of schools claims that the verses agreeing with his school are muhkam, and the verses agreeing with the opponent's school are mutashabih. The Mu'tazili says: His saying 'So whoever wills, let him believe; and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' is muhkam. And His saying 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' is mutashabih. And the Sunni reverses the case in this topic, and the examples are many. So there must be here a foundational principle (qanun asli) to refer to in this topic. We say: If the wording of a verse or report is apparent (zahir) in a meaning, we may abandon that apparent meaning only by a separate evidence (dalil munfasil), otherwise speech would cease to be beneficial, and the Qur'an would cease to be a proof. Then that separate evidence is either verbal (lafzi) or rational ('aqli). As for the first division, we say: this is only realized if there is a conflict between those two verbal evidences. And when conflict occurs between them, abandoning one to preserve the other is no more preferable than the opposite. Unless it is said: one of the two evidences is definitive (qati') and the other is apparent (zahir), and the definitive overrides the apparent. Or it is said: each one of them, although apparent, one of them is stronger. But we say: As for the first, it is false. Because verbal evidences cannot be definitive, for they depend on the transmission of languages, transmission of matters of grammar and morphology, and on absence of homonymy, metaphor, specification, ellipsis, and absence of conflicting transmitted and rational evidence. And every one of these premises is presumptive (maznun). And what is dependent on presumptive is more fitting to be presumptive. Thus it is established: none of the verbal evidences can be definitive. As for the second—that it is said: one of the two apparent (evidences) is stronger than the other—except that on this estimation, abandoning one apparent to establish the second becomes a presumptive premise. And presumptions cannot be relied upon in definitive rational matters. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: turning the wording from its apparent meaning to its preponderated meaning is not permissible except when there is a definitive evidence that its apparent meaning is impossible (muhal mumtani'). And when this meaning is obtained, then it is obligatory upon the legally responsible person (mukallaf) to be certain that the intended meaning of Allah from the wording is not what its apparent meaning indicates. Then at this juncture: whoever considers ta'wil permissible, he resorts to it; and whoever does not consider it permissible, he delegates (yufawwid) it to Allah Almighty. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument by Imam Razi on how to determine whether a Qur'anic verse is muhkam (clear) or mutashabih (ambiguous). He begins by noting the problem: each sect claims that verses supporting their view are muhkam and those opposing are mutashabih, citing the Mu'tazili and Sunni examples. To resolve this, he sets down a foundational principle (qanun asli): when the wording of a verse or report has an apparent (zahir) meaning, we cannot abandon that apparent meaning except by a separate evidence (dalil munfasil), otherwise speech would lose its benefit and the Qur'an would cease to be a proof. That separate evidence can be verbal (lafzi) or rational ('aqli). He then analyzes the case of conflict between two verbal evidences. If they conflict, abandoning one for the other is arbitrary unless one is definitive (qati') and the other apparent, or one is stronger. He refutes both possibilities: first, no verbal evidence can be definitive because it depends on numerous presumptive premises (transmission of language, grammar, morphology, absence of homonymy, metaphor, specification, ellipsis, and absence of conflicting evidence). Since each premise is only presumptive (maznun), the conclusion is also presumptive. Second, even if one apparent evidence is stronger, preferring it over another is a presumptive judgment, and presumptions cannot be relied upon in definitive rational matters. Therefore, the apparent wording can only be turned from its apparent meaning to a preponderated meaning when there is a definitive proof that the apparent meaning is impossible (muhal mumtani'). Once that is established, the mukallaf must be certain that Allah's intended meaning is not the apparent one. At that point, one either performs ta'wil (interpretation) or practices tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah). This argument establishes a strict rule for scriptural interpretation in kalam, emphasizing the primacy of definitive evidence over mere verbal preference.

Correction: Razi insists on an objective principle: the apparent meaning stands unless a separate evidence (not subjective preference) overrides it.

Checkpoint

A) The apparent meaning must always be followed without exception.
B) The apparent meaning can only be abandoned with a separate evidence.
C) The clearer verse outweighs the ambiguous verse.
D) The opinion of the majority determines the correct interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi establishes that the apparent meaning of a verse cannot be abandoned arbitrarily; it requires a separate evidence, either verbal or rational.

?

Will you commit to seeking a separate evidence before dismissing an uncomfortable verse?

Why Verbal Evidences Are Not Definitive
Razi refutes verbal definitiveness and gives the final criterion for interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that verbal evidences can never be definitive (qati') because they rely on presumptive premises, and how this leads to the final criterion for interpreting ambiguous verses.

Core Idea
Verbal evidences are presumptive, not definitive.

After establishing the foundational principle, Imam Razi examines the case of separate verbal evidence. He argues that verbal evidences can never reach the level of certainty because they depend on a chain of presumptive premises (like language, grammar, absence of metaphor, etc.). This means that only a definitive rational evidence can justify overturning the apparent meaning. In this lesson, we follow his critique and the final ruling: when the apparent meaning is proven impossible, one must be certain that Allah's intended meaning is other than the apparent, and then either engage in ta'wil or practice tafwid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage starting from 'As for the first division...'
Read carefully from 'As for the first division' to the end. Follow Razi's refutation of verbal definitiveness and his conclusion.
Pay attention to the list of presumptive premises.
2
Analyze Razi's refutation of verbal evidences being definitive
Identify the reasons why Razi claims verbal evidences cannot be definitive. Note the list of conditions that are only presumptive.
The argument is: each premise is presumptive → the conclusion is presumptive.
3
Understand the second possibility and its refutation
Razi also considers the case where one apparent evidence is stronger. Why does he reject this as insufficient for definitive matters?
Because preference would rely on a presumptive premise.
4
Grasp the final criterion and the two options (ta'wil/tafwid)
Razi concludes that the apparent meaning can only be abandoned when a definitive evidence shows it is impossible. Then one must be certain the apparent is not intended, and either interpret or delegate.

الفصل الثالث: الطريق الذي يعرف به كون الآية محكمة أو متشابهة. اعلم: أن هذا موضع عظيم وذلك لأن كل واحد من أصحاب المذاهب يدعي أن الآيات الموافقة لمذهبه: محكمة، والآيات الموافقة لمذهب الخصم: متشابهة. فالمعتزلي يقول: إن قوله: «فمن شاء فليؤمن، ومن شاء فليكفر»، محكم. وقوله: «وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله»، متشابه. والسنى يقلب القضية في هذا الباب والأمثلة كثيرة. فلا بد ههنا من قانون أصلي يرجع إليه في هذا الباب. فنقول: إذا كان لفظ الآية والخبر ظاهرا في معنى، فإنما يجوز لنا ترك ذلك الظاهر بدليل منفصل، وإلا لخرج الكلام عن أن يكون مفيدا، ولخرج القرآن من أن يكون حجة. ثم ذلك الدليل المنفصل إما أن يكون لفظيا أو عقليا. أما القسم الأول: فنقول هذا إنما يتم إذا حصل من ذينك الدليلين اللفظيين: تعارض. وإذا وقع التعارض بينهما، فليس ترك أحدهما لإبقاء الآخر أولى من العكس. اللهم إلا أن يقال: أحد الدليلين قاطع، والآخر ظاهر. والقاطع راجح على الظاهر. أو يقال: كل واحد منهما وإن كان ظاهرا، إلا أن أحدهما أقوى. إلا أنا نقول: أما الأول فباطل. لأن الدلائل اللفظية لا تكون قطعية، لأنها موقوفة على نقل اللغات، ونقل وجوه النحو والتصريف، وعلى عدم الاشتراك، والمجاز، والتخصيص، والإضمار، وعلى عدم المعارض النقلي والعقلي. وكل واحدة من هذه المقدمات مظنونة. والموقوف على المظنون أولى أن يكون مظنونا. فثبت: أن شيئا من الدلائل اللفظية لا يمكن أن يكون قطعيا. وأما الثاني: وهو أن يقال: أحد الظاهرين أقوى من الآخر، إلا أن على هذا التقدير يصير ترك أحد الظاهرين لتقرير الظاهر الثاني: مقدمة ظنية. والظنون لا يجوز التعويل عليها في المسائل العقلية القطعية. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن صرف اللفظ عن ظاهره إلى معناه المرجوح لا يجوز إلا عند قيام الدليل القاطع على أن ظاهره محال ممتنع. وإذا حصل هذا المعنى، فعند ذلك يجب على المكلف أن يقطع بأن مراد الله تعالى من اللفظ ليس ما أشعر به ظاهره. ثم عند هذا المقام: من جوز التأويل عدل إليه، ومن لم يجوزه فوض عليه إلى الله تعالى. وبالله التوفيق.
Chapter Three: The Way by Which One Knows Whether a Verse is Muhkam or Mutashabih. Know that this is a great place, because each one of the adherents of schools claims that the verses agreeing with his school are muhkam, and the verses agreeing with the opponent's school are mutashabih. The Mu'tazili says: His saying 'So whoever wills, let him believe; and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' is muhkam. And His saying 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' is mutashabih. And when conflict occurs between them, abandoning one to preserve the other is no more preferable than the opposite. Or it is said: each one of them, although apparent, one of them is stronger. But we say: As for the first, it is false.
