Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument by Imam Razi on how to determine whether a Qur'anic verse is muhkam (clear) or mutashabih (ambiguous). He begins by noting the problem: each sect claims that verses supporting their view are muhkam and those opposing are mutashabih, citing the Mu'tazili and Sunni examples. To resolve this, he sets down a foundational principle (qanun asli): when the wording of a verse or report has an apparent (zahir) meaning, we cannot abandon that apparent meaning except by a separate evidence (dalil munfasil), otherwise speech would lose its benefit and the Qur'an would cease to be a proof. That separate evidence can be verbal (lafzi) or rational ('aqli). He then analyzes the case of conflict between two verbal evidences. If they conflict, abandoning one for the other is arbitrary unless one is definitive (qati') and the other apparent, or one is stronger. He refutes both possibilities: first, no verbal evidence can be definitive because it depends on numerous presumptive premises (transmission of language, grammar, morphology, absence of homonymy, metaphor, specification, ellipsis, and absence of conflicting evidence). Since each premise is only presumptive (maznun), the conclusion is also presumptive. Second, even if one apparent evidence is stronger, preferring it over another is a presumptive judgment, and presumptions cannot be relied upon in definitive rational matters. Therefore, the apparent wording can only be turned from its apparent meaning to a preponderated meaning when there is a definitive proof that the apparent meaning is impossible (muhal mumtani'). Once that is established, the mukallaf must be certain that Allah's intended meaning is not the apparent one. At that point, one either performs ta'wil (interpretation) or practices tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah). This argument establishes a strict rule for scriptural interpretation in kalam, emphasizing the primacy of definitive evidence over mere verbal preference.

Textual Proofs Are Probable, Not Certain
Imam Razi argues that no scriptural text can be certain and that deviation from literal meaning requires a decisive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scriptural texts cannot serve as decisive evidence in theological reasoning and what condition justifies interpreting them non-literally.

Core Idea
No scriptural text can be certain; only a decisive rational proof can justify a non-literal reading.

Imam Razi addresses a fundamental methodological question: can we rely on the apparent meaning of scriptural texts when discussing divine attributes? He argues that all textual proofs involve probabilistic premises—such as whether specification, ellipsis, or counter-evidence exists—and therefore cannot yield certainty. Only when reason definitively proves that the literal meaning is impossible may we depart from it. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the probabilistic nature of textual premises
Read the passage and notice how Imam Razi lists common interpretive moves (takhsis, idmar, absence of counter-evidence) and labels each as probable (zanni). Ask yourself: why are these uncertain?
This is the core of his argument: if the building blocks are only probable, the conclusion cannot be certain.
2
Trace the argument that no textual proof is decisive
Imam Razi concludes that since each premise is probable, the whole textual proof cannot be certain. See how he reasons: 'that which depends on the probable is more deserving of being probable.'
mawquf 'ala al-maznun awla an yakuna maznuna.
3
Examine the second consideration: even stronger apparent meaning is still probable
Even if one apparent meaning is stronger, choosing it over another still involves a probabilistic judgment. So relying on it in decisive rational matters is invalid.
The second (al-thani) refers to the scenario where one literal meaning is stronger than another.
4
Learn the only valid condition for departing from literal meaning
Imam Razi states: only a decisive proof (dalil qati') that the literal meaning is impossible (muhal mumtani') justifies turning to a non-literal interpretation. This condition ensures that interpretation is not arbitrary.
This is the key takeaway: rational impossibility must be established.
5
Understand the two scholarly positions after that: ta'wil and tafwid
Once it is established that Allah's intended meaning is not the apparent one, scholars diverge: some perform interpretation (ta'wil) and others consign the meaning to Allah (tafwid). Both depend on the prior proof of impossibility.
Imam Razi does not here choose between ta'wil and tafwid; he presents both as acceptable responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتخصيص ، والإضمار ، وعلى عدم المعارض النقلى والعقلى. وكل واحدة هذه المقدمات . مظنونة . والموقوف على المظنون . أولى أن يكون من مظنونا . فثبت : أن شيئا من الدلائل اللفظية لا يمكن أن يكون قطعيا . وأما الثاني : وهو أن يقال : أحد الظاهرين أقوى من الآخر، إلا أن على هذا التقدير يصير ترك أحد الظاهرين لتقرير الظاهر الثاني : مقدمة ظنية . والظنون لا يجوز التعويل عليها فى المسائل العقلية القطعية . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن صرف اللفظ عن ظاهره إلى معناه المرجوح . لا يجوز إلا عند قيام الدليل القاطع على أن ظاهره محال ممتنع . وإذا حصل هذا المعنى ، فعند ذلك يجب على المكلاف أن يقطع بأن مراد الله تعالى من اللفظ ، ليس ما أشعر به ظاهره . ثم عند هذا المقام : من جوز التأويل عدل إليه ، ومن لم يجوزه، فوض عليه إلى الله تعالى ( وبالله التوفيق (۳) ).
(۳) سقط خ
And specification, and ellipsis, and the absence of a transmitted or rational counter-evidence. And every one of these premises is probable. And that which depends on the probable is more deserving of being probable. So it is established that none of the textual evidences can be certain. As for the second: that it is said: one of the two apparent meanings is stronger than the other, except that on this assumption, leaving one of the two apparent meanings in order to affirm the second apparent meaning becomes a probable premise. And probabilities cannot be relied upon in decisive rational matters. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that diverting a word from its apparent meaning to its less obvious meaning is not permissible except when there exists a decisive proof that its apparent meaning is impossible and absurd. And when this meaning is established, then at that point it is obligatory upon the morally responsible person to be certain that the intent of Allah Most High from the word is not what its apparent sense indicates. Then at this stage: whoever permits interpretation resorts to it, and whoever does not permit it consigns it to Allah Most High. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a proof that no scriptural text can be decisively certain in theological reasoning, and that departing from the literal meaning requires a decisive rational proof. The author begins by enumerating common interpretive premises—specification (takhsis), ellipsis (idmar), and the absence of counter-evidence—and notes that each of these is merely probable. Consequently, anything built on probable premises is itself probable. Therefore, no textual proof can be certain. He then addresses a second consideration: even if one apparent meaning is stronger than another, choosing to set aside one to uphold the other still relies on a probable premise. Since probabilities cannot support decisive rational conclusions, we cannot rely on such arguments. Hence, the rule is established: one may only divert a word from its apparent meaning if a decisive proof demonstrates that the literal sense is impossible. Once such a proof exists, a Muslim must affirm that Allah's intended meaning is not the obvious one. At that point, some scholars accept interpretation (ta'wil), while others consign the meaning to Allah (tafwid). The passage concludes with a prayer for divine success. This argument guards against the danger of treating probable textual arguments as certain in matters of divine attributes and other foundational creedal issues.

Misconception: A person might think that any strong scriptural evidence is decisive and can be used to settle theological disputes.
Correction: This passage clarifies that all textual proofs are probabilistic, not certain. Only a decisive rational proof can justify departing from the literal meaning.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
No scriptural text can provide certain knowledge in theology; only a decisive rational proof that the literal meaning is impossible justifies interpretation or consignment of meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Salaf's Position on Ambiguous Verses
Summarizes the Salaf's doctrine on mutashabihat and contrasts with Mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core of the Salaf's approach to ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashabihat) and how it differs from the Mutakallimun's method of allegorical interpretation.

Core Idea
The Salaf consign the meaning of ambiguous verses to Allah; the Mutakallimun interpret them.

Imam al-Razi presents the foundational divergence in Islamic theology regarding ambiguous verses (mutashabihat). This passage encapsulates the essence of the Salaf's position — a stance of restraint and delegation — and contrasts it with the rationalist approach of the Mutakallimun. Understanding this difference is key to navigating later debates on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read its literal translation. Note the key terms such as mutashabihat, tafwid, and ta'wil.
The passage is short but dense; take your time with each phrase.
2
Identify the three components of the Salaf's doctrine
The Salaf's position consists of three obligations: (1) certainty that the intended meaning is not the apparent one, (2) consigning the true meaning to Allah, and (3) refraining from delving into interpretation. Find each in the text.
These three steps are sequential and essential.
3
Contrast with the Mutakallimun's position
The passage then states the opposite view: the majority of the Mutakallimun oblige delving into interpretation. Notice the direct contrast signaled by 'rather' (bal).
This contrast sets up the author's subsequent arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في تقرير مذهب السلف حاصل هذا المذهب : أن هذه المتشابهات يجب القطع فيها بأن مراد الله تعالى منها شيء غير ظواهرها ، ثم يجب تفويض معناها إلى الله تعالى ، ولا يجوز الخوض فى تفسيرها . وقال جمهور المتكلمين : بل يجب الخوض في تأويل تلك المتشابهات
Chapter Four: On Presenting the Doctrine of the Salaf. The gist of this doctrine: that regarding these ambiguous verses, it is obligatory to be certain that the meaning of God Most High from them is something other than their apparent meanings, then it is obligatory to consign their meaning to God Most High, and it is not permissible to delve into their interpretation. And the majority of the Mutakallimun said: Rather, it is obligatory to delve into the interpretation of those ambiguous verses.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the fundamental divergence between the Salaf and the Mutakallimun on ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashabihat). It begins by stating the Salaf's position: one must be certain (al-qat‘) that God's intended meaning in such verses is not their apparent (zahir) meaning, then one must consign (tafwid) the true meaning to God, and it is forbidden to engage in interpreting them (al-khawd fi tafsiriha). In contrast, the majority of the Mutakallimun hold that it is obligatory to interpret them allegorically (ta’wil). This sets up the debate that the author will support with proofs.

Key Terms
متشابهات
محكم
مذهب السلف
تفويض
ظاهر
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Salaf's position is based on ignorance or avoidance of rational inquiry.
Correction: The Salaf's position is a deliberate methodological choice based on certainty that the apparent meaning is not intended, not a rejection of reason.

Checkpoint
What is the core difference between the Salaf and the Mutakallimun regarding mutashabihat?
A) The Salaf forbid all discussion, while the Mutakallimun permit discussion.
B) The Salaf consign the meaning to God and forbid interpretation, while the Mutakallimun oblige interpretation.
C) The Salaf deny the verses have meaning, while the Mutakallimun affirm their literal meanings.
D) The Salaf claim the verses are meaningless, while the Mutakallimun interpret them literally.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf consign the meaning of ambiguous verses to God and forbid interpretation, while the Mutakallimun oblige allegorical interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that describes Allah as 'settling on the Throne' or having a 'hand,' do you find yourself instinctively trying to interpret what that means, rather than simply trusting that Allah knows its true meaning? What would it cost you to practice tafwid right now?
?
Is there an area of your faith where you think you must understand everything to feel secure? Can you name one specific attribute of Allah that you struggle to accept without fully comprehending, and will you commit today to consign its meaning to Him?
?
The Salaf forbade delving into interpretation of ambiguous verses out of reverence and certainty. Is your own heart more inclined to intellectual control or humble submission? What would change if you treated every unclear verse as a direct command to trust, not to explain?

First Proof: The Verse on Interpretation
Presents the first proof of the Salaf from Quran 3:7 and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Salaf's first proof from Quran 3:7 for their methodology of stopping at the apparent meaning, and how they answer the objection that 'interpretation' refers only to eschatological knowledge.

Core Idea
The condemnation of seeking interpretation in Quran 3:7 proves it is impermissible to go beyond the apparent meaning.

The Salaf built their approach to ambiguous verses on a clear Quranic foundation. This lesson examines their first proof: the obligation to stop at Allah's statement that none knows the interpretation except Allah. We will see how they argue from the surrounding verses and then address an important objection that might limit the verse's scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: presentation of proof, sub-argument, objection, and answer.
Pay attention to the quotation of Quran 3:7 and the subsequent reasoning.
2
Identify the main claim
The Salaf claim that stopping (al-waqf) at the apparent meaning without interpretation is obligatory based on 'None knows its interpretation except Allah.' Understand why this implies stopping.
3
Examine the sub-argument
The preceding part of the verse condemns those who follow mutashabihat seeking interpretation. If interpretation were permissible, the condemnation would be unjust. This reinforces the obligation.
This is a logical extrapolation from the text.
4
Parse the objection
An objector suggests that 'interpretation' might refer only to specific hidden matters like the Hour, not to all ambiguous verses. Consider why this would weaken the proof if accepted.
The objection aims to limit the verse's application.
5
Understand the answer
The answer relies on the verse's division of the Book into muhkam and mutashabih. Reason shows this division applies generally. Thus, condemnation is general unless evidence specifies, and specifying without proof abandons the apparent meaning.
This refutes the objection by showing the verse's scope is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج السلف على صحة مذهبهم بوجوه : الأول : التمسك بوجوب الوقف على قوله تعالى : . وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله (۱). . والذي يدل على أن الوقف واجب وجوه : الأول : إن ما قبل هذه الآية يدل على أن طلب المتشابه مذموم حيث قال : « فأما الذين في قلوبهم زيغ ، فيتبعون ما تشابه منه ، ابتغاء الفتنة وابتغاء تأويله (۲) ، فلو كان طلب المتشابه جائزا ، لما ذم الله تعالى على ذلك فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد منه : طلب وقت قيام الساعة ، كما في قوله تعالى : « يسئلونك عن الساعة أيان مرساها ؟ قل : إنما علمها عند ربى (۳) ، ويحتمل أن يكون المراد منه : طلب العلم بمقادير الثواب والعقاب، وطلب الأوقات التى يظهر فيها الفتح والنصر . كما قالوا : « لو ما تأتينا بالملائكة (٤) . قيل : إنه تعالى لما قسم الكتاب إلى قسمين : محكم ومتشابه. و دل العقل على صحة هذه القسمة - من حيث إن حمل اللفظ على معناه الراجح هو المحكم ، وحمله على معناه الذي ليس راجحا : هو المتشابه .ثم إنه تعالى ذم طريقة من طلب تأويل المتشابه - كان تخصيص ذلك ببعض المتشابهات دون البعض : تركا للظاهر .
And the Salaf argued for the correctness of their doctrine by [several] proofs: The first: Holding fast to the obligation of stopping at His statement: 'And none knows its interpretation except Allah' (Aal-Imran 7). And what indicates that stopping is obligatory are [several] aspects: The first: That what precedes this verse indicates that seeking the ambiguous is blameworthy, for He said: 'As for those in whose hearts is deviation, they follow what is ambiguous of it, seeking sedition and seeking its interpretation' (Aal-Imran 7). So if seeking the ambiguous were permissible, God Most High would not have blamed [them] for that. If it is said: Why is it not possible that what is meant by it is seeking the time of the Hour's establishment, as in His statement: 'They ask you about the Hour: when is its arrival? Say: Its knowledge is only with my Lord' (Al-A'raf 187), and it is possible that what is meant by it is seeking knowledge of the amounts of reward and punishment, and seeking the times in which victory and triumph appear, as they said: 'Why do you not bring us angels' (Al-Hijr 7)? It is said: He Most High, when He divided the Book into two parts: decisive and ambiguous, and reason indicates the correctness of this division – in that carrying the word upon its preponderant meaning is the decisive, and carrying it upon its non-preponderant meaning is the ambiguous – then He Most High condemned the method of one who seeks the interpretation of the ambiguous – to specify that to some ambiguous verses without others would be an abandonment of the apparent.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is the first proof of the Salaf for their doctrine. It is structured as follows: The Salaf hold fast to the verse 'None knows its interpretation except Allah' and argue that stopping (al-waqf) at the literal meaning without interpretation is obligatory. To show this obligation, they present a sub-argument: the preceding part of the same verse condemns those who follow mutashabihat to seek interpretation, which would be pointless if interpretation were permissible. Then an objection is raised: the 'interpretation' condemned might refer to specific hidden matters like the Hour, not to all ambiguous verses. The answer refines the distinction: the verse divides the Book into muhkam (clear) and mutashabih (ambiguous). Reason shows the two categories: a word used in its preponderant meaning is muhkam; in a non-preponderant meaning it is mutashabih. Therefore, condemnation of seeking interpretation is general unless evidence specifies otherwise. To limit it to some cases without proof is to abandon the apparent meaning of the verse.

Key Terms
محكم
متشابه
وقت قيام الساعة
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the condemnation of seeking interpretation in Quran 3:7 only applies to eschatological knowledge like the Hour, not to all ambiguous verses.
Correction: The verse divides the entire Book into muhkam and mutashabih, and the condemnation is general unless evidence specifies. Therefore, it applies to all ambiguous verses.

Checkpoint
According to the Salaf's proof, why is seeking the interpretation of mutashabihat blameworthy?
A) Because the verse specifically condemns asking about the Hour.
B) Because the verse condemns those who follow mutashabihat to seek interpretation, and if it were permissible, the condemnation would be unjust.
C) Because the Mutakallimun forbid it.
D) Because all ambiguous verses are meaningless.

Key Takeaway
The condemnation of seeking interpretation in Quran 3:7 is general and applies to all ambiguous verses, not just eschatological knowledge, because the verse divides the Book into muhkam and mutashabih.

?

Second Proof: The Praise of the Firm in Knowledge
Second proof: praise of firm in knowledge supports tafwid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic praise of those firm in knowledge demonstrates that they do not know the detailed interpretation of ambiguous verses, but rather consign the meaning to God.

Core Idea
The firm in knowledge are praised for consigning meaning, not for knowing it.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the second proof of the Salaf: the Quranic praise of those firm in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-`ilm). He argues that these individuals are praised for saying 'We believe in it' regarding ambiguous verses, which would not be special if they knew the detailed interpretation. Their deep understanding is rooted in decisive rational proofs, leading them to confidently consign the true meaning to God alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: proof statement, Quranic evidence, reasoning, and conclusion.
The text uses 'فيعدون' which may be a variant of 'يعلمون' (know).
2
Identify the key components of the proof
Look for: (1) the Quranic verses cited, (2) the reasoning why detailed knowledge would negate extra praise, (3) the description of the firm in knowledge, (4) their action of consigning (tafwid) and certainty.
Focus on the logic: if they knew the meaning, they wouldn't be specially praised for believing.
3
Understand the role of decisive rational proofs
The passage states that the firm in knowledge know by decisive intellectual proofs that God knows all things, the Quran is His speech, and He does not speak falsehood. This allows them to recognize when a literal meaning is not intended.
This contrasts their knowledge with those who merely interpret literally.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal point
The conclusion is that the firm in knowledge are those who consign the meaning to God while being certain it is true. This tafwid is what earns them praise.
Common misunderstanding: they are not those who know the interpretation themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الله تعالى مدح الراسخين في العلم بأنهم يقولون: آمنا به (ه) وقال في سورة البقرة : د فأما الذين آمنوا فيعدون أنه الحق من ربهم (٦)) فهؤلاء الراسخون لو كانوا عالمين بتأويل ذلك المتشابه على التفصيل ، لما كان لهم في الإيمان به مزيد (۷) مدح . لأن كل من عرف شيئا على سبيل التفصيل آمن به . أما الراسخون فى العلم فهم الذين علموا بالدلائل القطعية العقلية أنه تعالى عالم بما لا نهاية له من المعلومات ، وعلموا أن القرآن كلام الله تعالى ، وعلموا أنه لا يتكلم بالباطل ولا بالعبث. فإذا سمعوا آية دلت القواطع على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون ظاهرها مرادا له تعالى ، بل مراد الله تعالى منها غير ذلك الظاهر . ثم فوضوا تعيين ذلك المراد إلى علمه ، وقطعوا بأن ذلك المعنى – أي معنى كان ـ ؟ هـ و الحق والصواب . فهؤلاء هم الراسخون في العلم . حيث ( يبعدون (۸)) أمثال هذه المتشابهات عن الإيمان و الجزم بصحة القرآن .
The second: That God Most High praised those firm in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-`ilm) because they say: 'We believe in it' (Aal-Imran 7) and He said in Surat al-Baqara: 'As for those who believe, they know that it is the truth from their Lord' (al-Baqara 26). So these firm in knowledge, if they were knowers of the interpretation of that ambiguous [verse] in detail, they would not have extra praise for believing in it. Because whoever knows a thing in detail believes in it. But the firm in knowledge are those who know by decisive intellectual proofs that He Most High is knowledgeable of infinite things, and they know that the Qur'an is the speech of God Most High, and they know that He does not speak falsehood or frivolity. So when they hear an ayat which definitive proofs indicate that its apparent meaning cannot be intended by Him Most High, but rather God Most High's intended meaning from it is other than that apparent meaning, then they consign the determination of that intended meaning to His knowledge, and they are certain that that meaning – whatever it may be – is the truth and correctness. So these are the firm in knowledge, because they distance [or: perceive] such ambiguous verses from faith and certitude in the soundness of the Qur'an.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage constitutes the second proof of the Salaf. It argues from the praise of the 'rasikhun fi al-`ilm' (those firm in knowledge) in the Quran. The reasoning: God praises them for saying 'We believe in it' regarding mutashabihat. If they actually knew the detailed interpretation, they would not receive special praise, since simply knowing something leads to belief naturally. Rather, their excellence lies in their profound theological knowledge: they know by rational proofs that God knows all things, that the Quran is His true speech, and that He does not speak in vain. Thus, when they encounter an apparently problematic verse, they are certain that the apparent meaning is not intended, and they consign the true meaning to God's knowledge, maintaining certainty that whatever that meaning is, it is true. This act of tafwid combined with conviction is what earns them praise. The phrase 'yub`idun' (they distance) indicates they set aside these verses from causing doubt in faith.

مدح
تفويض
دلائل قطعية عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The firm in knowledge are those who know the exact interpretation of ambiguous verses.
Correction: The passage argues they are praised for not knowing the detailed interpretation; they consign it to God while being certain of the Quran's truth.

Checkpoint
Why would the firm in knowledge not receive extra praise if they knew the detailed interpretation of mutashabihat?
A) Because they are already perfect.
B) Because anyone who knows a thing in detail naturally believes in it, so no special merit.
C) Because they are forbidden from knowing it.
D) Because the Mutakallimun already interpret them.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic praise of those firm in knowledge proves that tafwid (consigning meaning) is praiseworthy, not detailed interpretation.

?
The firm in knowledge are praised for their faith despite not knowing the details. Is your faith in Allah's words contingent on you understanding every detail, or is it rooted in the conviction that Allah does not speak falsehood? Will you now practice tafwid for a verse you struggle with, or will you continue seeking a human interpretation to feel in control?
?

More importantly, are you ready to humbly admit that you do not know the exact meaning of some verses, and to let that lack of knowledge deepen your trust in Allah rather than your frustration?

The Division of the Quran into Muhkam and Mutashabih
Imam Razi presents the Quranic division and infers a rule about ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the Quranic division into muhkam and mutashabih to argue against unrestricted ta'wil, and why reason confirms this division.

Core Idea
The Quran's own division into clear and ambiguous verses limits the scope of interpretive freedom.

Imam Razi begins his argument by citing the Quranic division of the Book into two categories: muhkam (clear) and mutashabih (ambiguous). He then shows that reason affirms this division by defining each term based on the preponderance of meaning. Finally, he points out that the Quran condemns those who seek ta'wil of ambiguous verses, implying that one cannot arbitrarily pick which ambiguous verses to interpret while ignoring others. This lesson unpacks that logical move.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the definitions of muhkam and mutashabih
Notice how the author defines each term based on the preponderance of the meaning of the word. This sets a rational criterion.
Preponderant meaning is the default or most common understanding.
3
Understand the implication of the blame
The author argues that God blamed those who seek ta'wil of some ambiguous verses but not others. Why is that an abandonment of the apparent meaning?
Think about consistency: if ta'wil is allowed for some, it should be allowed for all similarly ambiguous verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : إنه تعالى لما قسم الكتاب إلى قسمين : محكم ومتشابه. و دل العقل على صحة هذه القسمة - من حيث إن حمل اللفظ على معناه الراجح هو المحكم ، وحمله على معناه الذي ليس راجحا : هو المتشابه .ثم إنه تعالى ذم طريقة من طلب تأويل المتشابه به - كان تخصيص ذلك ببعض المتشابهات دون البعض : تركا للظاهر .
It is said: When God divided the Book into two parts: clear (muhkam) and ambiguous (mutashabih), and reason indicated the correctness of this division – in that carrying a word according to its preponderant meaning is the clear (muhkam), and carrying it according to its non-preponderant meaning is the ambiguous (mutashabih) – then God blamed the method of one who seeks the interpretation (ta'wil) of the ambiguous; such that specifying that to some ambiguous passages to the exclusion of others is an abandonment of the apparent meaning (al-zahir).
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument. It begins by stating that God divided the Quran into two categories: muhkam (clear, decisive) and mutashabih (ambiguous, similar). Reason confirms this division: when a word is taken in its preponderant (most likely) meaning, that is muhkam; when taken in a non-preponderant (less likely) meaning, that is mutashabih. Then it notes that God condemned the approach of those who seek ta'wil (interpretation) of the ambiguous. The point is: if one chooses to interpret some ambiguous verses while ignoring others, that is an arbitrary selection and abandons the apparent meaning (al-zahir) without justification. This undermines the consistency of the method that allows ta'wil for some verses but not others.

Key Terms
محكم
متشابه
تأويل
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division into muhkam and mutashabih is purely linguistic without rational basis.
Correction: Imam Razi asserts that reason confirms the division by defining each category based on preponderance of meaning, giving it a rational foundation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does carrying a word to its non-preponderant meaning produce?
A) The clear (muhkam)
B) The ambiguous (mutashabih)
C) The apparent (zahir)
D) The interpretation (ta'wil)

Key Takeaway
The Quran's own division into clear and ambiguous, confirmed by reason, shows that unrestricted ta'wil is inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an ambiguous verse in the Qur'an, do you immediately try to reinterpret it to fit your own understanding, or do you submit to its apparent meaning as established by the Salaf? What would it cost you to abandon your own interpretation and seek humility before the text?
?
The passage exposes inconsistency: those who permit ta'wil for some verses but not others. Is there any area in your beliefs where you are selective—accepting the apparent meaning in some cases but resorting to metaphorical interpretation in others, simply because it is more convenient? Will you repent from that and commit to consistency?
?
Allah has clearly divided the Qur'an into clear and ambiguous, and He condemns those who seek interpretations of the ambiguous. Right now, are you guilty of that condemnation? If you have ever tried to rationalize a problematic verse by forcing a meaning, will you now ask Allah for forgiveness and guidance to the straight path?

The Praise of the Rasikhun Proves They Do Not Know Ta'wil in Detail
Rāzī argues that the praise of the rasikhun shows they lack detailed ta'wil knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imām al-Rāzī uses the Qur'ānic praise of those firmly grounded in knowledge to argue that they do not possess detailed knowledge of the interpretation of ambiguous verses.

Core Idea
The praise of the rasikhun is based on their faith despite not knowing the specific ta'wil.

This lesson unpacks the second argument from Imām al-Rāzī's Asās al-Taqdīs. The author quotes the Qur'ānic praise of "those firmly grounded in knowledge" who say "we believe in it." He argues that if they knew the detailed interpretation, their belief would not be especially praiseworthy. Their merit lies in trusting God's knowledge while being certain of the truth of the Qur'ān.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise logical structure.
2
Identify the key claim
The author claims that if the rasikhun knew the ta'wil in detail, they would have no extra praise. Locate this premise and understand its implication.
This is the heart of the argument.
3
Examine the description of the rasikhun
Read the second part where the author describes what the rasikhun actually know: decisive rational proofs about God and the Qur'an. They entrust the specific meaning to God.
This shows their knowledge is of a different kind.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how this argument supports the Ash'arī view that ta'wil of mutashabihat is known only to Allah, and that the rasikhun are those who affirm without specifying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الله تعالى مدح الراسخين في العلم بأنهم يقولون: آمنا به وقال في سورة البقرة : فأما الذين آمنوا فيعلمون أنه الحق من ربهم فهؤلاء الراسخون لو كانوا عالمين بتأويل ذلك المتشابه على التفصيل ، لما كان لهم في الإيمان به مزيد مدح . لأن كل من عرف شيئا على سبيل التفصيل آمن به . أما الراسخون فى العلم فهم الذين علموا بالدلائل القطعية العقلية أنه تعالى عالم بما لا نهاية له من المعلومات ، وعلموا أن القرآن كلام الله تعالى ، وعلموا أنه لا يتكلم بالباطل ولا بالعبث. فإذا سمعوا آية دلت القواطع على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون ظاهرها مرادا له تعالى ، بل مراد الله تعالى منها غير ذلك الظاهر . ثم فوضوا تعيين ذلك المراد إلى علمه ، وقطعوا بأن ذلك المعنى – أي معنى كان – هو الحق والصواب . فهؤلاء هم الراسخون في العلم . حيث يؤمنون بأمثال هذه المتشابهات عن الإيمان و الجزم بصحة القرآن .
Second: God praised those firmly grounded in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-ilm) because they say: 'We believe in it.' And He said in Surat al-Baqarah: 'As for those who believe, they know that it is the truth from their Lord.' So these firmly grounded— if they were knowers of the interpretation (ta'wil) of that ambiguous (mutashabih) in detail, they would have no additional praise in believing in it. Because everyone who knows something in detail believes in it. Rather, those firmly grounded in knowledge are those who know through decisive rational proofs that He is Knower of infinite things, and that the Quran is the speech of God, and that He does not speak falsehood or frivolity. So when they hear a verse which decisive proofs indicate that its apparent meaning (zahir) cannot be intended by God, but God's intention is other than that apparent meaning, then they entrust the determination of that intended meaning to His knowledge, and they are certain that that meaning – whatever it is – is the truth and correctness. These are the ones firmly grounded in knowledge, who believe in such ambiguous passages through faith and certainty in the truth of the Quran.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument. It quotes the Quranic praise of the rasikhun (those firmly grounded in knowledge) who say 'we believe in it' (the mutashabih). The author argues that if they knew the ta'wil (detailed interpretation) of the ambiguous verses, their belief would not be especially praiseworthy, since anyone who knows something in detail naturally believes it. Their praise is due to their faith despite not knowing the specific interpretation. The author then describes their knowledge: they know through decisive rational proofs that God knows all, that the Quran is His speech, and that He does not speak in vain. When they encounter a verse whose apparent meaning contradicts decisive proofs, they entrust its true meaning to God and affirm that it is true, whichever meaning that may be. This demonstrates that their faith is based on certainty, not on detailed ta'wil.

تأويل
تفصيل

Correction: The praise of the rasikhūn would be meaningless if they already knew the ta'wil in detail, since knowing something leads to believing it necessarily. Their praise is based on their faith despite not knowing the specific interpretation.

C) Because ta'wil is forbidden
D) Because they already believe in the clear verses

Key Takeaway
The praise of the rasikhūn in the Qur'ān demonstrates that they do not know the detailed interpretation of ambiguous verses; their merit is in affirming faith without specifying the meaning.

6 min
Grammatical Refutation of Joining 'al-Rasikhun' to Allah
Imam Razi refutes the grammatical reading that makes 'wa al-rasikhun' conjoined to 'illa Allah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a grammatical analysis of the verse can decisively refute the reading that makes 'those firmly grounded in knowledge' equal to Allah in knowing the interpretation.

Core Idea
Grammar determines theology: the verse's flow forbids including al-rasikhun in the knowledge of ta'wil.

Imam Razi now turns to a purely linguistic argument. He examines the syntax of the verse and demonstrates that the reading favored by proponents of ta'wil leads to clumsy Arabic that requires unnecessary ellipses. This argument shows that even on grammatical grounds, the apparent meaning should be preserved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the author's main claim: if 'wa al-rasikhun' is conjoined to 'illa Allah', then 'yaquluna amanna bihi' becomes an independent clause, which is stylistically poor.
Notice that the author says 'it would have been more appropriate to say...' — he is judging the eloquence of the verse.
2
Examine the two proposed corrections
The objection suggests two ways to fix the problem: (1) by implying an ellipsis, (2) by making 'yaquluna' a circumstantial clause (hal) of al-rasikhun alone. Read the author's refutation of each.
The first correction is rejected because it adds unnecessary ellipsis; the second because it abandons the apparent meaning.
3
Understand the principle: avoiding ellipsis is preferable
The author concludes that the reading permitting ta'wil requires ellipsis, while the reading stopping at 'illa Allah' does not. Therefore, the latter is more appropriate. This ties the grammatical point to the theological issue.
This argument relies on a general principle of Quranic interpretation: the simpler reading (that avoids ellipsis) is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنه لو كان قوله تعالى والراسخون في العلم ، معطوفا على قوله إلا الله ، لصار قوله : يقولون آمنا به ابتداء ، وإنه بعيد عن الفصاحة . لأنه كان الأولى أن يقال : وهم يقولون آمنا به ، أو يقال ويقولون آمنا به . فإن قيل : في تصحيحه وجهان : الأول : أن يكون التقدير : هؤلاء القائلون بالتأويل ، ويقولون آمنا به . والثاني : أن يكون يقولون حالا من الراسخين قبل. أما الأول : فمدفوع . لأن تفسير كلام الله تعالى بما لا يحتاج معه إلى الإضمار ، أولى من تفسيره بما يحتاج معه إلى الإضمار . والثاني : ضعيف أيضا. لأن ذا الحال هو الذي تقدم ذكره، وههنا تقدم ذكر الله تعالى وذكر الراسخين في العلم ، فوجب أن يكون قوله : يقولون آمنا به حالا من الراسخين ، لا من الله تعالى . فيكون ذلك تركا للظاهر. من حيث إن الظاهر يقتضى أن يكون ذلك حالا عن كل من تقدم ذكره فثبت أن القول بجواز التأويل محوج إلى الإضمار فى هذه الآية ، والقول بعدم جوازه لا يحوج إليه . فكان أولى .
Third: If His saying 'and those firmly grounded in knowledge' were conjoined to His saying 'except Allah', then His saying 'they say: We believe in it' would become an independent clause, and that is far from eloquence. Because it would have been more appropriate to say 'and they say: We believe in it' or 'and they say: We believe in it'. If it is said: For its correction there are two aspects: First: the estimation is 'these are the ones who say the interpretation, and they say: We believe in it'. Second: that 'they say' is a circumstantial clause (hal) referring to 'the firmly grounded' prior. As for the first: it is rejected, because interpreting the speech of God without needing to supply an ellipsis is preferable to interpreting it with an ellipsis. The second is also weak, because the subject of the circumstantial clause is the one mentioned before, and here both God and the firmly grounded are mentioned before, so 'they say: We believe in it' must be a circumstantial clause of 'the firmly grounded', not of God. Thus that would be an abandonment of the apparent meaning, because the apparent meaning requires that it be a circumstantial clause for everyone mentioned before. So it is established that the statement permitting interpretation (ta'wil) necessitates supplying an ellipsis in this verse, while the statement not permitting it does not necessitate that. So it is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument, a grammatical refutation. The author considers the reading that makes 'wa al-rasikhun fi al-ilm' ('and those firmly grounded in knowledge') a conjunction (atf) to 'illa Allah' in the verse 'wa ma ya'lamu ta'wilahu illa Allah wa al-rasikhun fi al-ilm' (none knows its interpretation except Allah and those firmly grounded). If so, then the following phrase 'yaquluna amanna bihi' (they say: we believe in it) would be a new independent sentence, which is stylistically poor; better would be 'wa hum yaquluna' or 'wa yaquluna'. The author then addresses an objection: two possible corrections. First: an implied ellipsis ('those who say the interpretation and they say...'), but this is rejected because it adds an unnecessary ellipsis. Second: making 'yaquluna' a circumstantial clause (hal) referring back to 'al-rasikhun' alone, but this is also weak because the circumstantial clause normally attaches to the nearest antecedent, but here both God and al-rasikhun are mentioned, so the apparent meaning would attach it to both. Thus the reading that permits ta'wil requires ellipsis, while the reading that makes the stop at 'illa Allah' (so al-rasikhun do not know ta'wil) does not. Therefore the latter is preferable.

Key Terms
عطف
ابتداء
حال
إضمار
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The grammatical argument is trivial and has no bearing on the theological issue of ta'wil.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the grammatical structure directly affects which reading is more plausible; a reading that requires unnecessary ellipsis is weaker, thereby strengthening the position that only Allah knows the interpretation.

Checkpoint
What is the author's objection to the second proposed correction (making 'yaquluna' a hal of al-rasikhun only)?
A) It requires adding an ellipsis
B) The apparent meaning would make it a hal for both God and al-rasikhun
C) It violates the rules of Arabic grammar
D) It contradicts the Quranic verses

Key Takeaway
The grammatical argument demonstrates that the reading permitting ta'wil requires unnecessary ellipsis, while the reading stopping at 'illa Allah' is more natural and thus preferable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you honestly pay attention to its precise grammatical structure as a means to understand Allah's intended meaning? Or do you assume you already know what it says, and thus miss the depth of divine speech? How can you train yourself to be as meticulous as Imam Razi in seeking the most natural reading without adding your own ellipses?
?
The argument pivots on avoiding unnecessary additions (idmar) to Allah's words. In your own life, are there places where you have inserted your own interpretations or justifications into divine commands to make them fit your desires? Would you be willing to let the apparent meaning stand, even if it leaves you with uncomfortable demands?
?
Imam Razi chooses the reading that requires no ellipsis over one that does. What does this teach you about your own approach to uncertainty in faith? When you encounter an ambiguous verse (mutashabih), do you rush to impose a meaning, or do you humbly acknowledge that only Allah knows the full interpretation? Which attitude does this passage call you to adopt, and how will you implement it today?

Fourth Argument: 'Kullun min 'Indi Rabbina' and Objection on Waqf
Author gives fourth argument from the verse and an objection about the waqf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses the verse 'kullun min 'indi rabbina' (Al 'Imran: 7) to support the Salaf position that the rasikhun do not know the ta'wil of mutashabihat, and the objection based on the waqf reading.

Core Idea
The believers' confession 'all is from our Lord' proves they lack detailed ta'wil knowledge.

Imam al-Razi presents a key Qur'anic argument from Surah Al 'Imran verse 7. The verse reports that the firmly grounded in knowledge say 'we believe in it; all is from our Lord.' Al-Razi argues that this confession implies they do not know the specific interpretation of the ambiguous verses. He then anticipates an objection regarding the proper place to pause (waqf) in the verse. This lesson examines the argument and the beginning of that grammatical objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Note that the passage contains both an argument and the start of an objection.
The passage is incomplete; the answer to the objection is missing.
2
Understand the argument
Identify how Al-Razi interprets the phrase 'kullun min 'indi rabbina' as evidence that the rasikhun do not know the ta'wil in detail.
He says they believe in what they know and what they do not know in detail.
3
Grasp the logical implication
See his reasoning: if they knew the ta'wil, the statement 'but none knows its interpretation except Allah' would be pointless.
4
Examine the objection
Notice that the objection begins: the argument depends on proving that the waqf after 'illa Allah' is obligatory, and that 'al-rasikhun' is not conjuncted (i.e., not part of the exception). The answer is missing from this passage.
This objection is a grammatical argument about the reading of the verse.
5
Reflect on significance
Consider why this verse is central to the Salaf position and how the waqf reading affects interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : قوله تعالى : كل من عند ربنا يعني : أنهم آمنوا بما عرفوه على التفصيل وبما لم يعرفوا تفصيله وتأويله . إذ لو كانوا عالمين بالتفصيل في الكلام ، لم يبق لهذا الكلام فائدة . فهذا أجمل وجوه الاستدلال بهذه الآية في نصرة مذهب السلف . فإن قيل : إن هذا الاستدلال إنما يتم بإقامة الدليل على أن الوقف عند قوله تعالى : وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله واجب ، والعطف غير
Fourth: His saying 'all is from our Lord' means that they believed in what they knew in detail and what they did not know in detail and its interpretation. For if they were knowers of the detail in speech, no benefit would remain in this speech. So this is the most comprehensive of the ways of inference from this verse in support of the madhhab of the Salaf. If it is said: This inference is only completed by establishing the proof that the pause (waqf) at His saying 'and none knows its interpretation except Allah' is obligatory, and the conjunction is not...
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument and the beginning of an objection. The author quotes the verse 'kullun min 'indi rabbina' (all is from our Lord) and interprets it to mean that the believers affirm both what they know in detail and what they do not know in detail, including ta'wil. If they knew the detail of the speech (i.e., the specific interpretation), the statement 'but none knows its interpretation except Allah' would be pointless. Thus, this is a strong proof for the Salaf position (that the rasikhun do not know the ta'wil in detail). Then an objection begins: this argument depends on proving that the pause (waqf) after 'illa Allah' is obligatory, and that the conjunction (of 'al-rasikhun') is not (i.e., does not join to the exception). The text cuts off before the answer is given, but the objection is presented.

Key Terms

وقف
عطف
مذهب السلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the verse 'kullun min 'indi rabbina' supports the view that the rasikhun know ta'wil in detail because they affirm belief in all verses.
Correction: Al-Razi argues the opposite: their confession implies they do not know the ta'wil in detail, as they affirm both what they know and what they do not know.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'kullun min 'indi rabbina' imply about the rasikhun's knowledge of ta'wil?
A) They know all ta'wil in detail
B) They believe in what they know and do not know
C) They only believe in what they know
D) They reject the ambiguous verses

Key Takeaway
The verse 'kullun min 'indi rabbina' indicates that the rasikhun accept the mutashabihat without detailed ta'wil, supporting the Salaf doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a verse in the Quran that you do not fully understand, do you humbly say 'kullun min 'indi rabbina' and trust Allah's wisdom, or do you feel compelled to find a detailed interpretation even if it leads to speculation? What holds you back from complete submission?
?
Is there an Islamic teaching or a verse that you have struggled to accept because you cannot fully grasp its wisdom? Will you, beginning today, practice the belief that 'all is from our Lord' and stop insisting on a detailed explanation before you obey?
?
Think of a specific mutashabih verse that you have heard people argue about. Instead of seeking to resolve it by your own reasoning, can you write it down and sincerely say: 'I believe it is from my Lord, and I leave its true interpretation to Allah alone'? Will you do this now?

The Salaf's Second Proof: Consensus of the Companions
Razi invokes the consensus of the Sahaba and Tabi'un as evidence for the Salaf's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the consensus of the Companions and Followers as a proof that detailed interpretation of mutashabihat is impermissible.

Core Idea
The Companions' silence on ta'wil is a binding proof.

Imam Razi presents the second of three proofs for the Salaf's approach. He argues that because the Companions and Followers did not engage in detailed interpretation despite strong incentives, their collective silence constitutes a consensus that such interpretation is not allowed. This lesson examines that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, conditional, conclusion.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the key premise
Identify the two key facts: (1) many mutashabihat exist, (2) motivations to research them were abundant. Understand why these are important for the argument.
3
Trace the conditional reasoning
The proof uses a conditional: if detailed ta'wil were permissible, the Companions would have done it. Since they didn't, it must be impermissible. Think about why this reasoning is valid.
The conditional is based on the Companions being the most deserving of engaging in such interpretation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that delving into ta'wil is not permissible. Consider how this aligns with the Salaf's methodology of avoiding speculative interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جاز لأن هذا العطف قراءة مشهورة منقولة بالنقل المتواتر فإقامة الدليل على فساده طعن في النقل المتواتر وذلك لا يجوز قيل نحن لا نجعل هذه المسألة قطعية بل ظنية احتمالية وعلى هذا التقدير يزول السؤال الحجة الثانية على صحة مذهب السلف التمسك بإجماع الصحابة رضي الله عنهم أن هذه المتشابهات في القرآن والأخبار كثيرة والدواعي إلى البحث عنها والوقوف على حقائقها متوفرة فلو كان البحث عن تأويلها على سبيل التفصيل جائزا لكان أولى الخلق بذلك الصحابة والتابعون رضى الله عنهم ولو فعلوا ذلك لاشتهر ونقل بالتواتر وحيث لم ينقل عن واحد من الصحابة والتابعين الخوض فيها علمنا أن الخوض فيها غير جائز الحجة الثالثة إنا قد ذكرنا أن اللفظ المتشابه قسمان المجمل والمؤول أما المجمل فهو الذي يحتمل معنيين فصاعدا احتمالا على التسوية فنقول إنه إما أن يكون محتملا لمعنيين فقط أو لمعان أكثر من اثنين فإن كان محتملا لمعنيين فقط ثم دل الدليل على عدم أحدهما فحينئذ يتعين أن المراد هو الثاني مثل أن الفوق إما أن يراد به الفوق في الجهة أو في الرتبة ولما بطل حمله على الجهة تعينت الرتبة أما إذا كان اللفظ لمفهومات ثلاثة لم يلزم من بطلان واحد منها تعين الثاني والثالث بعينه ولا يمكن أيضا حمل اللفظ عليهما معا لما ثبت أن اللفظ المشترك لا يجوز استعماله في مفهوميه معا وأما المؤول فنقول اللفظ إذا كانت له حقيقة واحدة ثم دل الدليل على أنها غير مرادة وجب حمل اللفظ على مجازه ثم ذلك المجاز إن كان واحدا تعين صرف اللفظ إليه صونا عن التعطيل وإن لم يكن متعينا بقي اللفظ مترددا في تلك المجازات وحينئذ فذلك الكلام الذي ذكرناه في المجمل عائد ههنا بعينه فثبت بما ذكرنا أن تأويل المتشابه قد يكون معلوما وقد يكون مظنونا والقول بالظن غير جائز على ما سبق تقريره في باب أن التمسك بخبر الواحد في معرفة الله تعالى غير جائز فهذا هو جملة الكلام في تقرير مذهب السلف وأما المتكلمون القائلون بالتأويلات المفصلة فحجتهم ما تقدم من أن القرآن يجب أن يكون مفهوما ولا سبيل إليه في الآيات المتشابهة إلا بذكر التأويلات فكان المصير إليه واجبا والله أعلم
It is permissible. Because this conjunctive reading is a well-known recitation transmitted by mutawatir transmission, so establishing proof of its corruption is an attack on mutawatir transmission, and that is not permissible. It was said: We do not make this issue definitive, but rather speculative and probable. On this basis, the question is removed. The second proof for the correctness of the Salaf's school: Adherence to the consensus of the Companions - may Allah be pleased with them - that these mutashabihat in the Qur'an and narrations are many, and the motivations to research them and ascertain their realities are abundant. So if researching their ta'wil in detail were permissible, then the most deserving of creation for that would be the Companions and the Followers - may Allah be pleased with them - and if they had done that, it would have become widespread and transmitted by tawatur. Since it has not been transmitted from any of the Companions and Followers that they delved into them, we know that delving into them is impermissible. The third proof: We have mentioned that the mutashabih word is of two types: mujmal and mu'awwal. As for the mujmal, it is that which can bear two or more meanings with equal probability. We say: either it can bear only two meanings, or more than two. If it can bear only two meanings, and then evidence indicates the negation of one of them, then it is determined that the intended meaning is the second. For example, 'above' (al-fawq) either means above in direction or in rank. When its being taken in the sense of direction is invalidated, the rank is determined. As for if the word has three concepts, it does not follow from the invalidation of one of them that the second and third are specifically determined. Nor is it possible to apply the word to both of them together, because it has been established that a homonym cannot be used for both of its meanings simultaneously. As for the mu'awwal, we say: when a word has a single literal meaning, and then evidence indicates that it is not intended, it is obligatory to apply the word to its metaphorical meaning. Then if that metaphorical meaning is one, the word is necessarily directed to it, to protect against ta'til (negation of meaning). If it is not determinate, the word remains undecided among those metaphorical meanings. Then the same discussion we mentioned for the mujmal applies here identically. So it is established by what we have mentioned that ta'wil of the mutashabih may be known and may be speculative. And holding speculative opinion is impermissible, as previously established in the chapter that relying on a solitary narration in knowledge of Allah Almighty is impermissible. This is the summary of the discussion in establishing the madhhab of the Salaf. As for the mutakallimun who advocate detailed ta'wils, their proof is what has preceded, that the Qur'an must be understood, and there is no way to that regarding the mutashabihat except by mentioning ta'wils. So resorting to it was obligatory. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained argument for the Salaf's position on the mutashabihat (ambiguous verses) and concludes with the opposing view of the mutakallimun. The passage begins by concluding a previous point (likely the first proof) about a conjunctive reading being mutawatir and therefore not subject to critique; if it were merely speculative, the objection would disappear. Then Imam Razi presents three proofs. The second proof (the first in this selection) invokes the consensus of the Sahaba and Tabi'un: since these companions did not engage in detailed ta'wil despite strong motives, their silence indicates it is impermissible. The third proof is a detailed linguistic analysis. It classifies the mutashabih word into two types: mujmal (equivocal with equally probable meanings) and mu'awwal (having a single literal meaning but requiring metaphorical interpretation due to evidence). For mujmal, if it has exactly two meanings and one is negated by evidence, the other is determined. If it has more than two, negating one does not specify any remaining meaning, and using the word for multiple meanings simultaneously is prohibited. For mu'awwal, if evidence negates the literal meaning, the word must be taken metaphorically. If there is one clear metaphorical meaning, it is used; otherwise, the word remains undetermined, and the same reasoning as mujmal applies. This shows that ta'wil may be certain or speculative, but speculative ta'wil is impermissible, as established earlier regarding the use of ahad reports in knowledge of Allah. The passage then states that this is the summary of the Salaf's madhhab. Finally, it notes that the mutakallimun argue that understanding the Qur'an is obligatory and mutashabihat cannot be understood without ta'wil, so they consider it necessary. The text ends with 'Allah knows best'.

Key Terms
متشابه (mutashabih)
إجماع الصحابة (ijma' al-sahaba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Salaf had no method for interpreting mutashabihat and relied only on blind faith.
Correction: This proof shows that the Salaf had a rational method: they used consensus and historical transmission to determine the correct approach.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why does the lack of transmission from the Companions about delving into ta'wil indicate its impermissibility?
A) Because the Companions were forbidden from speaking about religious matters.
B) Because if it were permissible, they would have done it and it would have become widely known.
C) Because the mutashabihat are not important enough to discuss.
D) Because the Companions were incapable of understanding them.

Key Takeaway
The silence of the Companions on detailed ta'wil, despite strong incentives, proves that such interpretation is not permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the argument that the Companions' silence is binding, do you find yourself resisting it? Examine your heart: is there a part of you that wants to interpret ambiguous texts in your own way, even when the best of generations refrained? What does that reveal about your attitude toward divine revelation?
?
If you truly believed that the Companions' collective practice is a proof, how would it change the way you approach controversial theological questions today? Are you willing to submit your intellect to their precedent, even when you feel the urge to 'understand' everything?
?
The passage says the motive to understand was abundant, yet they didn't. When you encounter a difficult verse or hadith, what is your first instinct? Is it to accept and move on, or to demand an explanation? Does your reaction align with the Salaf's approach as described here?

The Salaf's Third Proof: Linguistic Classification of Mutashabihat
Razi classifies mutashabih words linguistically and shows the limits of ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi's linguistic analysis of mutashabih words (mujmal and mu'awwal) supports the Salaf's position on ta'wil.

Core Idea
Linguistic analysis shows that speculative ta'wil is often inconclusive and thus impermissible.

Imam Razi's third proof is a detailed linguistic argument. He classifies mutashabih words into two categories—mujmal (equivocal) and mu'awwal (requiring metaphorical interpretation)—and shows that in many cases, determining the intended meaning is not possible with certainty. This lesson unpacks that analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two categories
The passage begins by stating that the mutashabih word is of two types: mujmal and mu'awwal. Read the definitions and note the difference.
Mujmal has equally probable meanings; mu'awwal has a single literal meaning that is negated by evidence.
2
Analyze the mujmal case
Follow Razi's reasoning for mujmal words with two or more meanings. Understand why, if there are more than two meanings, negating one does not determine another.
The example given is the word 'above' (fawq) meaning either direction or rank.
3
Analyze the mu'awwal case
For mu'awwal, when the literal meaning is negated, the word must be taken metaphorically. Understand the condition for determination: if only one metaphorical meaning exists, it is used; otherwise, the word remains undetermined.
Note the principle of avoiding ta'til (negation of meaning).
4
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that ta'wil may be certain or speculative, but speculative ta'wil is impermissible, as established earlier. Reflect on how this supports the Salaf's avoidance of detailed interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جاز لأن هذا العطف قراءة مشهورة منقولة بالنقل المتواتر فإقامة الدليل على فساده طعن في النقل المتواتر وذلك لا يجوز قيل نحن لا نجعل هذه المسألة قطعية بل ظنية احتمالية وعلى هذا التقدير يزول السؤال الحجة الثانية على صحة مذهب السلف التمسك بإجماع الصحابة رضي الله عنهم أن هذه المتشابهات في القرآن والأخبار كثيرة والدواعي إلى البحث عنها والوقوف على حقائقها متوفرة فلو كان البحث عن تأويلها على سبيل التفصيل جائزا لكان أولى الخلق بذلك الصحابة والتابعون رضى الله عنهم ولو فعلوا ذلك لاشتهر ونقل بالتواتر وحيث لم ينقل عن واحد من الصحابة والتابعين الخوض فيها علمنا أن الخوض فيها غير جائز الحجة الثالثة إنا قد ذكرنا أن اللفظ المتشابه قسمان المجمل والمؤول أما المجمل فهو الذي يحتمل معنيين فصاعدا احتمالا على التسوية فنقول إنه إما أن يكون محتملا لمعنيين فقط أو لمعان أكثر من اثنين فإن كان محتملا لمعنيين فقط ثم دل الدليل على عدم أحدهما فحينئذ يتعين أن المراد هو الثاني مثل أن الفوق إما أن يراد به الفوق في الجهة أو في الرتبة ولما بطل حمله على الجهة تعينت الرتبة أما إذا كان اللفظ لمفهومات ثلاثة لم يلزم من بطلان واحد منها تعين الثاني والثالث بعينه ولا يمكن أيضا حمل اللفظ عليهما معا لما ثبت أن اللفظ المشترك لا يجوز استعماله في مفهوميه معا وأما المؤول فنقول اللفظ إذا كانت له حقيقة واحدة ثم دل الدليل على أنها غير مرادة وجب حمل اللفظ على مجازه ثم ذلك المجاز إن كان واحدا تعين صرف اللفظ إليه صونا عن التعطيل وإن لم يكن متعينا بقي اللفظ مترددا في تلك المجازات وحينئذ فذلك الكلام الذي ذكرناه في المجمل عائد ههنا بعينه فثبت بما ذكرنا أن تأويل المتشابه قد يكون معلوما وقد يكون مظنونا والقول بالظن غير جائز على ما سبق تقريره في باب أن التمسك بخبر الواحد في معرفة الله تعالى غير جائز فهذا هو جملة الكلام في تقرير مذهب السلف وأما المتكلمون القائلون بالتأويلات المفصلة فحجتهم ما تقدم من أن القرآن يجب أن يكون مفهوما ولا سبيل إليه في الآيات المتشابهة إلا بذكر التأويلات فكان المصير إليه واجبا والله أعلم
It is permissible. Because this conjunctive reading is a well-known recitation transmitted by mutawatir transmission, so establishing proof of its corruption is an attack on mutawatir transmission, and that is not permissible. It was said: We do not make this issue definitive, but rather speculative and probable. On this basis, the question is removed. The second proof for the correctness of the Salaf's school: Adherence to the consensus of the Companions - may Allah be pleased with them - that these mutashabihat in the Qur'an and narrations are many, and the motivations to research them and ascertain their realities are abundant. So if researching their ta'wil in detail were permissible, then the most deserving of creation for that would be the Companions and the Followers - may Allah be pleased with them - and if they had done that, it would have become widespread and transmitted by tawatur. Since it has not been transmitted from any of the Companions and Followers that they delved into them, we know that delving into them is impermissible. The third proof: We have mentioned that the mutashabih word is of two types: mujmal and mu'awwal. As for the mujmal, it is that which can bear two or more meanings with equal probability. We say: either it can bear only two meanings, or more than two. If it can bear only two meanings, and then evidence indicates the negation of one of them, then it is determined that the intended meaning is the second. For example, 'above' (al-fawq) either means above in direction or in rank. When its being taken in the sense of direction is invalidated, the rank is determined. As for if the word has three concepts, it does not follow from the invalidation of one of them that the second and third are specifically determined. Nor is it possible to apply the word to both of them together, because it has been established that a homonym cannot be used for both of its meanings simultaneously. As for the mu'awwal, we say: when a word has a single literal meaning, and then evidence indicates that it is not intended, it is obligatory to apply the word to its metaphorical meaning. Then if that metaphorical meaning is one, the word is necessarily directed to it, to protect against ta'til (negation of meaning). If it is not determinate, the word remains undecided among those metaphorical meanings. Then the same discussion we mentioned for the mujmal applies here identically. So it is established by what we have mentioned that ta'wil of the mutashabih may be known and may be speculative. And holding speculative opinion is impermissible, as previously established in the chapter that relying on a solitary narration in knowledge of Allah Almighty is impermissible. This is the summary of the discussion in establishing the madhhab of the Salaf. As for the mutakallimun who advocate detailed ta'wils, their proof is what has preceded, that the Qur'an must be understood, and there is no way to that regarding the mutashabihat except by mentioning ta'wils. So resorting to it was obligatory. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained argument for the Salaf's position on the mutashabihat (ambiguous verses) and concludes with the opposing view of the mutakallimun. The passage begins by concluding a previous point (likely the first proof) about a conjunctive reading being mutawatir and therefore not subject to critique; if it were merely speculative, the objection would disappear. Then Imam Razi presents three proofs. The second proof (the first in this selection) invokes the consensus of the Sahaba and Tabi'un: since these companions did not engage in detailed ta'wil despite strong motives, their silence indicates it is impermissible. The third proof is a detailed linguistic analysis. It classifies the mutashabih word into two types: mujmal (equivocal with equally probable meanings) and mu'awwal (having a single literal meaning but requiring metaphorical interpretation due to evidence). For mujmal, if it has exactly two meanings and one is negated by evidence, the other is determined. If it has more than two, negating one does not specify any remaining meaning, and using the word for multiple meanings simultaneously is prohibited. For mu'awwal, if evidence negates the literal meaning, the word must be taken metaphorically. If there is one clear metaphorical meaning, it is used; otherwise, the word remains undetermined, and the same reasoning as mujmal applies. This shows that ta'wil may be certain or speculative, but speculative ta'wil is impermissible, as established earlier regarding the use of ahad reports in knowledge of Allah. The passage then states that this is the summary of the Salaf's madhhab. Finally, it notes that the mutakallimun argue that understanding the Qur'an is obligatory and mutashabihat cannot be understood without ta'wil, so they consider it necessary. The text ends with 'Allah knows best'.

Key Terms
متشابه (mutashabih)
مجمل (mujmal)
مؤول (mu'awwal)
تأويل (ta'wil)
تعطيل (ta'til)
مجاز (majaz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any ta'wil is acceptable as long as it avoids anthropomorphism.
Correction: Razi shows that ta'wil is only valid when the intended meaning is certain; speculative ta'wil is impermissible, even if well-intentioned.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what happens if a mujmal word can bear three meanings and evidence negates one of them?
A) The word is applied to the remaining two meanings simultaneously.
B) The word is taken in its literal sense despite the evidence.
C) The intended meaning remains undetermined among the remaining two.
D) One of the remaining meanings is chosen by consensus.

Key Takeaway
Linguistic analysis reveals that many mutashabih words cannot yield certain interpretation, making speculative ta'wil impermissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about Allah's attributes, do you feel an inner pressure to 'explain' them fully? How does Razi's linguistic proof expose the danger of that impulse, and what would it look like for you to surrender that need for certainty today?
?
In what areas of your life—beyond theology—do you settle for speculation when clear evidence from revelation is available? Will you commit to seeking the clear teachings instead of comfortable guesses?
?
The Salaf refrained from speculative interpretation out of awe and fear of misrepresenting Allah. Do you share that fear? Or have you become comfortable with your own opinions about the unseen? What is one specific step you can take to cultivate that reverence in your daily reading of the Quran?

The Mutakallimun's Objection and Conclusion
Razi presents the mutakallimun's argument for detailed ta'wil and concludes the discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument for detailed ta'wil and how it contrasts with the Salaf's position.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun argue that understanding the Qur'an necessitates ta'wil, but the Salaf respond that certainty is required.

After presenting the Salaf's proofs, Imam Razi briefly states the opposing view of the mutakallimun, who advocate detailed ta'wil. Their core argument is that the Qur'an must be understood, and that requires interpretation of ambiguous verses. This lesson examines that counterargument and places it in context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the mutakallimun's premise
The passage states: the Qur'an must be understood. Identify this as the key premise. Note that it is presented as an obligation.
2
Understand their conclusion
From the premise, they conclude that ta'wil is necessary for the mutashabihat. Consider why they think this is the only way.
The conclusion is that resorting to ta'wil is obligatory.
3
Contrast with the Salaf's position
Recall the Salaf's proofs. How would the Salaf respond to the claim that the Qur'an must be understood? Think about the earlier linguistic analysis that showed many ta'wils are speculative and thus impermissible.
The Salaf might argue that understanding the Qur'an does not require detailed ta'wil; rather, affirming the texts without modality suffices.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'Allah knows best'
The passage ends with 'Allah knows best.' Consider how this pious conclusion fits with the overall tone of the discussion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a verse of the Quran that you do not fully grasp, is your first impulse to search for an interpretation that satisfies your intellect, or do you find peace in simply believing it as it is, trusting that Allah knows best? Which impulse is stronger in you right now?
?
The mutakallimun argued that understanding requires ta'wil. But what if true understanding is to admit your ignorance and submit to Allah's words without forcing your own meaning? Is your heart willing to remain unanswered for some matters for the sake of His pleasure?
?
Reflect on the last time you discussed an ambiguous verse. Were you more concerned with being right or with being safe from speaking about Allah without knowledge? How would your life change if you treated every mutashabih verse as a test of your submission rather than a puzzle to solve?
