No Replacing Ambiguous Words
Razi forbids replacing ambiguous words and justifies it with precaution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Salaf forbade replacing one ambiguous word with another, and the precautionary principle behind this ruling.

Core Idea
Replacing ambiguous words risks increasing false beliefs, so caution demands abstaining.

Razi now moves from general principles to specific branches of the Salaf's methodology. The first branch addresses a common temptation: to replace a difficult ambiguous word with another that seems clearer. But Razi warns that this is not allowed, because ambiguity varies, and without need, increased illusion is forbidden. He supports this with an analogy from Islamic legal precaution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: ruling, reason, difficulty, precaution, and an analogy.
Pay attention to the term 'iham' (illusion/false belief).
2
Identify the central ruling
Find the statement 'لا يجوز تبديل لفظ من الألفاظ المتشابهة بلفظ آخر متشابه'. This is the main ruling: it is not permissible to replace one ambiguous word with another.
3
Understand the rationale
Razi gives the reason: some words cause more false illusion (iham) than others. Increasing illusion without need is forbidden. Then he notes that distinguishing levels of illusion is difficult, so the cautious path is to abstain from all replacements.
This is an application of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to harm).
4
Analyze the analogy
Razi compares this to the waiting period (iddah) in Islamic law. Even though it is hard, it is required to avoid the greater risk of mixing lineages. Similarly, the risk in matters of Allah's essence and attributes is far greater, so caution is even more warranted.
This is a qiyas (analogy) to show the principle of preferring the lesser harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في تفاريع مذهب السلف. الأول: إنه لا يجوز تبديل لفظ من الألفاظ المتشابهة بلفظ آخر متشابه، سواء كان بالعربية أو بالفارسية. وذلك لأن الألفاظ المتشابهة قد يكون بعضها أكثر إيهاما للباطل من البعض. والزيادة في الإيهام من غير حاجة إليها لا يجوز. بلى، قد تكون زيادة الإبهام حاصلة في اللفظين، إلا أن التمييز بين هذا القسم والقسم الأول فيه عسر. فالاحتياط: الامتناع من الكل. ألا ترى أن الشرع أوجب العدة على الموطوءة، لبراءة الرحم، احتياطا لحكم النسب، ثم قالوا: تجب العدة على العقيم، والآيسة، وعند العزل، لأن بواطن الأرحام لا يعلمها إلا علام الغيوب؟ فإيجاب العدة أهون من ركوب الخطر. إلا أن الخطر في معرفة ذات الله تعالى وصفاته أعظم من الخطر في العدة. فإذا راعينا الاحتياط به، فلأن نراعيه ههنا أولى.
The fifth chapter on the ramifications of the doctrine of the Salaf. The first: it is not permissible to replace a word from among the ambiguous words with another ambiguous word, whether it be in Arabic or Persian. That is because some ambiguous words may cause more illusion of falsehood than others. And an increase in illusion without need is not permissible. Rather, an increase in ambiguity may occur in both words, but distinguishing between this category and the first category is difficult. So caution: abstaining from all. Do you not see that the Law obligated the waiting period for the woman who is cohabited with, to ensure the purity of the womb, as a precaution for the ruling of lineage? Then they said: the waiting period is obligatory on the barren woman, the post-menopausal woman, and in the case of coitus interruptus, because the inner parts of the wombs are not known except by the Knower of the Unseen. So imposing the waiting period is easier than risking danger. However, the danger in knowing the essence of Allah the Exalted and His attributes is greater than the danger in the waiting period. So if we observe precaution in that, then it is more fitting to observe it here.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is the first branch (far') of the Salaf's doctrine regarding mutashabihat. Razi rules that it is not permissible to replace one ambiguous word with another ambiguous word, whether in Arabic or Persian (or any language). The reason is that some ambiguous words may convey a greater false illusion (iham) than others. If there is no need, increasing that illusion is forbidden. However, it is possible that both words increase ambiguity, but distinguishing between cases is difficult. Therefore, the cautious approach is to abstain from all replacements. Razi supports this with an analogy from Islamic law: the 'iddah (waiting period) is required for a woman who is cohabited with to ensure the womb is free (for establishing lineage), and jurists extended it to barren, post-menopausal women, and cases of coitus interruptus because the inner state of the womb is unknown except to Allah. Even though imposing 'iddah is a hardship, it is lesser than the risk of mixing lineages. Similarly, the risk in matters of Allah's essence and attributes is far greater, so caution is even more warranted here. The argument uses qiyas (analogy) and the principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to harm).

Key Terms
mutashabihat
tabdil
iham
ihtiyat
iddah
sadd al-dhara'i

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that replacing an ambiguous word with another that is clearer or less ambiguous is permissible and even beneficial.
Correction: Razi forbids it because even if the new word seems clearer, it may still cause illusion, and distinguishing safe replacements is difficult. The cautious principle (ihtiyat) demands abstaining from all replacements to avoid increasing false belief.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Razi gives for forbidding replacement of an ambiguous word with another ambiguous word?
A) It leads to innovation in religion
B) Some ambiguous words cause more illusion of falsehood, and increasing illusion without need is forbidden
C) The Salaf did not do it
D) It makes the Quran unclear

Key Takeaway
To avoid increasing false beliefs about Allah, the Salaf forbade replacing ambiguous words with other ambiguous words, applying the principle of precaution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you discuss Allah's attributes with others, do you ever use words that might create wrong impressions just to sound knowledgeable? How will you guard your speech after learning Razi's warning?
?
The precaution in legal matters is lesser than in theology. In your daily life, are you as cautious about what you say about Allah as you are about mundane legalities? What change must you make?
?
Razi says distinguishing between ambiguous words is difficult, so better to abstain entirely. Are there areas in your faith where you try to 'clarify' things beyond the revealed texts? What does your desire to replace words reveal about your trust in Allah's wording?

The Prohibition of Combining Mutashabihat
Al-Razi states the third branch: prohibition of combining mutashabihat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi prohibits combining multiple mutashabihat and the linguistic reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Combining ambiguous texts can mislead by suggesting their literal meaning is intended.

This lesson examines the third methodological ruling from al-Razi regarding mutashabihat. He argues that gathering multiple ambiguous verses or hadiths together creates a false impression that their apparent, literal meanings are intended. This is because ordinary speech is predominantly literal, so a cluster of ambiguous expressions carries undue weight. Understanding this principle helps safeguard against anthropomorphism and other false beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Pay attention to the concise style and logical flow.
Notice the cause-and-effect structure: premise → reasoning → conclusion.
2
Identify the key components
Find the ruling (prohibition), the reason (induction shows most speech is literal), and the consequence (combination gives impression of literalism).
The key terms are جمع (combination), الألفاظ المتشابهة (ambiguous words), and الحقيقة (literal speech).
3
Understand the linguistic premise
Reflect on the idea that uttering one or two ambiguous expressions can be interpreted metaphorically. Why does adding more change the interpretation?
Al-Razi relies on induction (الاستقراء) to claim that literal speech is the norm.
4
Consider the practical implication
Think about how this ruling applies to studying or reciting Quran and hadith. How can we avoid causing misunderstanding?
This principle is meant for scholars and teachers to prevent reinforcing false beliefs in listeners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثالث : إنه لا يجوز جمع الألفاظ المتشابهة . وذلك لأن التلفظ باللفظ الواحد أو باللفظين ، قد يحمل على المجاز . لأن الاستقراء دل على أن الغالب على الكلام : التكلم بالحقيقة . فإذا جمعنا الألفاظ المتشابهة ورويناها دفعة واحدة ، أوهمت كثرتها : أن المراد منها ظواهرها . فكان ذلك الجمع سببا لإيهام زيادة الباطل . وإنه لا يجوز .
The third branch: It is not permissible to combine the ambiguous words. That is because uttering a single word or two words may be interpreted metaphorically. Because induction has shown that the predominant mode of speech is speaking literally. So if we combine the ambiguous words and narrate them all at once, their abundance gives the impression that their apparent meanings are intended. Thus that combination becomes a cause for suggesting an increase of falsehood. And it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the third ruling regarding mutashabihat. Al-Razi states that it is forbidden to combine multiple ambiguous verses or hadiths together. The reasoning is grounded in the nature of language. Normally, when a single expression is used, it can be taken metaphorically. But when multiple such expressions are brought together, the weight of their number makes the literal interpretation seem more likely. Induction (istiqra) shows that most speech is literal. Therefore, reciting a series of mutashabihat together gives the false impression that their apparent (and often anthropomorphic) meanings are intended. This combination becomes a means of reinforcing falsehood, so it is prohibited.

Key Terms
جمع
الألفاظ المتشابهة
المجاز
الحقيقة
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reciting many mutashabihat together strengthens faith because it shows the Quran's consistency.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that combining them can mislead people into thinking the literal (often anthropomorphic) meaning is intended, thereby reinforcing false belief.

Checkpoint
What is the reasoning behind forbidding the combination of mutashabihat?
A) Combining them is considered disrespectful.
B) It leads to the assumption that their apparent meanings are correct.
C) The Quran discourages reading multiple verses at once.
D) It confuses the listener.

Key Takeaway
Combining multiple mutashabihat is prohibited because their abundance creates a false impression that the literal meaning is intended, thereby reinforcing falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear multiple ambiguous Quranic verses recited together about Allah's attributes, do you feel a pull to imagine Him in human terms? How will you train your heart to reject that impression and affirm tanzih instead?
?
Have you ever used a collection of hadiths or verses to convince yourself or others of a belief that you now suspect might be based on literalism? What step will you take today to seek the true interpretation through tawil?
?
In your daily speech, do you sometimes repeat weak arguments to make them sound convincing? How can you apply al-Razi's warning to avoid creating false impressions through sheer repetition, even about religious matters?

The Qur'anic Method for Mutashabihat: Every Ambiguous Verse Has a Qarina
Al-Razi explains that every mutashabih has a qarina removing false understanding, and thus the method is ta'wil with adab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's principle that every mutashabih verse comes with an internal indicator (qarina) that prevents literal misunderstanding, and how this necessitates respectful ta'wil.

Core Idea
Every ambiguous Quranic verse carries its own key to correct interpretation.

Al-Razi concludes his discussion on mutashabihat by revealing a crucial observation: Allah never leaves a potentially misleading verse without a contextual clue. In this passage, he demonstrates through two key examples how the Qur'an itself resolves apparent anthropomorphism. This lesson unpacks that principle and the method of ta'wil that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source once without overanalyzing, then read the literal translation. Let Al-Razi's argument flow naturally.
Pay attention to the structure: principle, example 1, example 2, conclusion.
2
Identify the central principle
Al-Razi states that Allah does not mention any mutashabih without a qarina. What is a qarina? It is a contextual indicator that removes false illusion.
This principle is the key to understanding the entire discussion.
3
Examine the two Quranic examples
First, the 'light' verse: the qarina is the phrase 'like His light' (مثل نوره), which shows light is added to Allah, not identical to Him. Second, the 'throne' verse: the qarina includes the surrounding verses that establish the Throne as created.
Notice how each qarina refutes the literal anthropomorphic interpretation.
4
Learn the conclusion: Ta'wil with Adab
Al-Razi concludes that the correct method is ta'wil (interpretation) of the ambiguous words, while maintaining respect (adab) for the Necessary Existent.
Ta'wil here means interpreting in a way that befits Allah's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن الله تعالى لم يذكر لفظة المتشابهات ، إلا وقرن بها قرينة تدل على زوال الوهم الباطل . مثاله : أنه تعالى لما قال : « الله نور السموات والأرض » ذكر بعده مثل نوره ، فأضاف النور إلى نفسه. ولو كان هو تعالى نفس النور ، لما أضاف النور إلى نفسه . لأن إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه ممتنعة . ولما قال تعالى : « الرحمن على العرش استوى » ذكر قبله تنزيلا ممن خلق الأرض والسموات العلى ، وذكر بعده قوله : « له ما في السموات وما في الأرض وما بينهما وما تحت الثرى » ، وقد ذكرنا : أن هاتين الآيتين تدلان على أن كل ما كان مختصا بجهة فوق : مخلوق محدث. فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الطريق فى هذه المتشابهات هو التأويل في تلك الألفاظ تأدبا في حق واجب الوجود . وبالله التوفيق .
And know: that Allah, exalted, does not mention any word of the mutashabihat without coupling it with a qarina (indication) that removes the false illusion. Example: when He said: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth', He mentioned after it 'the likeness of His light', so He added light to Himself. And if He were Himself the light, He would not have added light to Himself, because adding a thing to itself is impossible. And when He said: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne', He mentioned before it a sending down from Him who created the earth and the high heavens, and mentioned after it His saying: 'To Him belongs what is in the heavens and what is in the earth and what is between them and what is under the soil'. And we have mentioned: that these two verses indicate that everything specific to a direction of above is a created originated thing. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the method regarding these mutashabihat is ta'wil (interpretation) of those words, with respect in the case of the Necessary Existent. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on mutashabihat. Al-Razi first establishes a general observation: whenever Allah mentions a word that could be misunderstood, He accompanies it with a qarina (contextual indicator) that removes the false illusion. Two examples are given. First: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' – the qarina is that immediately after, Allah says 'the likeness of His light', adding light to Himself. If He were literally light, He would not add it to Himself because adding a thing to itself is impossible (since it would imply duality). Second: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' – the qarina includes the preceding verse about creation of the heavens and the earth (implying that the Throne is a created thing) and the following verse stating that to Him belongs everything in the heavens and earth, indicating His dominion over all. These verses together show that anything associated with a spatial direction (like the Throne) is created and originated. Therefore, the correct method for dealing with mutashabihat is ta'wil (interpretation) that preserves respect (adab) for the Necessary Existent. This is the divinely guided way.

Key Terms
قرينة
مثل نوره
التأويل
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think mutashabihat can be taken literally without context, but Allah always provides a qarina to prevent that.
Correction: The passage shows that every mutashabih has a qarina that removes false illusion, so literal interpretation is not valid.

Checkpoint
What is the qarina that prevents taking the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' literally?
A) The word 'light' is defined elsewhere.
B) The phrase 'the likeness of His light' shows it is not Him.
C) The verse is metaphorical by nature.
D) The context is about guidance.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an itself provides internal clues to interpret ambiguous verses, requiring us to use ta'wil with respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that seems to describe Allah in human terms, do you immediately accept it literally or pause to seek the qarina that removes the false understanding? What will you change in your approach from now on?
?
Al-Razi says that every ambiguous verse has a qarina. Have you ever been lazy in seeking that qarina by not referring to tafsir or context? Will you commit to studying each mutashabih with the same care Al-Razi demonstrates?
?
The method is ta'wil with adab. Are you more concerned with intellectual satisfaction or with showing proper adab and humility when interpreting Allah's attributes? What practical step will you take this week to ensure adab is your priority?

Razi's Method for Interpreting Mutashabihat
Razi outlines the ta'wil methodology with two Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's method of using contextual clues (qarina) to interpret ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashabihat) in a way that upholds divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Contextual clues in the Quran always guide us away from literal falsehood.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a key principle for dealing with verses whose apparent meaning might suggest anthropomorphism (tashbih). He argues that Allah never mentions ambiguous expressions without surrounding them with indicators that remove misunderstanding. This lesson walks through his argument and two vivid examples from the Quran, showing why careful interpretation (ta'wil) is required out of respect for Allah's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for Razi's style, then read the full translation. Notice the structure: a principle, two examples, and a conclusion.
The passage is dense with reasoning; take your time.
2
Identify the general principle
Razi states that whenever Allah uses the word 'mutashabihat', He couples it with a qarina (clue) that eliminates false understanding. Look for this statement in the Arabic.
3
Examine the first example: 'Light'
Razi uses the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' (24:35). He points out that the following phrase 'like His light' shows that light is attributed to Allah, not identical to Him. Why would claiming identity lead to impossibility?
Self-attribution (adding a thing to itself) is impossible in Arabic grammar and logic.
4
Examine the second example: 'Throne'
The verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (20:5). Razi shows that the surrounding verses (20:4 and 20:6) indicate that anything associated with a direction is created, so Allah's 'istawa' cannot be literal.
5
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that the proper method is to interpret (ta'wil) such expressions figuratively, out of respect for the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud).
This does not mean rejecting the verses but understanding them in a way befitting Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن الله تعالى لم يذكر لفظة المتشابهات ، إلا وقرن بها قرينة تدل على زوال الوهم الباطل . مثاله : أنه تعالى لما قال : « الله نور السموات والأرض (۱۳) ، ذكر بعده مثل نوره ، فأضاف النور إلى نفسه. ولو كان هو تعالى نفس النور ، لما أضاف النور إلى نفسه . لأن إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه ممتنعة . ولما قال تعالى: «الرحمن على العرش استوى (١٤) ذكر قبله تنزيلا ممن خلق الأرض والسموات العلى ، وذكر بعده قوله : « لهما في السموات ، وما فى الأرض وما بينهما . وما تحت الثرى ، وقد ذكرنا : أن هاتين الآيتين تدلان على أن كل ما كان مختصا بجهة فوق : مخلوق محدث. فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الطريق فى هذه المتشابهات هو التأويل في تلك الألفاظ تأدبا في حق واجب الوجود . وبالله التوفيق
Know that Allah the Exalted did not mention the word 'mutashabihat' (ambiguous verses) except that He coupled with it a clue (qarina) indicating the removal of false illusion. An example: when He the Exalted said, 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' (13), He mentioned after it 'like His light,' thus attributing light to Himself. Had He the Exalted been the very light, He would not have attributed light to Himself, because attributing a thing to itself is impossible. And when He the Exalted said, 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (14), He mentioned before it a revelation from the One who created the earth and the high heavens, and mentioned after it His statement: 'To Him belongs what is in the heavens and what is on the earth and what is between them and what is beneath the soil.' And we have mentioned that these two verses indicate that everything specific to a direction is created and originated. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that the method (tariq) regarding these mutashabihat is interpretation (ta'wil) in those expressions out of respect for the Wajib al-Wujud (Necessary Being). And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents a methodological argument for dealing with mutashabihat (ambiguous verses) in the Quran. It begins by stating a general principle: whenever Allah uses the word 'mutashabihat' in the Quran, He couples it with a clue (qarina) that removes false understanding. Then two examples are given. First, the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' (An-Nur 35). Razi points out that immediately after, Allah says 'like His light' (mathalu nurihi), which shows that the light is attributed to Allah, not that He is identical to light. If He were literally light, attributing light to Himself would be impossible (self-attribution is impossible). Second, the verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (Taha 5). Razi notes that before this verse, Allah mentions 'a revelation from the One who created the earth and the high heavens' (Taha 4), and after it, He says 'To Him belongs what is in the heavens...' (Taha 6). These surrounding verses indicate that anything associated with a specific direction (like 'above' or 'over') is created and originated (makhluq muhdath). Therefore, the correct approach to these mutashabihat is to interpret them metaphorically (ta'wil) out of respect for the transcendence of the Necessary Being. The passage concludes with 'With Allah is success.' The author's point is that careful attention to context reveals the intended meaning without anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
المتشابهات
قرينة
التأويل
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think mutashabihat must be taken literally without any interpretation, or that we should remain silent (tawaqquf) about their meaning.
Correction: Razi shows that the Quran itself provides contextual clues that necessitate metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) to avoid attributing created attributes to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the function of the qarina (clue) in ambiguous verses?
A) It makes the verse clear and unambiguous.
B) It indicates the removal of false illusion.
C) It shows that the verse cannot be interpreted.
D) It confirms the literal meaning.

Key Takeaway
Razi's method shows that the Quran itself provides internal clues to interpret ambiguous verses in a way that preserves Allah's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Quranic verses about Allah's attributes, do you actively pause to consider the context, or do you rush to a literal image? How can you train yourself to seek the qarina before forming a mental picture?
?
Razi teaches that we must interpret ambiguous verses out of respect for Allah's transcendence. Is your heart truly filled with awe and reverence when you think of His attributes, or do you let casual familiarity breed careless thoughts? What will you do this week to cultivate deeper reverence?
?
The verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' is followed by 'like His light' — a clue that saves us from a false belief. Are you willing to let the Quran correct your misconceptions even when they are comfortable? What false notion about Allah are you holding onto that you need to surrender to His words?

The First Benefit: Increased Reward Through Difficulty
Imam Razi states the first benefit: ambiguous verses increase difficulty, which increases reward, citing Quran 3:142.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how ambiguous verses increase the reward for seeking truth by making the path more difficult.

Core Idea
Difficulty in seeking truth multiplies divine reward.

Many view ambiguous verses as obstacles to understanding. Imam Razi, however, shows that their very difficulty serves a spiritual purpose: the struggle to reach truth through ambiguity earns greater reward. This lesson examines the first benefit he lists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first benefit
Read the Arabic passage describing the first benefit. Notice how Imam Razi links difficulty (mashaqqa) to reward (thawab).
2
Connect difficulty to reward
Reflect on the logical connection: more effort required leads to greater spiritual reward. Imam Razi supports this with Quranic verses about struggle and patience.
Quran 3:142 is cited as evidence.
3
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: If ambiguous verses make truth harder to find, does that make them a blessing or a curse? According to this benefit, they are a blessing because they increase reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن العلماء المحققين ذكروا أنواعا من الفوائد في إنزال المتشابهات : الأول : إنه متى كانت المتشابهات موجودة ، كان الوصول إلى الحق أصعب وأشق . فزيادة المشقة توجب زيادة الثواب . قال الله تعالى : أم حسبتم أن تدخلوا الجنة ؟ ولما يعلم الله الذين جاهدوا منكم ، و يعلم الصابرين . والثاني : لو كان القرآن كله محكما ، لما كان مطابقا إلا لمذهب واحد فكان على هذا التقدير تصريحه مبطلا لكل ما سوى هذا المذهب. وذلك
And know that the verifying scholars mentioned types of benefits in sending down the ambiguous verses. The first: When ambiguous verses exist, reaching the truth is more difficult and arduous. So increased difficulty necessitates increased reward. God Almighty said: 'Or do you think that you will enter Paradise while God has not yet known those who have struggled among you and known the patient?' (Quran 3:142). The second: If the entire Quran were unequivocal (muhkam), it would only correspond to one school, and thus on this assumption its explicit statement would invalidate everything other than this school. And that...
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of the doubt by listing benefits (fawa'id) of ambiguous verses as stated by the verifying scholars (al-muhaqqiqun). Two benefits are mentioned.

First benefit: The existence of ambiguous verses makes reaching the truth more difficult and arduous (ashaqq). Increased difficulty (mashaqqa) entails increased reward (thawab). This is supported by Quran 3:142, which links struggle and patience with entering Paradise.

Second benefit: If the entire Quran were entirely clear and unequivocal (muhkam), it would conform only to one particular school of interpretation. Consequently, its explicit statements would invalidate all other schools. This suggests that the presence of ambiguity allows for multiple valid interpretations and prevents the Quran from being limited to a single understanding. The passage ends abruptly with 'وذلك' (and that...), possibly leading to further explanation in subsequent text.

Key Terms
فوائد (fawa'id)
مشقة (mashaqqa)
ثواب (thawab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ambiguous verses are pointless obstacles that make faith harder.
Correction: They serve a purpose: increasing the reward for those who strive to understand.

Checkpoint
What is the first benefit of ambiguous verses according to Imam Razi?
A) They make the truth easy to find.
B) They increase difficulty and thus increase reward.
C) They are only for scholars.
D) They prove the Quran is unclear.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous verses, by increasing the difficulty of attaining truth, increase the spiritual reward for those who strive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a verse of the Quran that you find difficult to understand, does your first reaction feel like a burden or an opportunity for increased reward? What would change in your heart if you truly believed the struggle itself multiplies your reward?
?
The ayah (3:142) connects patient striving with entering Paradise. Is there a difficult question about your faith that you have been avoiding? Will you commit to facing it this week, knowing that the very difficulty is a means of divine favor?
?
If increased difficulty leads to increased reward, then why do you often seek the easiest path in learning your religion? What would it look like for you to intentionally embrace the hard work of understanding Allah's words rather than waiting for easy answers?

The Second Benefit: Avoiding Exclusivity of One School
Imam Razi presents the second benefit: a fully clear Quran would only match one school, invalidating others; ambiguity prevents this.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second benefit: if the Quran were entirely clear, it would only correspond to one school and invalidate all others, but ambiguity allows for multiple interpretations.

Core Idea
Ambiguity prevents the Quran from being confined to a single interpretation.

If the Quran were completely unambiguous (muhkam), it would align with only one school of thought, thereby invalidating all other understandings. Imam Razi presents this as a second benefit of ambiguity: it allows the Quran to accommodate multiple valid interpretations without outright rejecting others.

Guided Path
1
Read the second benefit passage
Read the Arabic text for the second benefit. Note the conditional statement: if the Quran were entirely muhkam, it would correspond to only one school.
2
Understand the logical consequence
Consider that if the Quran explicitly supported only one interpretation, it would implicitly reject all others. This would create a problem for those following other schools.
3
Reflect on the value of ambiguity
Ambiguity allows multiple schools to find support in the Quran, preserving unity and avoiding the invalidation of diverse valid interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If ambiguity is a mercy from Allah to preserve unity, what does your impatience with unclear verses say about your trust in His wisdom? Will you now receive ambiguous verses with gratitude instead of frustration? Commit to one specific change in your approach to Quranic study.

The Objection Against Ambiguous Verses
Imam Razi narrates the shubha (doubt) against mutashabihat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part objection that mutashabihat could lead to criticism of the Quran, and how Imam Razi frames this shubha.

Core Idea
The Quran's ambiguous verses are not a flaw but a divine wisdom.

In this passage, Imam Razi begins by presenting a powerful objection that critics might raise: that the presence of ambiguous verses opens the Quran to criticism. He then sets the stage for answering it by showing why this objection fails. Understanding this doubt is crucial before exploring the benefits.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and English carefully, noting how the objection is structured and its two main critiques.
The objection starts with 'وعلى التقديرين'.
2
Identify the two criticisms
The objection has two parts: first, that metaphorical speech can mislead; second, that there are no explicit verses on tanzih and tawhid.
3
Recognize the consequence
The objection concludes that this necessitates criticism of the Quran. Ask yourself why this is a serious challenge to the Quran's clarity.
4
Prepare for the answer
Notice that Imam Razi ends by saying 'So this is the narration of this doubt' and then transitions to the benefits, setting up the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى التقديرين: فالطعن في القرآن لازم. وأيضا: فهب أنا نحمل هذه الآيات المتشابهة على الكلام بالمجاز. لكن من الكلام مجاز وهم لقول باطل، واعتقاد فاسد، وعليه فإنه كان يجب أن نتكلم بذلك الحق على وجه التصريح به، لا بالمتشابهات. ليصير ذلك سببا لزوال الإيهام الباطل. ولا يوجد في القرآن ألفاظ تدل على التنزيه والتوحيد على سبيل التصريح. فإن قوله: قل هو الله أحد، وقوله: ليس كمثله شيء وهو السميع البصير، لا يدلان على التنزيه، إلا دلالة ضعيفة. وكل ذلك يوجب الطعن في القرآن.

فهذه حكاية هذه الشبهة في هذا الباب.

واعلم: أن العلماء المحققين ذكروا أنواعا من الفوائد في إنزال المتشابهات:

الأول: إنه متى كانت المتشابهات موجودة، كان الوصول إلى الحق أصعب وأشق. فزيادة المشقة توجب زيادة الثواب. قال الله تعالى: أم حسبتم أن تدخلوا الجنة ولما يعلم الله الذين جاهدوا منكم ويعلم الصابرين.

والثاني: لو كان القرآن كله محكما، لما كان مطابقا إلا لمذهب واحد. فكان على هذا التقدير تصريحه مبطلا لكل ما سوى هذا المذهب. وذلك من الأمور المنفرة لأرباب سائر المذاهب عن قبوله، وعن النظر فيه، والانتفاع به. أما لما كان مشتملا على المحكم والمتشابه، فحينئذ يطمع صاحب كل مذهب، أن يجد فيه ما يقوي مذهبه، ويؤيد مقالته. وحينئذ ينظر فيه جميع أرباب المذاهب. ويجتهد في التأويل كل صاحب مذهب. وإذا بالغوا في ذلك التأويل، صارت المحكمات مفسرة المتشابهات. وبهذا الطريق يتخلص المبطل عن باطله، فيصل إلى الحق.

والثالث: إن القرآن إذا كان مشتملا على المحكم والمتشابه، افتقر الناظر فيه إلى الاستعانة بدلائل العقل، والاستكثار من سائر العلوم. وحينئذ يتخلص عن طلبة التقليد، ويصل إلى ضياء الاستدلال والحجة. أما لو كان كله محكما لم يفتقر إلى التمسك بالدلائل العقلية، وحينئذ يبقى في الجهل والتقليد.

والرابع: إن القرآن لما كان مشتملا على المحكم والمتشابه، افتقر إلى تعلم طريق التأويلات، وترجيح بعضها على بعض، وافتقر في تعلم ذلك إلى تحصيل علوم كثيرة، من علم اللغة والنحو وعلم أصول الفقه، ومعرفة طرق الترجيحات. ولو لم يكن القرآن مشتملا على هذه المتشابهات، لم يفتقر إلى شيء من ذلك. فكان لإيراد المتشابهات هذه الفوائد.

الخامس: - وهو السبب الأقوى - أن القرآن مشتمل على دعوة الخواص والعوام. والعوام تنبو في أكثر الأمور عن إدراك الحقائق العقلية المحضة. فمن سمع من العوام في أول الأمر إثبات موجود ليس بجسم ولا متحيز ولا مشار إليه، ظن أن هذا عدم محض، فوقع...
And on both estimates: criticism of the Quran is necessary. Also: grant that we interpret these ambiguous verses as metaphorical speech. But some metaphorical speech gives an illusion of a false saying and a corrupt belief. Therefore, it would have been necessary to speak that truth explicitly, not through ambiguous verses, so that it becomes a cause for the removal of the false illusion. And there are no words in the Quran that indicate transcendence and oneness explicitly. For His saying: 'Say: He is Allah, One' and His saying: 'Nothing is like Him, and He is the All-Hearing, All-Seeing' do not indicate transcendence except with weak indication. And all of that necessitates criticism of the Quran. So this is the narration of this doubt in this chapter. Know: that the verifying scholars mentioned types of benefits in the revelation of ambiguous verses: First: When ambiguous verses exist, reaching the truth is harder and more difficult. So increased difficulty necessitates increased reward. Allah says: 'Or do you think that you will enter Paradise while Allah has not yet made evident those who strive among you and made evident the patient?' Second: If the Quran were entirely clear, it would only be compatible with one school. Then on this basis, its explicit statement would invalidate everything other than this school. And that is among the things that repel the adherents of other schools from accepting it, from considering it, and from benefiting from it. But when it includes both clear and ambiguous, then every school's adherent hopes to find in it what strengthens his school and supports his statement. Then all adherents of schools consider it. And every school's adherent strives in interpretation. And when they go to extremes in that interpretation, the clear verses become the interpreters of the ambiguous ones. And by this way, the false one is delivered from his falsehood and reaches the truth. Third: When the Quran includes both clear and ambiguous, the one who examines it needs to seek help from rational proofs and to increase in other sciences. Then he is delivered from the pursuit of blind following and reaches the light of inference and proof. But if it were entirely clear, he would not need to cling to rational proofs, and then he would remain in ignorance and blind following. Fourth: When the Quran includes both clear and ambiguous, one needs to learn the method of interpretations and to prefer some over others. And in learning that, one needs to acquire many sciences: linguistics, grammar, principles of jurisprudence, and knowledge of methods of weighting. And if the Quran did not include these ambiguous verses, it would not need any of that. So introducing ambiguous verses had these benefits. Fifth: — and it is the strongest reason — that the Quran includes the call to the elite and the common people. And the common people are in most matters averse to grasping purely intellectual truths. So if one of the common people hears at the beginning the affirmation of an existent that is not a body, not located, and not indicated by pointing, he thinks that this is pure non-existence, so he falls...
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. It first presents a shubha (doubt) that the presence of mutashabihat (ambiguous verses) in the Quran leads to criticism. The objection runs: if we interpret them metaphorically, the intended truth is not explicit, causing false beliefs; moreover, there are no explicit verses on tanzih (transcendence) and tawhid (oneness) — even 'Qul huwa Allahu ahad' and 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' only weakly indicate them. Hence, the Quran is open to criticism. The author then transitions by saying 'So this is the narration of this doubt' and states that the verifying scholars have mentioned benefits. Five benefits are listed. (1) Difficulty increases reward, citing Q. 3:142. (2) If the Quran were entirely muhkam (clear), it would only suit one school, alienating others; but with both muhkam and mutashabih, each school hopes to find support, leading them to study and interpret; eventually the muhkam clarifies the mutashabih, guiding the false to truth. (3) The presence of mutashabihat necessitates the use of rational proofs and acquisition of other sciences, freeing one from taqlid (blind following). (4) It necessitates learning tafsir methodology, linguistics, grammar, usul al-fiqh, and methods of tarjih (preference), thus promoting scholarship. (5) The strongest reason: the Quran addresses both khawass (elite) and 'awamm (common people). The common people cannot grasp purely intellectual truths; if they hear of a non-corporeal being, they might think it is nothing. The text cuts off here, but the benefit is that ambiguous verses help gradually convey complex truths to the masses without causing them to fall into disbelief.

Key Terms
المتشابهات
التنزيه
التوحيد
المجاز
الطعن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The presence of ambiguous verses is a flaw in the Quran.
Correction: Imam Razi presents this as a shubha to be refuted, showing that it is not the final word.

Checkpoint
According to the objection presented by Imam Razi, what is the first criticism against mutashabihat?
A) They are too difficult for common people to understand.
B) Metaphorical speech can create false illusions, so explicit truth would be better.
C) They contradict the clear verses (muhkam).
D) They lead to blind following (taqlid).

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi first presents a strong objection against mutashabihat to set the stage for demonstrating their benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt frustrated or confused by a Quranic verse that seemed ambiguous? Did that feeling ever cause you to doubt the Quran's perfection? How does this lesson’s presentation of such a doubt affect your trust in divine wisdom?
?
The objection claims that ambiguous verses could create false beliefs. Think of a time you misunderstood a verse. What was the consequence, and how did you correct it? What will you do differently now to protect yourself from misinterpreting mutashabihat?
?
When you read verses like 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad', do you truly feel they are sufficient to affirm tawhid in your heart, or do you sometimes wish for more explicit statements? How will you strengthen your conviction that the Quran’s wording is complete and perfect?

Five Benefits of Ambiguous Verses
Imam Razi enumerates five benefits of having ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five wisdoms behind the revelation of ambiguous verses as explained by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses serve multiple purposes: increasing reward, encouraging study, necessitating reason, promoting scholarship, and addressing different audiences.

After presenting the doubt, Imam Razi now lists five benefits that the verifying scholars have derived from the existence of mutashabihat. Each benefit shows that what seems like a problem is actually a divine mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of benefits
Read the entire passage of the five benefits in Arabic and English, noting the structure.
The benefits are numbered in the text.
2
Examine each benefit
For each benefit, ask: what problem does it solve? How does it make the Quran more effective?
3
Reflect on the strongest reason
The fifth benefit is considered the strongest. Even though incomplete, try to grasp the point about addressing the common people gradually.
4
Connect back to the objection
See how these benefits answer the objection and show that mutashabihat are not a flaw.

?

?

?

The First Benefit: Attracting All Sects to Interpret the Quran
Razi explains how the presence of both clear and ambiguous verses attracts all sects to interpret, leading to truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the presence of both clear (muhkam) and ambiguous (mutashabih) verses in the Quran serves a pedagogical purpose, attracting all sects to engage with it and ultimately leading them to the truth.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses invite all sects to interpret, and clear verses guide them to truth.

Imam Razi presents a profound insight into the wisdom behind the Quran containing both clear and ambiguous verses. While some might think ambiguity repels people, Razi argues it actually invites engagement from all sects. This lesson explores that argument and its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial concern
Read the first part of the passage to see what Razi identifies as a potential repellent. Then see how he counters it.
The concern is that ambiguity might repel people.
2
Recognize the invitation to all sects
Notice how the presence of both types of verses makes each school hope to find support for its own views.
3
Trace the process of interpretation
See how each follower strives in interpretation (ta'wil), and when they go to excess, the clear verses interpret the ambiguous ones.
This is the key mechanism.
4
Understand the final outcome
Read the last sentence: the false person extricates from falsehood and arrives at truth.
This shows the ultimate purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مما ينفر أرباب سائر المذاهب عن قبوله ، و عن النظر فيه ، والانتفاع به أما لما كان مشتملا على المحكم والمتشابه ، فحينئذ يطمع صاحب كل مذهب ، أن يجد فيه ما يقوى مذهبه ، ويؤيد مقالته . وحينئذ ينظر فيه جميع أرباب المذاهب . ويجتهد في التأويل كل صاحب مذهب . وإذا بالغوا فى ذلك التأويل، صارت المحكمات مفسرة المتشابهات. و بهذا الطريق يتخلص المبطل عن باطله ، فيصل إلى الحق .
From that which repels the adherents of other schools from accepting it, and from looking into it, and benefiting from it: but since it comprises the clear and the ambiguous, then the follower of every school hopes to find in it what strengthens his school and supports his statement. Then all adherents of schools look into it, and every follower of a school strives in interpretation. And when they go to excess in that interpretation, the clear verses become interpreters of the ambiguous ones. And by this way the false person extricates himself from his falsehood and arrives at the truth.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents one of the benefits (or reasons) for why the Quran contains both clear (muhkam) and ambiguous (mutashabih) verses. Razi begins by noting a potential problem: that the presence of ambiguous verses might repel people from accepting and studying the Quran. However, he argues the opposite: since the Quran contains both, every school (madhhab) hopes to find support for its views, so they all engage with it. Each follower strives in interpretation (ta'wil). When they go to excess in interpretation, the clear verses actually serve to explain the ambiguous ones. In this way, the false person abandons his falsehood and reaches the truth. Thus, the ambiguity leads to dialectic that ultimately clarifies truth.

Key Terms
محكم (muhkam)
متشابه (mutashabih)
تأويل (ta'wil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ambiguous verses in the Quran repel people and cause them to reject the Quran.
Correction: Razi argues that the presence of both clear and ambiguous verses actually invites all sects to engage with the Quran, and through interpretation they arrive at truth.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what happens when adherents of different schools go to excess in interpretation of ambiguous verses?
A) They become more confused.
B) The clear verses become interpreters of the ambiguous ones.
C) They abandon the Quran entirely.
D) The ambiguous verses become clear by themselves.

Key Takeaway
The presence of both clear and ambiguous verses in the Quran invites all sects to interpret, and through excessive interpretation the clear verses clarify the ambiguous, leading the false person to truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi says that each sect hopes to find support for its own views in the Quran. Have you ever approached the Quran looking only for confirmation of what you already believe? Will you now commit to letting the clear verses correct your excesses, even if it means abandoning a cherished opinion?
?
The false person extricates himself from falsehood and arrives at truth through this process. What falsehood might you be holding onto because you haven't allowed the Quran's ambiguity to draw you into deeper study? What step will you take today to engage more sincerely with both clear and ambiguous verses?

The Hidden Blessing of Ambiguous Verses
Razi explains the third benefit of the Quran's mixture of clear and ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of ambiguous verses in the Quran necessitates rational inquiry and protects against blind imitation.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses drive us toward reason, not away from it.

Razi presents a counterintuitive argument: the very ambiguity of some Quranic verses is a blessing because it forces the seeker to engage rational proofs and acquire knowledge. Without this challenge, one would remain content with blind imitation. Let's explore this third benefit as Razi outlines it.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi contrasts the situation with ambiguous verses versus a hypothetical Quran of only clear verses.
2
Identify the key contrast
Razi compares two scenarios: when the Quran contains both clear and ambiguous, and if it were all clear. Identify the outcomes in each case.
3
Reflect on the purpose of ambiguity
Why does Razi consider ambiguity beneficial? How does it lead to using rational proofs and avoiding taqlid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إن القرآن إذا كان مشتملا على المحكم والمتشابه ، افتقر الناظر فيه إلى الاستعانة بدلائل العقل ، والاستكثار من سائر العلوم وحينئذ يتخلص عن طلبة التقليد ، ويصل إلى ضياء الاستدلال والحجة . أما لو كان كله محكما لم يفتقر إلى التمسك بالدلائل العقلية ، وحينئذ يبقى فى الجهل والتقليد .
And the third: that the Quran, when it comprises the clear and the ambiguous, the one who looks into it needs to seek help from rational proofs and to acquire many other sciences. And then he extricates himself from the pursuit of imitation and arrives at the light of evidence and proof. But if it were all clear, he would not need to adhere to rational proofs, and then he would remain in ignorance and imitation.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage gives the third benefit. Razi states that because the Quran contains both clear and ambiguous verses, the student or researcher is forced to seek help from rational proofs (adillat al-'aql) and to acquire many other sciences. This process enables him to free himself from the pursuit of blind imitation (taqlid) and to reach the light of evidence (istidlal) and proof (hujja). In contrast, if the Quran were entirely clear (muhkam) and unambiguous, there would be no need to cling to rational proofs, and the person would remain in ignorance and imitation. Thus, the ambiguity serves a pedagogical purpose, driving the seeker toward intellectual rigor.

Key Terms
دلائل العقل
تقليد
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume the presence of ambiguous verses is a flaw or hindrance in the Quran.
Correction: Razi argues that ambiguity actually forces us to use reason, preventing us from remaining in blind imitation and ignorance.

Checkpoint

A) People would still need rational proofs.
B) People would remain in ignorance and imitation.
C) People would easily understand everything.
D) People would abandon the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous verses are not a defect; they are a divine design that compels us to use reason and escape blind imitation.

Necessity of Multiple Sciences for Interpretation
Razi states that ambiguous verses necessitate learning multiple sciences for proper interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the existence of ambiguous verses in the Quran necessitates the study of multiple Islamic sciences for proper interpretation.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses are not obstacles but drivers of scholarly excellence.

In this benefit, Imam Razi presents a positive purpose for ambiguous verses. Rather than being a source of confusion, they compel the interpreter to master disciplines like linguistics, grammar, and usul al-fiqh. Read carefully to see how Razi turns a potential problem into a profound intellectual imperative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the logical flow from condition to conclusion.
The passage is concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the condition and result
What is the condition Razi begins with? What is the result? Trace how he connects the presence of ambiguous verses to the need for learning sciences.
Look for the words 'افتقر' (needs) and 'فكان' (so).
3
Reflect on the listed sciences
Why does Razi specifically mention linguistics, grammar, usul al-fiqh, and methods of preference? Think about how each contributes to sound interpretation.
These are foundational for any serious interpreter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع : إن القرآن لما كان مشتملا على المحكم والمتشابه ، افتقر إلى تعلم طريق التأويلات ، وترجيح بعضها على بعض ، وافتقر في تعلم ذلك إلى تحصيل علوم كثيرة ، من علم اللغة والنحو وعلم أصول الفقه ، ومعرفة طرق الترجيحات . ولو لم يكن القرآن مشتملا على هذه المتشابهات ، لم يفتقر إلى شيء من ذلك . فكان لإيراد المتشابهات هذه الفوائد .
And the fourth: that the Quran, since it comprises the clear and the ambiguous, needs (the reader) to learn the methods of interpretations and to prefer some over others, and in learning that he needs to acquire many sciences: linguistics, grammar, principles of jurisprudence, and knowledge of methods of preferences. And if the Quran did not comprise these ambiguous verses, he would not need any of that. So for the introduction of ambiguous verses there are these benefits.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth benefit. Razi argues that because the Quran contains both clear and ambiguous verses, the interpreter is compelled to learn the methods of interpretation (turuq al-ta'wilat) and to weigh some interpretations over others (tarjih). This in turn requires acquiring many sciences: linguistics (lugha), grammar (nahw), principles of jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh), and methods of preference (turuq al-tarjihat). If the Quran had no ambiguous verses, none of these sciences would be needed. Thus, the presence of ambiguity enriches Islamic scholarship and necessitates a rigorous intellectual tradition.

Key Terms

ترجيح (tarjih)
أصول الفقه (usul al-fiqh)

Correction: Razi shows that ambiguous verses necessitate the development of multiple sciences, thereby elevating Islamic scholarship.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous verses are a means to elevate Islamic scholarship by necessitating rigorous training in foundational sciences.

Fifth Benefit: Addressing Common People with Ambiguous Expressions
Razi explains that ambiguous verses protect common people from falling into denial when presented with pure intellectual truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran includes ambiguous verses and how they serve a pedagogical purpose for common people.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses protect common people from falling into denial when confronted with pure intellectual truths.

Razi presents the fifth and strongest reason for the existence of ambiguous verses. He explains that the Quran addresses both the elite and the common people, and that common people cannot immediately grasp pure intellectual realities. Therefore, ambiguous expressions serve as a gradual pedagogical tool to lead them from anthropomorphic imagery to true tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Razi's argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong phrasing: 'وهو السبب الأقوى'.
2
Understand the problem
Identify why Razi says common people recoil from pure intellectual truths. Pay attention to the example of affirming a non-corporeal existence leading to denial.
3
Identify the two kinds of address
The passage distinguishes between two kinds of address: one for the initial stage (ambiguous) and one for later (clear). Notice the terms 'متشابهات' and 'محكمات'.
Razi calls the first kind 'from the category of ambiguous' and the second 'from the category of clear'.
4
Reflect on the pedagogical wisdom
Consider why this approach is appropriate. How does mixing ambiguous expressions with hints of truth prevent common people from falling into denial?
This connects to the broader theme of divine wisdom in revelation.
5
Connect to the broader topic
Think about how this explanation fits into the overall discussion of mutashabihat and muhkamat in the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : - وهو السبب الأقوى - أن القرآن مشتمل على دعوة الخواص والعوام ) والعوام تنبو فى أكثر الأمور عن إدراك الحقائق العقلية المحضة . فمن سمع من العوام في أول الأمر إثبات موجود ليس بجسم ولا متحيز ولا مشار إليه ، ظن أن هذا عدم محض ، فوقع فى التعطيل . مكان الأصلح أن يخاطبوا بألفاظ دالة على بعض ما يناسب ما تخيلوه وتوهموه . ويكون ذلك مخلوطا بما يدل على الحق الصريح. فالقسم الأول : وهو الذي يخاطبون به في أول الأمر ، يكون من باب المتشابهات . و القسم الثاني : وهو الذي يكشف لهم فى آخر الأمر ، هو من المحكمات . فهذا ما لخصناه فى هذا الباب ) وبالله التوفيق .
The fifth – and it is the strongest reason – is that the Quran includes the call to the elite and the common people. And the common people in most matters recoil from perceiving pure intellectual realities. So if a common person hears at the first instance the affirmation of an existent that is not a body, not located, and not pointed to, he thinks that this is pure non-existence, and thus falls into denial. It is more appropriate that they be addressed with expressions indicating something that suits what they imagine and suppose, and that be mixed with what indicates the explicit truth. So the first kind – that by which they are addressed initially – is from the category of ambiguous (mutashabihat). And the second kind – that which is revealed to them at the end – is from the category of clear (muhkamat). This is what we have summarized in this chapter. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage gives the fifth and strongest reason for the existence of ambiguous verses. Razi explains that the Quran addresses both the elite (khawass) and the common people ('awamm). Common people recoil from pure intellectual realities. For example, if at first they hear that God is not a body, not located, and not pointed to, they might think that means God is nothing, leading to denial (ta'til). Therefore, it is more appropriate to address them initially with expressions that correspond to their imagination and suppositions, but mixed with indications of the explicit truth. The first kind of address, for beginners, belongs to ambiguous verses (mutashabihat). The second kind, which is gradually revealed to them later, belongs to clear verses (muhkamat). Thus, the ambiguity serves a pedagogical function, gradually leading the common people from anthropomorphic imagery to a more refined understanding, without causing them to fall into disbelief.

Key Terms
خواص (khawass)
عوام ('awamm)
تعطيل (ta'til)
محكم (muhkam)
متشابه (mutashabih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that anthropomorphic verses in the Quran are literally true about God.
Correction: Razi explains that such verses are pedagogical concessions for common people to gradually lead them to a proper understanding of God's transcendence.

Checkpoint

A) To confuse them.
B) Because they cannot grasp pure intellectual realities like God's incorporeality without falling into denial.
C) Because the Quran is unclear.
D) To test their faith.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous verses in the Quran serve a pedagogical purpose, gradually leading common people from anthropomorphic ideas to a true understanding of God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a description of Allah that seems to imply a body or location, do you feel a tension between your imagination and what you know to be true? How does Razi’s explanation of gradual guidance help you navigate that discomfort without falling into denial?

Al-Razi's Method of Interpreting Mutashabih by Muhkam
Al-Razi's hermeneutical method for interpreting mutashabih verses by muhkam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses the clear verse 'Nothing is like Him' to determine the correct meaning of the ambiguous verse 'The hand of Allah is above their hands'.

Core Idea
The clear (muhkam) verse governs the interpretation of the ambiguous (mutashabih) verse.

Imam al-Razi presents a systematic method for understanding ambiguous Quranic verses about Allah's attributes. He uses two key verses: one that is absolutely clear in negating any resemblance to Allah, and another that could be misunderstood literally. This lesson introduces his hermeneutical principle of referring ambiguous verses back to clear ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two verses
Read the passage and note that al-Razi starts with two verses: one muhkam ('Nothing is like Him') and one mutashabih ('The hand of Allah is above their hands').
2
Understand the rule
Recognize that the muhkam has one clear meaning (negation of likeness), while the mutashabih has two possible meanings. The rule is to refer to the muhkam when ambiguity arises.
The muhkam acts as the authoritative interpreter.
3
Apply the rule
See how al-Razi applies the rule: the metaphorical meaning (power) is consistent with the muhkam, while the literal hand would imply similarity and is therefore rejected.
This yields the correct understanding of the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و بيان رأى الإمام فخر الدين هكذا :
(أ) يد الله فوق أيديهم، (ب) ليس كمثله شيء.
قوله: «ليس كمثله شيء»: قول محكم، له معنى واحد، وهو نفي الشبيه والنظير عن الله عز وجل. وقوله: «يد الله»: قول متشابه يحتمل معنيين: الأول: اليد الجارحة، والثاني: اليد كناية عن القدرة. وحيث أنه متشابه، يتعين الرجوع إلى المحكم. والمحكم وهو «ليس كمثله شيء» لا يدل على معنيين كالمتشابه، بل يدل على معنى واحد، وهو نفي التشبيه.
ويسأل الإمام فخر الدين هذا السؤال: أي المعنيين من معنيي المتشابه هو الذي يتفق مع المحكم؟ إذا عرفته فقد عرفت مراد الله تعالى من قوله: «يد الله فوق أيديهم»، ويجيب بقوله: إن المعنى المتفق مع المحكم هو أن اليد كناية عن القدرة، لأن اليد الجارحة لا تتفق مع المحكم في التشبيه.
And the explanation of the view of Imam Fakhr al-Din is as follows:
(a) 'The hand of Allah is above their hands', (b) 'Nothing is like Him'.
His saying: 'Nothing is like Him' is a muhkam (clear) statement, having one meaning, which is the negation of resemblance and likeness from Allah Almighty. And His saying: 'The hand of Allah' is a mutashabih (ambiguous) statement that can bear two meanings: first, the physical hand; second, the hand as a metaphor for power. Since it is mutashabih, it is necessary to refer to the muhkam. And the muhkam, which is 'Nothing is like Him', does not indicate two meanings like the mutashabih, but indicates one meaning, which is negation of tashbih.
Imam Fakhr al-Din asks this question: Which of the two meanings of the mutashabih is that which agrees with the muhkam? If you know that, then you have known the intended meaning of Allah from His saying 'The hand of Allah is above their hands'. And he answers by saying: The meaning that agrees with the muhkam is that the hand is a metaphor for power, because the physical hand does not agree with the muhkam in tashbih.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage details Imam al-Razi's hermeneutical principle for handling ambiguous (mutashabih) verses in the Quran. He presents two key verses: 'The hand of Allah is above their hands' (mutashabih) and 'Nothing is like Him' (muhkam). Al-Razi identifies the muhkam verse as having a single, clear meaning: the absolute negation of any similarity to Allah. The mutashabih verse, on the other hand, admits two possible interpretations: a literal physical hand, or a metaphorical reference to power. Since the mutashabih is ambiguous, the method is to turn to the muhkam as the authoritative judge. The muhkam, 'Nothing is like Him', unequivocally negates all resemblance, so the literal physical hand cannot be the intended meaning because it would imply similarity. Therefore, the only interpretation consistent with the muhkam is the metaphorical understanding of 'hand' as power. Al-Razi frames this as a question-and-answer: the agreed meaning is the one that conforms to the muhkam, which is the metaphorical sense.

Key Terms
محكم
متشابه
يد الله
ليس كمثله شيء
التشبيه
نفي الشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Razi denies the hand entirely or rejects the attribute of power.
Correction: Al-Razi does not deny the verse; rather, he interprets 'hand' as a metaphor for power, affirming the attribute in a manner consistent with divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, how many meanings does the ambiguous verse 'yad Allah' have?
A) One meaning: the literal hand.
B) Two meanings: literal hand and metaphorical power.
C) Three meanings including power, mercy, and blessing.
D) It has infinite meanings.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's method is to refer ambiguous verses about Allah's attributes to clear verses that negate any likeness, thereby affirming the attributes in a manner befitting Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about Allah's attributes that seem to imply a physical form, do you immediately seek out clear verses that affirm His transcendence? Or do you rely on your own initial impression? Will you commit to using al-Razi's method from now on, even when it challenges your first thought?

Al-Razi's method shows that the clear verse governs the ambiguous. Is there an area of your life—your beliefs, actions, or worship—where you prioritize ambiguous or unclear evidence over the clear principles of the Quran? What will you do to realign yourself with the muhkam?
?
The passage emphasizes that the correct meaning is the one that agrees with the muhkam. Have you ever held a belief about Allah that, upon reflection, contradicts the clear teaching that 'Nothing is like Him'? If so, will you humble yourself and correct that belief today, regardless of how familiar or comforting it was?

The Quranic Foundation for Muhkam and Mutashabih with Examples of Deviant Interpretations
Imam Razi expounds on Quran 3:7 with examples of deviant ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran itself distinguishes between muhkam and mutashabih verses, and how deviant groups exploit the ambiguous verses while the firmly rooted scholars respond with humility and faith.

Core Idea
The Quran contains both clear and ambiguous verses; the righteous affirm them all from Allah.

This lesson examines the key Quranic verse that establishes the muhkam-mutashabih distinction, followed by concrete historical examples of how Jews and Christians misused ambiguous verses. Imam Razi then contrasts this with the proper attitude of the rasikhun, those firmly rooted in knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse
Begin by reading the verse of Surah Al Imran (3:7) which forms the foundation of the discussion. Notice the terms muhkam (clear) and mutashabih (ambiguous).
The verse is quoted at the start of the passage.
2
Identify the two main lessons
The author draws two lessons from the verse: first, that the Quran contains both types of verses; second, that those with deviation in their hearts exploit the ambiguous ones.
Look for the numbered points '1 - يدل' and '2 - يدل'.
3
Examine the deviant examples
Study the two examples: the People of the Book (Jews) using the Quran's praise of the Torah to reject its guidance, and Christians using the description of Jesus as 'His word' and 'a spirit from Him' to support their trinitarian doctrine.
These are real historical misinterpretations.
4
Contrast with the rasikhun
Read how those firmly rooted in knowledge respond: when they understand they praise Allah, when they don't they blame themselves and seek divine openings. They affirm all verses from Allah.
This shows the proper attitude of humility and faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى: (هو الذي أنزل عليك الكتاب منه آيات محكمات هن أم الكتاب وأخر متشابهات فأما الذين في قلوبهم زيغ فيتبعون ما تشابه منه ابتغاء الفتنة وابتغاء تأويله وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله والراسخون في العلم يقولون آمنا به كل من عند ربنا) .
1 - يدل على أن في القرآن المحكم والمتشابه، كما ذكرنا في «يد الله» وفي «ليس كمثله شيء».
2 - ويدل على أن الذين في قلوبهم زيغ، أي بعد عن الحق، يأخذون المتشابه للفتنة، ولتأويله تأويلا فاسدا. فمثال أخذهم المتشابه للفتنة: أن أهل الكتاب قالوا في قوله تعالى: «إنا أنزلنا التوراة فيها هدى ونور»: إن القرآن شهد بصحة التوراة، وأنها تكفي في هداية الناس إلى الحق.
ومثال أخذهم المتشابه للتأويل الفاسد: إن النصارى قالوا في قوله تعالى: «وكلمته ألقاها إلى مريم وروح منه»: أن القرآن اعترف بصحة أقنوم الابن الذي هو الكلمة، وصحة أقنوم الروح القدس.
- وأما الراسخون في العلم الإلهي، لتأكدهم من الله بالبراهين والحجج، وخشيتهم منه، فإنهم إذا فهموا المعنى حمدوا الله، وإذا لم يفهموه اتهموا أنفسهم بالتقصير في العلم، أو طلبوا الفتوح من الله. ويقولون في ما فهموه وفي ما لم يفهموه: «كل من عند ربنا».
And His saying, Exalted is He: (It is He who has sent down to you the Book; from it are clear verses (muhkamat) which are the mother of the Book, and others ambiguous (mutashabihat). As for those in whose hearts is deviation, they follow what is ambiguous from it, seeking discord and seeking its interpretation. And no one knows its interpretation except Allah. But those firmly rooted in knowledge say: 'We believe in it; all is from our Lord.') 
1 - This indicates that in the Quran there are muhkam and mutashabih, as we mentioned in 'the hand of Allah' and in 'nothing is like Him'.
2 - And it indicates that those in whose hearts is deviation, i.e., turning away from the truth, take the mutashabih for discord and for interpreting it with a corrupt interpretation. An example of their taking the mutashabih for discord: The People of the Book said regarding His saying 'Indeed, We sent down the Torah, in which is guidance and light': The Quran testified to the truth of the Torah, and that it suffices for guiding people to the truth.
And an example of their taking the mutashabih for a corrupt interpretation: The Christians said regarding His saying 'And His word which He cast into Mary and a spirit from Him': That the Quran acknowledged the truth of the hypostasis of the Son, who is the Word, and the truth of the hypostasis of the Holy Spirit.
- As for those firmly rooted in divine knowledge, due to their certainty of Allah with proofs and evidence, and their fear of Him, then when they understand the meaning they praise Allah, and when they do not understand it they blame themselves for deficiency in knowledge, or they seek openings from Allah. And they say regarding what they understood and what they did not understand: 'All is from our Lord.'
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage expands on the Quranic foundation for the muhkam-mutashabih distinction, citing verse 7 of Surah Al Imran. The author explains two main lessons derived from this verse. First, the Quran contains both clear and ambiguous verses, as exemplified by the earlier discussion of 'hand' and 'nothing like Him'. Second, people with deviation in their hearts (ziygh) intentionally follow the ambiguous parts to create discord (fitna) and to impose a corrupt interpretation (ta'wil fasad). Two concrete examples are given: Jews using the Quran's praise of the Torah as an argument that the Quran confirms the Torah's sufficiency, thus rejecting the Quran's own guidance; and Christians using the description of Jesus as 'His word' and 'a spirit from Him' to support their trinitarian doctrine of the Son and Holy Spirit. In contrast, those firmly rooted in knowledge (rasikhun fi al-'ilm) have certainty from proofs and fear of Allah. When they understand the meaning, they praise Allah; when they do not, they blame themselves and seek divine openings. They affirm that all verses, clear and ambiguous, are from their Lord.

Key Terms
محكم
متشابه
زيغ
الراسخون في العلم
تأويل
فتنة
أهل الكتاب
النصارى
أقنوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the verse implies no one can know the interpretation of mutashabih at all.
Correction: The rasikhun say 'we believe' but are not necessarily ignorant; they may understand, but they do not follow their desires or claim exclusive knowledge as the deviant do.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do Christians point to in the Quran to support their doctrine of the Trinity?
A) The verse about the Torah being a light.
B) The verse describing Jesus as 'His word' and 'a spirit from Him'.
C) The verse about the muhkam and mutashabih.
D) The verse about 'hand of Allah'.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's muhkam-mutashabih distinction is a safeguard against fitna, and the proper response to ambiguity is faith and humility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse in the Quran that you don't fully understand, do you immediately blame yourself for deficiency in knowledge, or do you seek openings from Allah? Which response reflects the attitude of the rasikhun, and which leads to the path of deviation?
?
Think of a time you used a verse from the Quran to argue with someone else, perhaps to 'prove' your view was right. Did you consider that you might be following the mutashabih to create discord? What would it look like to instead say, 'All is from our Lord' even in that disagreement?
?
The Christians took the phrase 'His word' and twisted it for their own theology. Have you ever taken a part of the Quran out of context to justify a convenience in your own life? What would it cost you to admit that you don't know the full interpretation and to trust Allah instead?
